THE RED PAGE .
Loyalty’s Literary Product.
THE Duke of Cornwall and York, heir
co the British throne—“ our sailor
prince,” who, it is reported in Syd-
ney Telegraph of 16th May, preferred
to go overland from Melbourne to Brisbane
because he is “subject to sea-sickness”—
spoke a stodgy piece the other day to some
Victorian school-pupils ; and this was part
of it—
If I may offer advice I should say be thorough.
Do your level best in whatever work you may be
called upon to perform. Remember that we are
all fellow subjects of tbe British Crown. Be
loyal—yes, to your parents, your country, your
King and your God.
If his hearers took his advice, and were
thorough—thorough in thought, thorough
in argument, thorough in demanding that
everybody’s say-so should stand and deliver
reasons for acceptance, they would naturally
ask “ Why ? Why be loyal ? How does
loyalty pay us ? What are we going to get
out of loyalty ? ” For a loyalty which does
not pay the loyalist has plainly no valid leg
to stand on.
Loyalty to parents ? We can understand
and endorse that ; for in most cases our
parents have deserved it: we live, we have
lived, at their cost : what we are we owe to
them, partly or chiefly. It is true that the best
gratitude to one’s .parents is shown in taking
care of one’s own children—in passing on to
the next generation the care and protection
which we have received from the last. In
begetting us and nourishing us our parents
sought the satisfaction of their own instincts,
and they cannot claim as a right the grati-
tude which we nevertheless often owe to
them as a duty. Loyalty to our parents pays
us because we in turn hope to earn loyal con-
fidence and support from our own children.
Or we can deem parents our best friends,
and give them the loyalty due to friends.
In either of these cases there is a clear justi-
fication for loyalty.
Loyalty to our country—that, too, is in-
telligible, - for it spells self-preservation.
Communal life is only possible on a basis of
giving for getting—that is to say, on a basis
of exchange. We exchange our loyal sup-
port for. our country’s help and protection,
its means and laws of life, its sympathy
and shelter. Even loyalty to a God may be
justified for those who believe in a beyond-
the-grave heaven and hell : there is clear
payment in heaven for prayers and oblations
rendered. But loya'ty to a king? How is
it possible to justify that for people who do
not find in such a kind of loyalty the satisfac-
tion of their hereditary instinct to crawl to
the steps of a throne ? ,>v r
It is not possible : the modern monarch
has no justification ; he exists in modern life
because monarchy is a mental tradition which
many human minds are not yet original
enough to destroy. Possibly, while most
people remain mental cripples, monarchy
can be defended as a useful crutch ; but even
that defence is shattered by the fact that
already many communities get along as well
or better without a monarch.
In advancing along the line of evolution
humanity retains many acquisitions, physical
and mental, long after the need of them has
been outgrown. There was probably a time
when the ancestor of humanity found good
use for his vermiform appendix—that little
pipe-stem cul-de-sac which sprouts from
the large intestine, and which at present
seems to serve no useful, purpose what-
ever, while it is a frequent contributor
of illness and death. Monarchy is
humanity’s mental vermiform - appendix.
The ancient monarch could usually give
good reason for his presence, such as js
given by the savage chieftain of to-day. He
was probably the best man of his tribe ; he
won his place by prowess ; in difficulty or
danger his followers relied on his brain
and arm. But the puny scion of a
dying stock, so weakened that it re-
quires to be mated continually with good
blood in order)to perpetuated, gives no
such reason for the honours paid him. The
hereditary system of monarchy which issues
in a Duke of Cornwall and York is a self-
convicted absurdity.
Why, then, do people honour the heredi-
tary monarch ? Simply because the habit of
reverence for monarchy is inherited, and
they are born true to type. Why are
people born with two eyes, not three ?
with five toes on a foot, not
four or seven ? Just because the germ
plasm follows its law of life, and perpetu-
ates the ancestral habit—varying slowly,
very slowly, in countless generations. Simi-
larly, the causes which impel Dr. Graham,
Mayor of Sydney, to give gold albums
to the Duke of York, are probably
just as much beyond his control as is his
physical shape. His loyalty is an ancestral
inheritance, like his Mayoral legs. By
reason, of course, homage is rather due from
' the Duke of York to Dr. Graham, from the
physically, and mentally weaker man to his
superior But the ancestral force in Dr.
Graham is, as it were, stronger than himself.
Go to a South Sea Island, and you will find
the chief busily receiving the offerings of
his tribesmen. The women dig his yams,
the men catch his fish, and the best of the
product is reserved for the chief When his
appetite is satisfied, the followers get their
i uferior share. The Victorian Govt.’s offering
<»f a black wood trophy to the Duke of Yorkt
the Sydney Council’s offering"of illuminated
addresses, proceed exactly on the same prin-
ciple with the difference that their chief
does not earn them. They are honouring
the ghost of the shadow of the memory of
something their ancestors honoured in
Edward the Black Prince or William the
Conqueror. And there is no sense in them
—only heredity.
The concerts, the reviews, given for the
Duke of York, have their parallels in savage
life, which represents now-civilised life at
perhaps a thousand or two thousand
years ago. And the countless “poems”
written for the Duke represent the
savage bard's enthusiasm when his chief re-
turns in warrior-guise from some triumphant
foray. (The Duke of York is not a warrior ;
but he plants trees, and that satisfies the
hereditary fetish.) Naturally, the “ poems ”
are bad ; because poetry nowadays is a more-
or-less intelligent product, and the first
result of intelligence is the abolition of here-
ditary fetishes as far as possible. But some
samples of the way in which hereditary
loyalty displays itself are interesting as
showing just how far we have progressed,
just how little, after all, is our reason-gain
from the slow, slow centuries. Here is a
Ballarat specimen—
Tha t is a very fair average of local loyal
“ poetry ” ; and it has all the hereditary
attributes which result in a Fijian canoe
being launched on “ ways ” of loyal stomachs.
The stomach, as has been noted, plays a great
partin “loyalty ” ; and it is not impossible
that the hereditary seat of some loyal
emotions is in the region of the solar plexus.
But there are loyal “poems” and loyal
“ poems ” —such as this one :
The Author trusts that at this crucial juncture
in the history of Australia and of the British
Empire, the preceding impromptu lines, com-
posed by request, on the eve of the Common-
wealth Elections, will be received by the people
of the Commonwealth of Australia in the patriotic,
federative, hopeful spirit in which they have been
written, and that they will assist to bring together
a Parliament capable of worthily laying the foun-
dation of the Australian Nation.— f.l.w.
These verses are composed by Mr. F. Lyon
Weiss, F.E.I.S. Mr. Weiss is now Principal of
the Bureau and School of Mines, Perth ; has been
Head-master, Lecturer, Assistant, &c., at the
largest and most reputable University Colleges
and Schools of Mines and Technics in New South
Wales, Victoria, South Australia. He is a scien-
tific and educational author, and is also the com-
poser of the anonymously published poem,
“ Rally, comrades ! round our Empire Flag un-
furled,” which has been recorded in Parliament,
and upon which the honor (unprecedented in his-
tory) has been conferred of being dedicated by
Lords Roberts and Wolseley to the British army.
The following is a copy of an autograph letter
received by the Hon. Sec from the LIEU-
TENANT GOVERNOR of VICTORIA, His
Exoellency SIR JOHN MADDEN, K.C.M.G.,
8.A., LL.D., Chief Justice, Chancellor of the
University, endorsing the laudatpry letters pre-
viously received from their Excellencies EARL
HOPETOWN (THE GO VERNOR - GEN-
ERAL) ; from LORDS BEAUCHAMP,
TENNYSON, LAMINGTON, RANFURLEY,
Sir A. MILNER (Governor Cape Colony), and
Sir A. ONSLOW, and from Generals, Command-
ants, Poets, Actors, Scholars, Municipalities, &c.
“ Government House, Melbourne,
17th January, 1901.
Dear Sir, —I am obliged to you for the copies
which you were good enough to send me of Mr.
F. Lyon Weiss’s anthem and panegyric odes.
They are broad in conception, lofty in patriotism,
and spirited and poetic in expression, and are
likely to become “ songs of the people.” In this
respect their adaptation to well-known music is
no small advantage, and I willingly add my con-
gratulations to Mr. Weiss on the success with
which, while others have been fighting our
Country’s battles, he has been making its songs.—
Faithfully yours, JOHN MADDEN.”
Mr. Weiss has shared the hardest and fnost
dangerous manual labor ; has organised, addressed
and influenced important movements as to Min-
ing, Banking, Justice, Life-saving, etc! (three
gratuitously); has had his services recognised by
the Australian Contingents and by democratic
organisations, etc. ; and has been publicly eulo-
fised by such representative men as Sir Henry
’arkes, Right Hon. E. Barton (Premier of Aus-
tralia), and Sir A. Ren wick, M.D. This League
(a non-party-political, un-sectarian, but patriotic
body) therefore issues these memorable verses at
this crisis, in the belief and trust that the advice
of a man with Mr. Weiss’s record commands re-
spect, and that Australian-Britons will once again
be influenced by his graphic and stirring words.
A. Cooper, Acting Ho<n. Sec.
The League would be pleased if you would cir-
culate these verses, and if you would kindly send
any reprint or expression of opinion to
THE PRESIDENT, EMPIRE PATRIOTIC LEAGUE, ;
53 William Street, Perth, W.A.
Where also requisite information, membership,
&c., can be obtained.
Here we see loyalty passing from the
monarch (the little round button atop) to the
Great Panjandrum of Imperialism. Com-
ment is hopeless.. But note how the
trail of notoriety follows in the loyal
“poet’s” wake, Here are Governors
galore, and Chief Justice Madden of
Victoria, patting this producer of balderdash
on the back—because he points the right
loyal way, apparently. They say Judge
Madden knows so little law that he
ought to know a little literature by
rule of contraries ; but- ! Similar stuff to
be “broad in conception,” “spirited
and poetic in expression ” ! Surely even
Judge Madden is not so ignorant as that.
No ; his praise, like the others’, must be
due to the High Policy of praising the tenets
approved for the people who sweat in
shadow at the root of the social tree by
those who sit in sunshine at the top. The
sentimeut of—' • _ ■
God bless the squire and his relations,
And keep us in our proper station's—
will always find an echo from decorated dig-
nitaries.
To vary the round, choose from the work
of James Purtell, a thoroughly loyal poet, a
piece in another hereditary category. Pur-
tell, like the kangaroo, is “an amoosin’
little cuss,” and he is the only professional
poet in Australia —the only, man who makes
a living solely and wholly by poetry. He
invariably refers to his product as “ effu-
sions ” —sentimental gush poured out in a
stream—and the effusions are sold by thou-
sands to the illiterate bulk of Melbourne’s
population. All news-agents—one penny
per effusion ! And Barnum-Povvell (no less)
has praised them.
Kronstadt, South Africa, 3/9/00.
Sir, —Many thanks for your spirited lines, and
I thank you for the kind motive that prompted
you to send me your song on the Relief of Mafe-
king.—Yours very truly, Baden-Powelb.
To James Purtell, Melbourne, Australia.
This is printed because Mr. Purtell wants it
printed. Some time ago a Bulletin comment
brought this indignant letter—
Sir.—l have been shown your scathing article
about Myself in the issue of your Dirty Contempt-
able Rag of two or 3 weeks back, and it is a great
pity you can not stick to truth, for when you say
My songs are hawked around the City of Melb
on the milk-walk Principle, you tell a Most Bare
Faced Lie, and the public of Victoria at Least
Must Smile When they Read your Article, For
My Songs are Sold at All Respectable Agents in
Victoria, and it is the Public of Victoria Who I
Look upon as The Critics, Which thev have
Proved by buying Thousands upon thousands of
Every Effusion I have Written during the past
ten years. I dont Look upon The Editor of the
Meanest Rag in Australia as a Critic, For you
are not a Critic, you are What I term a cynic,
And a Mean one at that. Although your scathing
article Does Me no harm, I believe In Fair
Criticism, and yours Is Most unfair, because it Is
untrue, and again you Make your own paragraph
Look Small when you say I have cold bloodedly
adopted Poetry for a Living, for a Man who can
obtain a good Living for nine or ten years out- of
his writings (penny Effusions) Clearly shows that
the Public Appreciate what he has put before
Them. I Enclose for dear old Bull my Latest bit
of Doggerel Re the Return of our Contingent, also
(on the songs) A Copy of Baden Powells Letter
To Me. of course it would Not suit your paper
to publish The Letter for Me. However I Thank
you for all your Extremely Kind Advertisements
And when I take a trip to My dear old native
Land (Sydney) I will call and pay you for Kind-
ness, so you can Expect Me Before Christmas.
And now Dear old Bully in conclusion Let Me
tell you that I do Hot Write about the Birds and
The Sea and Fishes and all Nature Like some of
your Clever Poets, if I did I would soon Be in the
Benevolent Asylum. Bub what Ido write about is
Fact, Rhythm, Rhyme, sentiment And Last but
not Least Common sense.
Yours Etc
James Purtell
And here is a copy of what, some date last
year, was— /
Observe that the quoted effusion is not
half bad—is indeed very good—for Purtell's
audience. It hits them where they live.
They do not want “ the Birds and The Sea
and Fishes and all Nature.” They want
the titillation of hereditary instincts—
“ loyalty ” one of them. Purtell lays to his
book ; and, as he is very common-sensible,
it may be that his effusions do good rather
than harm. They give his readers the
emotions which fit his readers. By-and-by,
via Purtell, Purtell's readers may learn tb
appreciate something better. By-and-by,
via loyalty to the Duke of York, Australians
may learn to appreciate loyalty to Australia.
You can’t hurry the universe or the race.
Let us hope.
THE BOOKFELBOW.
A HYMN OF WELCOME.
“GOD BLESS THESE TWAIN SO GREAT.”
[On the occasion ot the visit of His Royal Highness
the Duke of Cornwall and York, accompanied by his
Consort, the Grand Duchess, May, 1901.]
Tune —“ Grafenberg.”
J oy-bells of earth are ringing',
“God bless this Prince so great,
Who comes from dear old England,
Its Royal Delegate.
With heart and voice united,
God’s blessing we entreat,
While we for angels listen,
They this refrain repeat ”
High above the welkin,
Beyond all earthly ken,
To such appeal unto our King
We add — Amen.
Hark ! sweet-voiced children singing,
“God bless this Prince so great.”
Their love is worth the winning.
Their love will ne’er abate,
Good Prince, fair Consort, list ye—
Those rosy lips reveal,
A pure white love for you twain,
A love all yours to seal.
High above the welkin,
Beyond all earthly ken,
To childhood’s prayers unto our King
We add — Amen.
Our Austral sons are calling,
“ God bless this Prince so great :
Whoever doubts our loyalty
The red veldt should him sate.
We’re true to God in heaven ;
Hence, true to man on earth :
Then swell the crescent praises,
Till strains like these break forth
High above the welkin,
.. Beyond all earthly ken,
>ll manhood’s prayers unto our King
We add — Amen.
The agisd sigh but softly,
“ God bless these twain so great,
Whose veins contain the purple
Of saints in heav’nly state.
O may these lives when ended,
A regal weloome have
Where royalties commingle
Beyond the muted grave ! ”
High above the welkin,
Beyond all earthly ken,
To old folks’ prayers unto our king
We add — Amen.
BALLARAT.

AN ELECTORAL APPEAL.
AUSTRALIANS! GUARD YOUR RIGHTS!
Australians ! Guard your rights, and keep
Your Race the grandest born !
Australians ! watch your weal, and sweep
Your fair land free froip scorn !
Your warriors brave, on land and wave, shall
guard ’gainst foreign foe,
But who shall fight, for People’s Right, ’gainst
trusted traitor’s blow.
If men to whom you trust its doom, would wreck
or wrong your State,
At country’s call, up spring ye all, and mete
them miscreants’ fate 1
Australians ! Gather 1 Guard your Right;
And let but patriots guide your Might.
11.
Australians ! Guard your rights! and see
They ne’er shall be betrayed !
Your Isles the home of JUSTICE be,
Of free men undismayed !
The maxim trite that “ measures should be valued
more than men, ”
Though Thebes disproved, may catch the ear,
e’en now as it did then.
But earnest, fearless, noble men, make laws and
statesmen true.
So, choosing thus, each State will have good men
and measures too !
Australians ! Gather ! Guard your Right !
And let but patriots guide your Might!
111.
AUSTRALIANS ! Guard your rights, and pass
Your fiat to elect a man
Who merely represents a class,
A creed, cabal, or clique or clan—
Ye sons and daughters N-ENGLISH, IRISH,
SCOTCH—true Britons all!
Your offspring hands for Motherland ye joined at
Empire’s call:
Now, join hearts here; sink strife and schism ; •
outvie far-named Idalia;
Send honest, righteous, loyal men to GLORIFY
A USTRA ij 1A !
Australians 1 Gather! Guard, your Right !
And let but patriots guide your Might !
18/3/1901.
F. Lyon Weiss.
PURTELL'S LATEST.
The Soldier’s Dream of Home.
Air —“ The Miner's Dream of Home."
’Tis nine weary months since he left Melbourne’s
shore
To fight for the flag of the free,
And he longed to return to his fond loving wife,
And children far over the sea.
But the fever was raging, and sad cruel Fate
Decreed that no more he should roam,
So while he reposed on his sick bed of straw,
He dreamt of his dear friends £ft home.
j Chorus.
He saw in his dream, all the faces he loved
He thought his poor mother stood near,
And he listened with joy to his bright little boy
And the daughter he cherished so dear.
His head was burning frightful,
From the fever contracted in War,
As he dreamt of the poor broken hearted wife,
He would see no more.
He dreamt of dear Melbourne the land of his birth,
And the school where he went at Fitzroy,
He thought he was home with his mother once
more,
In the house where he lived when a boy. ,
His sweet little sister, he saw her bright face,
As she kissed him and begged him to come,
With her arms folded round him she sang that
sweet song,
The song that he loved —Home Sweet Home.
Repeat Chorus.
When the morning broke forth, he awoke from his
• dream,
The cannon’s loud roar he could hear,
The bright sun was shining, and close by his side
The Nurse and the Doctor stood near.
“ 1 am dying,” he murmured, for Empire and
Queen,
And the flag that is justly unfurled,
So tell young Australia I died like a man
For England the Pride of the World.
Repeat Chorus.
James Purtell.
Price: One Penny.
Extra copies of this effusion will be posted to
any address in Australia on receipt of stamps for
number required,
Mb. JAMES PURTELL,
The Young Australian Composer of Many

A Card.—l desire to thank my numerous friends
and the general public for the kind and liberal
patronage accorded to all my Songs, .Poems, etc.,
during the past few years, arid it is my intention
(D.V.) to keep putting these Penny effusions
before the public, I sincerely hope to merit the
same patronage in the future as I have in the past.
My next effusion will deal with Death Sentences on
flimsy evidence, and also our lunatic asylums and
the perversion of Justice.—Yours faithfully,
JAMES PURTELL.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Poor Man's Prayer.
Protect me, Lord, from these Thy saints, the
sanctimonious few;
Oh ! save me from their clutches when my mort-
gages come due.
Oh ! put me not into their hands —these brethren
long and pale,
Who call this earth a “ vale of tears” and promptly
seize the vale.
Oh ! guard me from the blue-nosed good, who lend
at 10 per cent, .
And take a good five hundred lien for ninety
shillings lent.
Make me instead the debtor of some man with
human taints —
At any rate, protect me, Lord, from these Thy
pallid saints.
Oh ! save me from the sanctified, the too uncom-
mon good,
Who tell us what we shouldn’t do, and preach us
what we should;
Those saints who squeeze a shilling twice and
wear cheap aureoles ;
Wno take our children’s bread and then attempt
to save our souls !
Give me instead a worldly man, with some few
healthy stains
That show he has the common blood of manhood
in his veins,
And heart that swells enough sometimes to over
throw constraints,
But in my need protect me, Lord, from these Thy
hungry saints.
MAORILAND.
The Master Mood.
The moon hath lifted the heart of the sea,
And great is the sweetness thereof:
Oh, great is the moon, and great the sea,
But greatest of all is love.
The stars withdraw from the bridal hour,
The sun shines not in the skies :
Oh, kind are the stars, and kind the sun,
But kindest of all thine eyes.
Exults the sea in the light of the moon,
The moon in the peace of the flood ;
And sea and moon they have filled my soul
With the light of a master-mood.
O thou who sittest so pale and still,
With lips like the reddest wine,
Be thou the moon to the sea —my soul:
Draw thou my heart to thine.
The Sea roars loud when the moon shines not,
And rage at his heart hath he ;
And I will go like a lion forth
If thou smile not on me.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
Horse o’ Gold.
Fantasy, Fantasy, fly away !
I’ll mount you; we’ll follow the hurrying Day,
Chase the gold through amethyst lakes,
Burn our bosoms with scarlet flakes,
And leaping over the sunset’s brim
Steal a chord of the young star’s hymn.
Ah, Fantasy, you and I
Stop for nothing on earth or sky !
Charger of Gold, with arrogant feet,
Carry me, serve me !
Let me ride and dream on your haughty hock
Till ive come to the Little Death Track;
Then, Fantasy ! then, ah then,
A bosom of earth, and a horse of black.
Fantasy, Fantasy, fly away !
I’ll mount you : we’ll follow no Gold to-day,—
Turn our ways to some uttermost grove,
Look once more for our Perfect Love,
And calling, calling, into the mist
Hear a voice that the stars have kissed.
Charger of gold, with arrogant feet,
Carry us, serve us !
Let us ride and dream on your haughty back
Till we come to the Little Death Track.
Then Fantasy ! Then, ah, then !
Her passionate arms and her hair of black.
Fantasy, Fantasy, ah, the day !
I mount, and you sadly refuse the way,
Tired or halt, with impotent feet,
Blind and weary of wind and heat, —
Ah, stumbling, sorrowful, deadly hour
When Fantasy falls like a rain-torn flower.
Once, Fantasy ! you and I
Stopped for nothing on earth or sky.
Charger of gold, ivith wayworn feet,
Onward a little !
While I promise and pray on your weary back,
Soon to come to the Little Death Track.
Then, Fantasy ! gladly then
I'll yield you, my steed, for a horse of black.
N.S.W.
LOUISE MACK.

SATT7EDAY, MAY 25, 1901.
Prior 6d.
THE SYDNEY TOWN HALL MEETING LAST MONDAY.
HIS COLONIAL OATH ; THE PARSONS TRYING TO REVIA T E THE SMOULDERING EMBERS.
Rev. Mervyn Archdall, M.A., was received with tremendous applause as he rose to move the first resolution, which was worded as follows “ That this meeting protests against any alteration
being made in the form of the Coronation Oath and Declaration of the Sovereigns of Great Britain, on the following grounds, viz. : —That by the Act of Settlement it is decreed that the Sovereign of
these realms shall be of the Protestant baith; that the aggressive claims and attitude of the Roman Catholic Church are such that it is necessary that there shall be no relaxations of the safeguards
which ensure the continuance of the Protestant succession; that, as the Roman Catholic doctrine of mental reservation permits oaths to be taken in other than their generally-accepted meaning, it is
incumbent on the Sovereign to make his declaration in terms that are not capable of being taken in any other than their ordinary and obvious meaning, and to declare his adhesion to the Protestant
Faith in terms that cannot be misunderstood.

In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
PI, with 2s. 2d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

to The Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.

exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition o/Tiik Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.

SATURDAY, MAY 25, 1901.
Colored Labor Legislation— At Once!
The colored laborer in Australia, like the
moon, presents through varying phases one
single face. In different languages and with
many tones he asks the white Australian one
question only : “Is this country to belong
to me or to you ? ” There is no third cate-
gory ; for unrestricted influx of colored
races is not compatible with maintenance of
the white breed and the white civilisation.
Either White or Colored must be Eclipse ;
for in the strife of races Piebald is nowhere
The Bulletin asks the Commonwealth Par-
liament to justify its election by voting
Australia All White.
• * * *
Even the restricted influx of colored
races means social disease in the present,
social danger in the future. For, like the
grave and the barren womb, the employer
of colored labor is never satisfied. Once the
thin end of the wedge is in, you can rely
upon him to drive it home and split our new
nation asunder. Look at America ! Who
would have thought, when the first cargo of
negroes reached Virginia in 1620, that in
less than 300 years the negro population of
the United States would reach eight millions
—8,000,000 of aliens, who cannot be per-
mitted to mix their blood with the blood of
the whites, yet whose birth-rate is greater
than the white birth-rate, and who are
gradually ousting the whites from some of
the former slave-States. Two hundred years
ago—even one hundred years ago—how few
foresaw such national danger from the plan-
tation laborer, the idyllic “ nigger ” ? Can
we not learn from America’s experience ?
Must we iu Australia pursue the old road of
error, stumbling into every pitfall, flounder-
ing through every quagmire, in defiance of
the Past ?
“A little more sleep, a little more slum-
ber,” says the sluggard. “A little more
laborer, a little more time,” says the national
sluggard of Australia the employer of
colored labor who is kept warm between his
Asiatic blanket and his Kanaka blanket. It
is into Queensland that the colored laborer
is coming faster than elsewhere; and his
employer pleads continually for “a little
more laborer ! a little more time ! ” He
never dares to advocate permanent admis-
sion of colored laborers ; but he pleads for
“ an extension of time ” before the dreaded
day of exclusion. As long as 12 years ago,
A. S. Cowley—the gentleman who says
“I love the Kanaka ! ” and who is, perhaps,
the most prominent Queensland advocate of
colored labor—said in Queensland Parlia-
ment (Hansard, June 6, 1889): “An ex-
tension of five years would answer all present
requirements, and would not pledge the
country to anything very great.” Only five
years—in 1889 ! Only five years —in 1901 !
The colored laborer, even by his advocates,
is always put forward as “ temporary.” But
well those advocates know that you have
only to continue “ temporary ” long enough,
and it spells Permanent. Look at America !
* * * *
Yet every Queenslander who has studied
the colored-labor question knows that the
introduction of colored laborers, even tem-
porarily, involves the gravest national risk.
The two most prominent Queensland
politicians in recent years were Thomas
MTlwraith and S. W. Griffith, the latter
now Q. Chief .Justice. More than twenty
years ago, on May 22, 1877, Thomas
MTlwraith spoke these words in Queens-
land Parliament—
We have to deal with a real tangible evil- -the presence
of an alien and degraded race, who are coming more and
more into competition with us every year, and crushing
out the white population. . . '. White labor has
deteriorated in the interior, although the rate of wages
may still be the same ; and this, I contend, is owing to
the fact that the best part of the population has been
thrust out in the face of the black labor. Therefore, I
maintain that it is the duty of the Government . . .
to attack the evil at the foundation, and to adopt this
night some steps by which the House will commit itself
to the abolition of the traffic —at all events at some future
time, if it cannot he done at once. . . . It is a mistake
to suppose that the sugar industry cannot be carried on
without black labor, and if many of the small farmers,
who have been reduced almost to starvation by growing
maize, had been employed in sugar growing, they would
be in a better position than they are now.
Six years later, on July 30, 1883, S. W.
Griffith published this as his opinion—
After a very full consideration of the matter, I am con-
vinced that it is impossible for an inferior servile class,
and a free white laboring population, to permanently
exist side by side with one another.
Nine years later still, S. W. Griffith’s
opinion had not changed ; for, in his mani-
festo to the electors of Brisbane in 1892, he
wrote—
The permanent existence of a large servile population
amongst us, not admitted to the franchise, is not com-
■ patibie with the continuance of our free political insti-
tutions.
And The Bulletin affirms that those are
Chief-Justice Griffith’s opinions to-day ; it
challenges him to deny them—despite the
fact that in 1892 he weakly yielded to the
“extension” plea, and revoked the policy
of exclusion based on the report of the
Royal Commission seven years before.
Here, then, are Queensland’s two most pro-
minent men uttering, with full knowledge,
deliberate opinions adverse to the continu-
ance of colored labor. Even the chief
Brisbane, newspapers, when it suited their
policy to look the facts in the face, have
denounced the coloured-labor traffic. So
lately as 14th May, 1895, Brisbane Tele-
graph wrote that “Nothing that can be said
in favor of Kanaka labor should reconcile us
to the idea of its permanence on these
shores.” And, as for Asiatic labor, even A.
S. Cowley condemns it.
• * * *
And why this unanimity of opinion (when
they think they can afford to speak the
truth) from these men and others who know
most about colored labor ? Because they
have seen the thing go on ; they have looked
beyond the present into the national future ;
and they have looked at America. The
colored laborer is a danger, firstly, because
he is cheap ; and the curse of cheap labor is
that it always tends to swallow up dear
labor, even moderately-paid labor. That is
an economic fact—a commonplace of the
text-books—an induction from a million
observations. Employers’ greed or competi-
tion invariably forces wages down to the sub-
sistence-limit, or as nearly as circumstances
admit. The colored man lowers the subsist-
ence-limit. A Japanese tradesman can sup-
port a family on 10s. to 15s. a week ; a Jap.
laborer does it on less. The regulation
Queensland Kanaka’s wage is £6 a year,
with food (!) and clothes (!!) and lodging (!!!)
estimated to cost about £9 a year more.
And it is these men whose competition the
white Queensland laborer has to face. How
can he do it, without descending to their
level of life ? How can he descend to their
level of life without descending to their
level of civilisation—without losing his
white value, his white attributes, and becom-
ing a kind of “white nigger” himself?
Even the small cane-farmers in Queensland,
supplying mills erected by Government
money, employ colored labor as far as possi-
ble—employ it and indent it. They want
cheap labor just as much as the capitalists ;
they have to compete with the capitalists
who employ the Jap. and the Kanaka.
And every year in Queensland sees
the colored - laborer extend his boun-
daries. He is supposed to be brought
for plantation-work only, but he does
many things beside plantation - work.
Before the rising tide of cheap labor, the
white man and the white civilisation are
every year receding. Thomas MTlwraith
saw that. The Associated Workers’ Union
at Mackay (Q.) saw it when its 1894 mani-
festo affirmed that “ the tendency here is to
reduce the white worker to the level of the
Kanaka.” A public meeting at Ballina
(N.S.W.) in 1891, three years before, had
affirmed the same thing —“ Hindoo competi-
tion reduces the price of white labor to
Hindoo rates.”
* * * *
Further, if Australia is to be a coun-
try fit for our children and their
children to live in, we must Keep the
Breed Pure. The half-caste usually inherits
the vices of both races and the virtues of
neither. Do we want Australia to be a
community of mongrels ? There are now
some 100 Queensland Kanakas married to
white women ; do we want an increase
of such marriages or similar mar-
riages ? No ; for such union is degra-
dation. Brutal whites fall as low
as brutal Asiatics ; but Asiatics have not
white possibilities. Whatever our failings,
we are the heirs of European civilisation ;
and we cannot merge our nationality in a
barbarism, or in an alien civilisation to which
European ideals are incomprehensible.
* • * *
So, if there were question of the ruin of
Australia or the ruin of the sugar industry,
The Bulletin would unhesitatingly prefer
the ruin of the sugar industry. It would
not put sugar before civilisation, sugar
before humanity. But there is no such
question. The proof that white labor can
do all the work of the sugar industry is found
in the fact that white men are doing it—in
New South Wales, and in Queensland as far
north as Cairns. That is not the main
point; for colored labor has to be excluded
because it is a cancer in a white community—
a menace to the white Australian race. And
it has to be excluded now. The State most
concerned is Queensland ; and Queensland’s
wish was signally shown at the Senate elec-
tion, w’hen, on a clear issue, manhood-
suffrage elected all white-labor advocates,
and rejected all notorious colored-labor
advocates. Yet already the Queensland
members of the Commonwealth Parliament,
are complaining of the Barton Cabinet’s
indecision, of the Barton Cabinet’s very
guarded language in the Opening Speech.
And it is whispered to The Bulletin from
Queensland that a very prominent member
of the Barton Cabinet has given a highly
confidential hint to the Queensland Govern-
ment ring and the Queensland financial ring
that colored-labor will be let down as lightly
as possible with “ another extension—a little
more laborer—a little more time ! ”
• * * *
There is no need of indecision. There is
no room for dubious language. There is no
reason for “a little more laborer—-a little
more time.” There is no excuse for waiting
for reports and Commissions—all the in-
genious apparatus of delay—while indents
are being hurried forward, cables sent, re-
cruiting-vessels despatched in a hurry ; and
every effort is made by the colored-labor
gang to get in some thousands more cheap
aliens before the door closes.
* + *
Let the Barton Cabinet move at once—
the first week that Parliament meets. There
is no need to complicate legislation by deal-
ing immediately with the colored population
that is already here. The vital thing is to
stop the comi"g flood. Let the Legislature
pass at once a short Act levying a £lOO poll-
tax on all colored immigrants, as is done in
N.S.W. in the case of the Chinaman, or
applying a stringent education-test ; and let
the Govt, anticipate assent by regulation,
without losing one day. There can be no
question of the Commonwealth’s right to do
this ; nor of the necessity to do it. There
is only one question : Is the Barton Ministry
courageous enough to do it ? And, if it is
not, will the Commonwealth Parliament see
to it ?
The Case of See and Lee.
New South Wales will shortly have a State elec-
tion, and J. See and G. Lee have both laid their
programmes before the electors. The people
know See as the second-in-command of the bad,
shuffling, prodigal Lyne Ministry. It remembers
Lee as an inferior hanger-on of the still worse
Reid Ministry—worse because it had all the bad
qualities of the Lyne Government, together with
a unique capacity for mendacity, and an aggregate
snuffle that the olden Pharisee might have envied.
See is Premier, and Lee wants to be Premier.
See isn’t a wildly-promising Premier, and Lee
has all the ear-marks of a dull and ordinary public
ass; the fact that he never rose to be more than a
stop-gap and a last chance in the very ordinary
crowd which included Garrard and Sunday
BrunkEr points strongly that way. Both See
and Lee promise a reduction of the
membership of the State Parliament (in-
cluding Council Reform of some kind),
an improved system of Local Government,
land-law reform (good old stand-by), and many
other things. On most of these and the other
subjects both men are vague and painfully non-
committal. On almost every point Lee is either
“ prepared to take steps ” or to “ give the subject
his earnest consideration,” or he is going “to
deal with the matter, ” or else he intends to appoint
a commission, or he proposes to “facilitate”
things. See is a little more definite, but not
much.
* * * *
See comes before the public as the representa-
tive of a Government which, with only 20 months
of office, and these sadly broken in upon by
Federal elections and Federal politics, at least
contrived to give the country two great demo-
cratic measures—the Old-Age Pension law and
the Early-Closing law. Also be was a leading
member of the Government which gave N.S.W.
One-Man-One-Vote. Lee represents the party
which promised N.S.W. a Local Government Bill,
and indignantly withdrew it because the House
refused to let it give the Fatman sixteen votes at
municipal elections, and cut him down to four;
which withdrew its Closer Settlement Bill; which
was twice returned with a majority for the special
purpose of reforming the Council and left it unre-
formed ; and which promised a State Bank, a
State note-issue, a good Industrial Arbitration
law, and many other things, and gave none of
them. He professes to represent the gang of
political liars and fakirs who called themselves
Freetraders, and raised over £700,000 a year by
Protective duties, and denied that they did it. On
the other hand, Lee is such a painfully humble
person that he can’t be held responsible for these
things. To hold him responsible would be like
charging the faults of the commander-in-chief to
the ammunition-mule.
* * * •
See represents a Ministry which has the worst
and most shameless record for financial faking
in N.S.W. history —except the record of the
Ministry with which Lee was connected. The
Lyne-See Ministry is spending loan money at
the rate of about 2f millions per annum. It
has made its revenue look about £126,000 more
than it fairly is by taking possession of a large
area of Sydney and spending the* rents as if they
were clear income with no liabilities against them.
When it pays for the resumed property, which it
must do some day, it will also have to pay interest
on the purchase money from the date when it
took the property over, and all these rents
will be wanted to pay the interest with. There-
fore they are really trust funds. But by gaily
“ blowing ” the rents this year, and leaving the
interest to be paid next year, the disgraceful
Lyne-See Government makes things look better
for the present and cheerfully damns the future.
* * * *
Further, the Lyne-See Government, which See
now represents, is the awful crowd of political
jobbers who annexed as revenue the extra railway
receipts created by the rush of people to witness
the Commonwealth celebrations, and didn’t charge
the cost of these celebrations to expenditure.
Neither did it charge to expenditure tne plague
outlay nor the war bills. It hadn’t the front to
pay these items out of permanent loans, nor to
add them to the deficit, so £445,000 of money
actually paid away isn’t mentioned among the
public expenditure anywhere. It only appears
among the trust funds. These are crude, stodgy
ways of frying the public accounts. In any
country where the finances weren’t run on the
principles of the confidence-man and the wheel-
of-fortune spieler, not only would the Lyne-See
Ministry be dead on account of things like these
—it would also be so dead that none of its mem-
bers would be ever likely to see office again. And,
in addition to all this, the Lyne-See Ministry is
using the loan funds for purposes of ordinary ex-
penditure—purposes other than the “ permanent
and reproductive works ” for which they are sup-
posed to be reserved—at the rate of about £850,000
per annum.
* * * *
And, on the other hand, Lee was a member of
the Reid Ministry, which first reduced politico-
financial dodgery to a science—which practically
invented the system of stealing the loan funds for
revenue purposes which did the historical
£700,000 “fake” in 1896—which made the public
finance of N.S.W. a cash Ananias, and a swindle
and a leprosy, and a thing beside which a cork-
screw would look like a straight line. And the
best that can be said for Lee personally is that
he was such a very temporary and obscure mem-
ber of the Ministry that he couldn’t be held per-
sonally liable for these things. Practically Lee is
acquitted on the ground of deficient intelligence.
* * * *
See says nothing about reforming the monetary
methods of the old Lyne Ministry, but Lee says
he is much shocked at See’s doings. Lee declared,
in his address of last Thursday night, that the
building of roads, bridges and harbour works out
of loan moneys and calling them “ permanent and
reproductive works” was a Wrong Thing, and he
said this was the first time—the very first time—
he had heard such things called “reproductive.”
As Lee was a member of the Reid Government
which spent nearly £3,000,000 of loan money in
four years on exactly the same kind of unpro-
ductive jobs, and also called them “ permanent
and reproductive,”—the Government which prac-
tically invented this unsavoury dodge; and as
Lee, before he entered that Government, consis-
tently voted for and supported it and its
road and bridge dodges, the said Lee looks like
either a very weary humbug or a bitter and
unexampled public ass. And Lee, though he was
so hurt at the See Government doing these things,
didn’t offer anything at all resembling a promise
that he would leave off doing them if he became
Premier. He also promised to largely reduce
railway fares, so it seems likely that, in order to
keep up a pretence of solvency, he will have to
boil the public accounts even harder than the See
crowd has been doing, though he probably doesn’t
realise this yet.
* * * *;
Also Lee was justly indignant on the night of
16th May, 1901, because the Lyne-See Govern-
ment hadn’t called Parliament together since its
adjournment on sth December, 1900—a period of
162 days—and called on jthe community to smite
the Cabinet which thus ruled “behind the backs
of the people.” The Lyne-See crowd has cer-
tainly this much excuse : There has been a
Federal election. It was impossible for many
weeks to get Parliament together because half its
members were away canvassing Federal elec-
torates. It was difficult to get Parliament to-
gether after the election because almost every
politician was away celebrating, and waving his
brolly, and uttering wild cheers in Melbourne.
And so many State members have stayed in
Melbourne to be Federal members that there is
no Parliament —only a tatter of a Parliament —
left to call together until after the general elec-
tion. If the tatter was called together, and pro-
fessed to legislate, many electorates would have
no member at all, and the way Lee and his crowd
would curse about the unconstitutional character
of that proceeding may be guessed. Besides, Lee
himself belonged to a Ministry which, without
any such excuse, kept Parliament shut up for 145
consecutive days in 1895-96 and .162 consecutive
days in 1896-97, so Lee’s shocked state seems
quite uncalled-for.
* * * *
Behind See and Lee, the two dull, incompetent,
mud-throwing financiers, stand the main causes of
the trouble—Sydney’s two disastrous morning
papers. For years the Telegraph and Herald
have demanded “the reduction of the public bur-
dens” in order to prevent taxation being levied on
their especial friends, the British and German
Calico Jimmy firms of Sydney. They harried
Dibbs out of office because he taxed these foreign
vampires, and thereby raised about enough income
to keep the State solvent. They whitewashed
Reid’s huge deficits, and denied their existence,
for Reid freed the rich foreign importers of mo3t
of their tax burdens ; and the fact that it was only
done by leaving the State in a condition of prac-
tical insolvency had to be covered up. Few
printed sheets have been so grievous a curse to
any country as the two shoddy organs of Sydney,


and very few people have been such a public dis-
aster as the conductors of them.
» * • *
And if N.S.W. rejects both See and Lee —both
of whom, on their financial policy, any sane com-
munity would reject—its third alternative is the
Labor party, which doesn’t profess to have
any financial policy at all, not even a
bad one. But, on the other hand, it
has never been in office, and it has no
frauds and deficits of its own to its discredit. If
it announced itself now as the party which was
going to absolutely stop deficits, and borrowing
for unproductive works, and the use of loans as
revenue, and promised to make N.S.W. raise
enough money to pay its way, it would get
a lot of support. If it declared that it
would no longer vote for, or connive at,
the frauds of either the See party or the
Lee party, and that in future it was going to play
a lone hand as the Honest party, it would start
a new era in politics. It would be the possessor
of the only political war-whoop that hasn’t been
talked to death. It would be the one refreshing
novelty in Parliament. It would be the one
crowd which had something fresh to say,
and which had a policy that would do good
to everybody. The Labor party is supposed
to be the party of democratic ideals, and if public
honesty isn’t a democratic ideal then it is time for
decent men to go out of the democratic business
That party had sufficient voting power to stop
almost every one of the frauds of the last six years,
and it didn’t use the power ; it hardly even raised
its voice in protest. Still, it didn’t devise these
frauds, and it has plenty of time to swear off.
The See and Lee crowds appear to be past all
swearing off.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Bail-way Smash.
While the N.S.W. Railway Commissioners are
dancing attendance on the Duke and Dukess, the
little sins of their administration have been finding
them out. The terrible smash at Sydenham was
due to the simultaneous coming home to roost of
several small defects, none of which in themselves
were likely to cause any serious trouble. But
when they all happened together, several people
suddenly found themselves in eternity. The two
latest smashes do not fall so heavily on the Com-
missioners, but still they leave their mark behind
them, The collision at Rydal was due to care-
lessness on the part of the train-running staff,
and partly due to the Commissioners having failed
to provide other checks ; and the railway manage-
ment that does not provide checks for every ordi-
nary contingency is guilty of criminal careless-
ness. At Rydal the Western railway is only
a single line. A heavy goods train was
waiting there to cross another coming from an
opposite direction. The station is on a dan-
gerously steep incline (partly 1 in 75 and partly
1 in 95). That train had waited 40 minutes, and
the air had leaked out of the Westinghouse-brake
tubes, and the grip of the brake became relaxed.
The driver alone was on the engine, the fireman
and guard being on the platform, waiting to re-
ceive the train-staff from the incoming train.
When the brake had become sufficiently relaxed
the weight of the train began to take effect, and
the whole mass of engine and trucks commenced
to slide down the incline towards the track up
which the other train could be heard ap-
proaching. The driver jammed on the West-
inghouse brake, but it was inoperative; all the
air had escaped, and there was no power. The
grade rapidly became one in 50, and though the
driver had reversed the engine, it could make only
the slightest headway against the weight behind,
and as the grade grew steeper it lost even that
semblance of control. The fireman and guard let
down the drop brakes, applied the hand brakes,
and opened the tap of the empty Westinghouse
tube at the rear of the van, and still the train
slid down, gathering speed as it went. Then
realising that they were helpless against gravita-
tion, they dropped off and waited breathlessly
for the smash, that came in a few seconds.
The Commissioners have sacked the driver,
fireman and guard of the train that was waiting,
for not having all the available brake power hard
on. That it should have been is, of course, patent.
But the fact remains that had the engine been fitted
with the steam brake in addition to the Westing-
house, and the crossing place fixed at a station
with a less dangerous grade, the carelessness of
the staff would not have had such tremendous re-
sults. It is merely a matter of money. If Rydal
is a regular crossing-place its grade should be cut
down or levelled up; and every engine in the
service should have something more powerful
than the ordinary screw-brake, in case of the
sudden failure of the Westinghouse. The West-
inghouse is a good brake, but it is not infallible ;
and every time it goes wrong on N.S.W.
railways something expensive seems to happen.
The practice of crossing trains on heavy
grades is bad, and shows slipshod manage-
ment. There ought to be absolutely safe
crossing-places provided at regular intervals.
The Commissioners say that had the train been
braked with the drop-brakes of the trucks and
the screw-brakes of the guard’s van and the
engine, the slipping could not have happened.
That is very good as far as it goes. But what is
required on a single line in a country of heavy
grades is a duplicate apparatus by which a train
can be promptly brought to a standstill on any
grade in about twice its own length. The steam-
brake on the engine won’t do that, but it will act
a lot better and more quickly than the ancient
hand-screw brake with which Pharaoh stopped
his war-chariot when he found it bolting down a
steep place into the Nile.
The Great White Elephant.
The Federal Premier has already allowed it to be
known th at hef eels no very immediate interest in the
idea of taking over the Northern Territory, which is
one of the most disappointing things he has said
up to date. He is coldly considering the matter,
but he isn’t rushing at it. In The Bulletin’s
opinion one of the most urgent duties and most
profitable opportunities of the Commonwealth
lies in the direction of the Territory—the Great
White Elephant of Australia ; but it fully recog-
nises that it has that opinion very much to itself.
And by way of putting as clearly as possible its
views on a large and very urgent matter which
doesn’t seem to seriously interest many people, it
summarises the case once more :
(1) The Territory includes 523,620 square miles (almost
twice the area of New South Wales), of which 739 square
miles have been sold, 291,596 square miles are leased, and
231,285 square miles (almost as much as all New South
Wales) are neither sold nor leased.
(2) It contains some of the best rivers in Australia, and
much good land (as good land goes in this country), and
a great part of it has a good rainfall.
(3) The population, when last reckoned, was 3600
Chinese, 1160 Europeans, and a considerable number of
aboriginals. There h one white inhabitant to 450 square
miles. The debt is about £2,852,000 (£1,013,200 for an
unfinished railway which hardly pays working expenses
and contributes nothing towards interest on cost ;
£452,713 for a very profitable telegraph line ; and the
rest for miscellaneous works and revenue deficits). The
debt is at present lumped in with that of South Aus-
tralia, but if the Territory and its responsibilities are
taken over the liability is £2460 for every white inhabi-
tant.
(4) The annual deficit is something awful.
(5) The whole trouble lies in the fact that the Territory
has no convenient market and no convenient outlet.
Population settled in the south and east of Australia
largely because the original gold-rush was there, and the
north has been neglected. South Australia can’t find the
money to finish the transcontinental railway, or to con-
nect the Territory with Queensland and N.S.M ~ so the
bit of rai 1 way already constructed is almost useless. The
Territory could produce almost anything or everything,
but its products are nearly worthless for want of access
to a market.
(6) It is just in the position that the Great Boulder
mine would be if it belonged to a person who hadn’t and
couldn’t raise the money to sink a shaft and get at the
wealth below, and who could do nothing but hold the
ground and see taxes accumulate on it till they ruined
him.
(7) If it remains in the hands of South Australia, all
that State can do is to lease or sell it for a song,
in huge estates, to land-grab syndicates, in order to raise
a trifle (it is only a trifle at best) toward the deficit.
These syndicates only run a few cattle, or float a few
wild-cat mines, and do nothing to encourage real settle-
ment. It is impossible to g§t an agricultural or pastoral
population until there are railways to carry products to
a market, and S.A. can’t supply the railways.
(81 If the Federal Government takes over the Territory,
and builds the railways, then it will have the earn-
ing’s of the railways (which would probably earn
something when they led to some definite place
and went somewhere); the earnings of the tele-
graph system which is already a paying property ;
231,285 square miles of land (much of it valueless,
by the way, though some is good); and a lien upon the
291,596 square miles of leased land (mostly good), much
of which is already forfeitable for non-observance of the
leasehold conditions, and most of which could be
recovered for closer settlement purposes under a judicious
land and absentee tax law and a system of compulsory
repurchase. Also, any big freehold estates could be
broken up for closer settlement by wise legislation.
(9) And if the Federal Government doesn’t take over
the Territory, it will have to build the transcontinental
line all the same, some day or other, for defence purposes,
but then the increased land values which will be thus
created will go, not into the Federal Treasury, but into
the hands of the big syndicates which are gradually
devouring the country.
(10) And if it takes over the Territory some years hence
the only result of the delay will be that there will be a
bigger debt to take over, less State lands to set against
the debt, and more syndicates to deal with.
(11) The commercial aspect of the question is this : Is
it probable that 522,881 square miles of unalienated
(though partly leased) land is worth £2,852,000 (say £5
10s. per square mile or less than 2d. per acre). This is
saying nothing of the existing railway, telegraph line,
and some sundries, which will be thrown in. Tub Bul-
letin reckons most emphatically that it is.
By way of an alternative proposal, a retiring per-
son named Butters (who is connected with a
Melbourne syndicate which has leased a huge
area of the Territory), and certain friends of
Butters demand that the Territory shall be
made into a separate State. Of course, it
would promptly go bankrupt, with its debt
of £24GO for every white inhabitant, and the
only thing it could do would be to hand itself
over bodily to the syndicate. And the syndicate
and its employes would probably outvote the
other 1160 white inhabitants, and return the Ter-
ritory’s six Senators and five members of the
House of Representatives (presumably these
would be the numbers) on its little own. Conse-
quently the syndicate would not only govern the
Territory, but have a very large say in governing
All Australia as well. And the syndicate wants
the Federal Government, in addition to other
trifles, to build the transcontinental line without
getting the benefit of the increased land-values
which will thereby be created. There is certainly
nothing of a needlessly bashful character about
the good old syndicate.
The Troubles of Preacher Hay.
A curious side issue in connection with the case
of preacher Hay, which lately filled up so much
space in the Sydney papers, consisted in the
powerful remarks made by the Law and the Bench
re matrimonial agencies. Preacher Hay has long
been the tame parson of one of these places where
the cheap and scrambling marriage is put through
with as much dignity as can be expected for the
money. He is also the representative of a more
or less spectral denomination called the “ Inde-
pendent Presbyterian” Church. A little while
ago the Registrar-General removed him from the
list of clergymen authorised to perpetrate mar-
riages, on the ground that he was not a regular
officiating clergyman with a findable congregation
“ within the meaning of the Act.” What the
exact meaning of the Act may be, or how findable
Mr. Hat’s congregation may be, this writer hasn’t
inquired, but when Mr. Hay (whose matrimonial
agency crust had disappeared) appealed, much of
the Crown lawyer’s time was taken up with de-
nunciations of matrimonial agencies in general.
Julian Salomons said, among other things, that
Preacher Hat’s conduct in haunting these places
and putting through the economical ceremony
there was an “awful reflection” upon his holy
calling—which perhaps it was. Also Judge
Stephen suggested that, if allowed to resume
business, he should abjure such agencies, and be
a better parson in future, and he promised to do
so. Seeing that Hay was abolished, for marrying
purposes, because, in the opinion of the Law, he
wasn’t connected with any sufficiently discover-
able sect, all this was, of course, a secondary
element in the case. Still these questions natur-
ally crop up : Does the Law object to matri-
monial agencies ? Does the fact of a parson
marrying people at these agencies tend to make
the Law inquire into his status as a marrying par-
son under the Act, when otherwise it might let him
•slide —in other words, is a marriage agency so
objectionable that its parson is naturally a sus-
picious character who calls for inquiry ? And if
so, does the Law intend to suppress the agencies ?
When the authorities hire an expensive barrister
to point out that any connection with one of these
establishments is an “awful reflection” on a
preacher’s character (present writer doesn’t know
enough about preachers’ characters to have any
opinion of his own on the point) its opinion of the
said places must be pretty bad. Yet these same
authorities allow the agencies to exist, and
Preacher Hat may have thought that when good
men like See and Hassall and O’Sullivan per-
mitted them to remain in the land that was suffi-
cient proof of their respectability. Now that
their absolute want of respectability has been
discovered, at the cost of much painful language
directed at Preacher Hat in a public place, The
Bulletin presumes that next Parliamentary
session will see them wiped out. If it doesn’t
there is a dreadful discrepancy somewhere.
-
Gambling' -with the Hangman.
Sooner or later the Federal Parliament will be
entrusted with the work of making a uniform
Australian Penal Code. When it does this it
should consider something more than the code it-
self ; it should likewise deal with the administra-
tion of it. At present the chief products of the
system are uncertainty of punishment and dis-
agreements of juries. The trouble is that juries
feel their position to be one of vast responsibility
with very little power. A jury is in the position
of a child who sets a stone rolling down a hill.
Once it gives its verdict—say in a capital case—
and starts the State Slaughter Department mov-
ing towards the neck of the accused, it has no
power to stop it. On the other hand, it is weighed
down with the heavy responsibility of endangering
the welfare of the community by letting dangerous
criminals loose to dv further damage to society.
In civil cases the jury has not only the power to
fix the guilt, but the <\ "ree of guilt, by assessing
the amount of damss. In criminal cases,
juries, in Australia, have no such power,
though they have in some other countries.
They can say “guilty ” or otherwise, but with the
punishment they have nothing to do. They must
either set the stone rolling or let it alone. A
jury that brings in a verdict of guilty, with a
recommendation to mercy, has no guarantee
that the recommendation will be acted upon.
In the hands of a skilful and daring counsel this
fact can be made of great use in defeating justice.
It can be hammered into the jury that it is its
Verdict that sets in motion the whole train of
incidents which finally culminates on the gallows ;
and that once it is cajoled into convicting, it has no
power to influence the consequences. Imagine a
dramatic, eloquent lawyer, drawing himself up to
full height, pointing with the finger of conviction
at the twelve, and hissing out something to this
effect: “Gentlemen, remember that it is not the
stons that rolls down the hill, but the individual
who sets it rolling'who is responsible. It is not
the Judge who passes sentence, or the Executive
which confirms it, or the official who signs the death
warrant, or the hangman who adjusts the rope and
pulls the bolt, who is going to slay that person
sitting there behind those spikes, but YOU !
And, gentlemen, for ever more, at every hour and
every place, your mothers and wives and children
will know you for a person, who, in company with
eleven others, slew a fellow-creature. You would
scorn to sit down with the hangman who pinioned
the prisoner, and drew the bolt, but your
children and your friends, when they sit with
you, will be in company with the man who
was the real hangman. When the wife of
each one of you caresses you she will know she is
touching the lips of a thing stained with human
blood—a thing that once broke a human neck,
and, perhaps, if she is imaginative, pictures of
you breaking it may flit through the silences of
the night and haunt the days.” It would be hard
to find twelve men—in a case where the law pro-
vides the death penalty, yet in which something
less than the death penalty seemed called for—
some of whom would not flatly refuse to convict in
the face of such an appeal. And the imaginative
wife is not a fabulous circumstance. She is a solemn
fact, and belonged to a Sydney juryman once
engaged on a celebrated murder case. She was a
highly-strung, nervous woman, and, in the illogical
manner of women, she asked her husband if he was
going to hang the prisoner. He replied that the
jury’s duty was merely to say if the accused was
guilty. The punishment, and the infliction of it,
were matters for which the Judge and others were
responsible. He said he wasn’t going to hang the
accused, but the local Ketch of the day probably
was. Like Pilate, he washed his hands of the
responsibility in a most complete and circumstan-
tial way, but his sensitive wife could not see it.
She went to bed brooding over it, and awoke in the
night, shrieking that she had seen her husband
hanging the prisoner with his own hands. In the
morning she declared that if the prisoner hanged
through any verdict of her husband’s she
would go mad with horror. And that prisoner
didn’t hang. He went free. Whether the case
was a rare one or not it is impossible to say—pro-
bably it isn’t rare. And the country doesn’t want
a judicial system under which murderers shall
have an exciting gamble, and mostly go free, if
they have the means to hire a clever counsel
capable of rousing the jury to a horror of its
unknown and indefinite responsibility. Yet so
long as the law demands life or nothing it will
continue to mostly get nothing. Wherever there
is room for the shadow of a doubt the thoughtful
and conscientious juryman is haunted by the
fact that he can’t find the prisoner guilty without
almost certainly consigning him to a penalty which
is beyond any possibility of repair if it proves to
have been inflicted by mistake, and for which the
wrongly-convicted person can in no way be com-
pensated. So the conscientious juryman (who is
the kind that is wanted) often refuses to take the
responsibility and lets the offender loose. And
the butcher-juryman (who is the kind that isn’t
wanted) hangs him, or anybody else, cheerfully and
isn’t in the least troubled by bis responsibility.
Hobart has a stipendiary “ beak ” who, the othss
day, sentenced a man to 12 months for stealing s»
few shillings worth of goods, and fined a “ push "
leader 40s. for assisting, without the slightest pro-
vocation, to kick a citizen almost to death. This
sort of thing is getting so common that even
Hobart threatens to wake up and protest. How
the average Australian magistrate loves brutality ’.
Even from a purely property standpoint, the m m
who was battered by the “push” suffered more
loss than the other plaintiff ; the damage done to
his clothes by his assailants was more, from a
strictly cash standard, than the damage done to
the other fellow’s goods by the thief. Therefore,
the only possible inference is that the brutality,
approaching very nearly to murder, was regarded
as an extenuating circumstance. The worst in-
centive to crime in Australia is the brutal push-
sympathising magistrate. The next worst incen-
tive is the weak-kneed, push-sympathising Govern-
ment. Some day, perhaps, a sane Parliament will
havethe courage to come down in real earneston the
push-ruffian by making 12 months hard labour the
minimum punishment for unprovoked assault in
company, but it is very long waiting.

There is a certain low-down quality about things
when the police and the J.P. arrange publicly to
lay a false charge against a man, and run him in
on it. That really happened the other day in
Melbourne. Old William Millard, aged 78, and
unable to walk, was hanging about the court
in a cab while the policeman explained things to
his Washup. Old William had just been dis-
charged from the hospital, as cured as he was ever
likely to be till he died. Something had gone
wrong with the schemes for getting him into a
charitable institution, as something often does in
the city of the Ducal Arch. He had an old-age
pension of 10s. a week, but he was quite helpless,
and he couldn’t hire a nurse or keeper out of that
sum. He couldn’t be left permanently in the cab
outside, for the fare was accumulating audibly.
He couldn’t be put in a nice warm gaol for
vagrancy, because he wasn’t quite penniless. So
the court agreed to charge him with “insulting
behaviour,” being perfectly well aware that the
respectful old man hadn’t insulted anybody, and
he was shifted off to gaol on these terms. It
is a beautiful satire on Melbourne’s benevolence
when his Washup and two well-meaning police-
men conspire in open court to bring a false
accusation against a perfectly well-conducted old
man to prevent him being left out in the rain.

Another Sydney Telegraph shout (16/5/’01):
“ The existing tariffs in all the States,” says the Gover-
nor-General’s speech, “ have given rise to industries, many
of them so substantial that any policy tending to destroy
them is inadmissible. A policy of destroying substantial
industries has never yet been propounded by a respon-
sible person in this or any other part of the world. If the
industries that have grown up under the tariffs are of a
substantial character, no one would be less inclined to
wantonly injure them than the intelligent freetrade
elector of the Commonwealth. Freetraders do not pro-
pose to interfere with any industries ; their policy is to
give absolute freedom to both the substantial and the
unsubstantial, so that neither may be taxed for the
support of the other.
The Bulletin hasn’t the least hesitation in saying
that the above is the flattest kind of lie. There
isn’t an alleged Freetrader of any standing in
N.S.W. politics who is game to live up to his
creed and give “absolute freedom” to all in-
dustries, and there never has been. To do so it
would be necessary to abolish the free newspaper
postage by which the Telegraph’s industry is
pampered and propped up; to put another 3d.
excise on local beer so as to put it on a level with
the imported article ; to enormously increase the
tobacco and cigar excise so as to put them on a level
with the imported articles ; to abolish the sugar-
duty, or levy a corresponding excise on local sugar;
and to take the existingproteetivedutiesoff biscuits,
confectionery, and a large number of other things.
Th.. is how they do things in England (or did,
until the new sugar-tax capsized the Freetrade
legend somewhat); but there isn’t one among all
the howling frauds of the N.S.W. Freetrade party
who dares to propose real Freetrade of that pat-
tern. There is much talk in a general way about
the “ bracing atmosphere of Freetrade,” but as to
letting it in on the protected Colonial Sugar Co.
and the protected Tooth brewery and the pro-
tected Herald and Telegraph —well, wild oxen
wouldn’t drag even a mention of the painful sub-
ject out of any notable of the great Dry Dog
party.

At the first meeting of the Federal Labor party
in Melb. 23 members were present. Some, like
the Victorians and South Australians, were pledged,
definitely to Protection ; a very few, exclusively
from N.S.W., were pledged, though less definitely,
to Freetrade. The members, of course, were
utterly unable to agree on the fiscal question.
Some couldn’t discuss it because they were pledged,
and the rest said it was no use arguing with men
who were committed already. In the end' they
had to comfort one another with the remark that,
anyhow, there were scores of questions on which
they could agree. But all these scores of things
are not the one test question ; while the very
thing they cannot agree about is just the thing on
which members will have to give a definite “ yes”
or “no.” And if the Laborites are divided on
this test question, George Reid’s troubles, and
the troubles of every other average political
leader, whether they are agreed on others or not !
The sequel of the murders of missionaries in New
Guinea —the so-called “ punitive expedition ”
has resulted in the indiscriminate slaughter of a
couple of dozen or so of savages and the destruc-
tion of certain villages. As the offenders in such
cases nearly always make for the interior, the
chances are that they have entirely escaped, while
many perfectly innocent persons nave been
slaughtered. And what is the good of it all ?
James Chalmers knew—no one better —that
sooner or later he would perish as he did ; and
when he landed in the delta of the Aird, he
knew that he was running into imminent danger.
Chalmers was an enthusiast who carried round
the doctrine of non-resistance and the forgiveness
of injuries into dangerous places. And “ religious ’'
people all over Australia —people who also hold
in holy reverence the doctrine of non-resistance
and forgiveness—howled for blood and murder
and vengeance when Chalmers and his companion
were advanced to that crown of martyrdom which
they, above all things, desired; The idea is, per-
haps, to make the mission business absolutely
safe, and so deprive it of its greatest attraction to
the best men ; but the missioner who ivants to be
safe has missel his vocation.

One of the features of last week’s Burst of Glory
in Melbourne was a sumptuous and stately smoke-
night at which the Federal Ministers entertained
the Select Press. Federal Premier Barton was
in the chair, with Morning Post Knight on his
right and the editor of the Argus on his left hand.
It was a solemn and impressive function. Barton
proposed the toasts of “The King,” “The Duke,”
“ The Governor-General,” and “ The Press,” and
—as if carried away by the gorgeousness of his
responsibilities—ended a gushingly loyal speech
with the triumphant remark—“l am no Repub-
lican.” There was no necessity for this statement,
but it told magnificently. Is Toby going the way
of all the other Lost Leaders ?
Was it the strain and stress?
Was it the bill of fare ?
Was it the Loyal Press,
Witli lurid eyes aglare,
That sent him rampant there ?
Alas, it matters not:
The fact confronts us here—
He rushed, with haste red-hot,
His loyal fame to clear,
And raise a Tory cheer.
He is no more his own
Master, for good or bad ;
The Shadow of the Throne
It seems, has sent him mad,
And made the Toadies glad.
“I’m no Republican ! ”
He said, that crowd to please :
Australia’s Chosen Man
Is going in the knees—
Prepare his obsequies !
Nay, let us fondly trust,
When York has left this shore,
And over is the Bust,
That Toby —sound at core—
Will sober up once more.
C.R.

The Cheap Politician.
The Federal Constitution fixes the rate of pay for
Federal members at £4OO a year until Parliament
otherwise provides, and, as it is impossible to get
the most capable men to work for the salary of a
manager of a little branch bank, the Federal Par-
liament, if it has any sense, will provide otherwise
(very much otherwise) with all possible speed.
Australia has either to raise the pay of its Federal
representatives to about £lOOO a year, or else it
has to hand over the business mainly to rich Tories,
who can afford to work for a nominal wage while
looking after their own interests; or to incompetent
poor men, to whom £2OO a year (the other £2OO is
inevitably absorbed in expenses) is an adequate
wage because they can’t earn more at any other
occupation; or to clever and unscrupulous men,
who will supplement the salary by steals and axe-
grinding. Britain, it is true, gets along with-
out paying its legislators. In Britain, 80
per cent, of the total wealth of the coun-
try is owned by per cent, of the adult
population, and out of its (say) 400,000 very rich
adults, it can easily find a sufficiency of legislators.
The natural result of entrusting legislation almost
wholly to the very rich is that Britain has the
most rigid land monopoly in all Europe, that of
Portugal alone excepted; and even the most
liberal of British Parliaments holds this awful
land monopoly as something sacred. If wealth
was reasonably well-diffused in Britain, instead of
1J per cent, of the population being rich while 98J
per cent, ranges from comparatively poor down to
destitute, then Britain would also have to pay its
legislators. Already the Australian Tory press
is barracking for a system which will
tend to keep Federal legislation (as State
legislation has been kept) in the hands of
the rich man, the incompetent man, and the pur-
chaseable axe-grinding adventurer. Though the
Federal Constitution gives unlimited power to
increase the remuneration of members, the
Sydney Telegkaph, with its usual vast inac-
curacy, denounces any such increase as a “gross
violation of the Constitution.” And in this matter
it and its Tory contemporaries appeal to a very
common public prejudice. When one considers
the calibre of the average politician he appears to
be well enough paid already, and the popular idea
is to wait till he shows signs of being worth more
before giving him more. But the existing man is
never likely to be worth more, and the other kind
of man isn’t likely to be found without adequate
inducement. No country buys a £2OO a year rail-
way commissioner, with a vague half-promise that
he may get £2OOO a year if he proves worth it. It
understands that it would probably get a man
whose whole value was £2OO a year, and that he
might very speedily make £200,000 worth of
disaster. Therefore it buys a £2OOO a year man at
the beginning, and the same principle should be
followed in politics. The cheap ruler doesn’t in-
variably give cheap and nasty results, but the
balanoe of probabilities is always that way. And
out of 125 cheap rulers it is almost certain that 100
will be just the kind one might reasonably look for
at the money.
SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Royal Grand Opera Co.
Lyceum “ Night Birds of London."
Queen’s Hall Snazelie.
Criterion ............ “ The French Spy.”
Palace “Message from Mars.”
Her Majesty's........ “Florodora.”
Empire Fuller Co.
Tivoli Rickards Co.
