MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Her, Majesty's. ....... “A Royal Divorce.”
Theatre Royal “ Woman and Wine.”
Bijou “The Power of Wealth.”
New Opera House Rickards Variety Co.
Athenaeum Hall G.B. Biograph (Last Nights).
Town Hall Trebelli-Dolores Concerts.
George Musgrove’s Grand Opera Co. opened
its second Sydney season, at the Royal, on Satur-
day night last, with Gounod’s setting of the dolo-
rous old tale of Dr. Faust. Grand Opera is a
world in which people carry on the ordinary con-
versations of every-day life in song, and even their
request to the old woman to fasten a refractory
shirt-stud is a solo. They swear in song, their joy
is a burst of melody, and their grief is a wail of
music, and, though it takes a long time to express
the ordinary emotions in that way, it is pictur-
esque. In “Faust,” the world-weary old doctor,
who has neglected to sow his wild oats at the
proper season, bewails his missed opportunities in
doleful numbers. Few things are so sad as the
sins we omitted and the badness we didn’t do.
Though no one can recognise a word Berthald
sings in his somewhat ineffectual voice, the dullest
listener cannot help understanding that the old gen-
tleman in the brown cloak is upset about some-
thing. Berthald looks, on the whole, a small, ineffec-
tive man to conjure up so very large a Devil.
And, on the other hand, Lempriere Pringle looks a
stupendous character to spend his time in pursuit
of the very little soul of Berthald. Pringle’s
towering figure fills the stage all through, and his
Mephistopheles has the dignity of a fallen angel
—not a mere jeering imp. Also, he has a voice
which it is worth going far to hear, and his con-
ception of the character is excellent. The Prince
of Darkness shouldn’t be thin and shrill, with the
manners of an angry police-court solicitor in a
dirty paper collar. Pringle understands this and
makes him a gentleman. He can be best summed
up that way.
• • • •
Madame Slapoffski’s impersonation of
Marguerite emphasises very strongly the moral
danger of moonlight and jewels. When Mar-
guerite enters the garden she looks somewhat like
a somnambulist, and one cannot help being im-
pressed with the idea that a little vice would rub
off some of the mawkishness and be a decided
improvement. Madame Slap’s Marguerite, who
may be called Slapguerite to distinguish her from
other people’s Marguerites, is almost impossibly
innocent and unsophisticated in the early stages of
the play. But later, Madame Slap puts her whole
soul into the tragedy, and she has a considerable
amount of soul when the occasion presents itself.
She and Lempriere Pringle are the only people
who leave a very enduring impression behind, and
Pringle leaves the largest impression. It is ques-
tionable if any other Mephistopheles that Sydney
has seen gave more general satisfaction. Law-
rence Mooney makes at least a respectable Valen-
tine, and Agnes Janson’s Siebel is a very fine
piece of work. “Faust” will probably re-appear
later in the season, but by the time this gets into
print the co. will be busy reviving “Lohengrin,”
“ Tannhauser,” “ Carmen,” and other trifles. In
fact, the succession of plays goes so fast that it is
hardly worth while attempting to keep an eye on
them. The present attempt is something extra,
by reason of Pringle’s Mephistopheles.

Tony, the sacred bear of India, is adver-
tised on Sydney Empire programme as presenting
“ an imposing appearance when standing erect on
his hind legs, ” and considering that the majority
of the human species fail to look imposing under
these circumstances, this attribute alone redounds
greatly to the holy one’s credit. Tony also plays
at see-saw, and walks a globe up and down a
plank, or revolves two globes among his pretty
black paws while lying on his back. But the only
occasion on which he docs not pause now and then
to utter ungentlemanly remarks to his keeper is
at supper, when he is invited to drink a bottle of
something to the very last drop ; and Tony
manipulates for that drop till he gets it. The
Lester Bros, perform trapeze acts over the heads
of the audience, thus giving their patrons a chance
in _ the slaughter that may occur some day.
Miss Ivy Fulton reminds Tommy that the nation
will look after the little things he forgot to attend
to personally—a series of remarks which by this
time may be regarded as a biting satire. The rest
of the entertainment proves that Ben. Fuller’s
variety show is sufficiently various to deserve its
name;

Thanks to the talent of Snazelle and the
punctuality of the man behind the scenes who
makes the water swish and the cannon bang just
at the right moment, “Our Navy” has been
arousing steady interest in the jewel-like theatre
opposite Sydney Lyceum. A picture of the
Queen lying in state has been added to the others,
and King Edward opens Parliament in the snow
and fog to stately music from an invisible source.
Our late Sovereign has died and our new one
opened. Parliament several times already in this
world’s history, and as the Cin. has settled down
to stay during the coming centuries, it looks as if
all our entertainments in this line are to be
clogged by Royalty and its various deaths and
burials. Snazelle moves his show on Monday to
the Queen’s Hall.

“Florodora” has evidently struck this
continent permanently. Sometime during the
present week it reached its 150th performance in
Melbourne and Sydney, and still the people pour
into Her Majesty’s to hear Gilfain’s many girls
declaim, “I must love somebody, and it
might as well be you ! ” and to watch the vagaries
of Professor Tweedlepunch and the lost Dolores.
A No. 2 Florodora Co. is being formed to raid
Westralia, with Cecile Englehart as leading lady,
but it will suffer sadly for want of the one and
only Lauri as Tweedlepunch. In the meantime
this paper continues to marvel at the taste of the
world, which makes such a success of “Floro-
dora,” and is only coldly polite to the more
brilliant “Rose of Persia.” It may have men-
tioned this before, but it feels sufficiently aston-
ished to repeat the statement.

“ The Night Birds of London,” at Sydney
Lyceum, has many discrepancies besides the
small boy who keeps the lost will so successfully,
first in the pocket of his night-shirt, and later in
the ragged garments graciously allowed him by
Bill the Slogger. In fact, his discrepancies
multiply at a fearful rate on extra inspection.
We note, for instance, that London houses are
generally built with convenient steps down from
the roof, and though this is handy in case of a gas-
explosion, it must be rather useful for the mid-
night burglar. There is nothing to steal in this
house certainly, except a child, but as the stage
infant is generally a valuable asset, and is
frequently burgled along with the jewellery case,
the changing of a homestead into a staircase may
be regarded as an architectural blunder. In this
play most of the people are married two or three
deep, and the heroine is continually in a state of
expecting the good husband and getting the bad
one, or expecting the Stolen Infant and getting
the husband—the bad one—again. She also
appears in her night-dress, and the pronunciation
of her name varies as much as her clothes. The
play ends in the usual way with the hero explain-
ing to the casual crowd which collected to see the
fire that he is heir to his estate at last, while the
poverty-stricken humorous man receives notice in
the middle of the night that he has also come into
a property. In the last act of a big melodrama it
practically rains property.
** * *
To-night (Saturday) Madame Belle Cole
and her Imperial Concert Co. commence their
Australian concert tour in the big Centennial
Hall, Sydney. There are a lot of shows in the
city, but there is still room for a good concert co. ;
and the Imperial C.C. has a strong cast. Madame
Cole, the contralto, has been in this country be-
fore ; and brings with her Mdlle. Virginie Cheron,
Avon Saxon —a big Canadian baritone, late of the
Carl Rosa co., and a young Sydney violinist in the
person of Raimund Pechotsch, who was born at
Waverley in 1888. Madame Rudyini, the accom-
panist, is also a Sydney woman. This writer has
discovered that there are a number of people born
in Sydney with fearful and wonderful names.
** * *
The story of the Missing Document of
great national importance is still being told at
Sydney Criterion, and the various people who are
all trying to find one another out still look care-
fully round tho stage before mentioning things
that don’t matter, and then blurt out really im-
portant fac.'. < when several of the enemy are
obviously listening. Charles Holloway acts well
as the chief of the Russian police, who grunts and
grumbles in general, but is very respectful when
anyone mentions England. On the stage,
foreigners become very humble, even in their own
land, when a casual stranger remarks that Eng-
land has its eye on them.

Wirth’s Circus, as already faintly indi-
cated in these columns, opens in Sydney this
(Thursday) evening.
« » *
In Harry Rickards’ French Parlor a nice
little girl with a pleasant face sings a song of a
firl and her motor-car, and the infantile Baby
’arkes does a humble ditty about a water-melon
party. Miss Rosie Aquinaldo is back again, in
green all spangled over with silver stars, and her
limbs and figure twine inextricably and wonder-
fully till she finally whirls herself violently round
her own head and sits on her hair. The new
arrivals are Austin Rudd, James Wallace and
Frank Latona. The latter is the much-advertised
Musical Tramp, and his dummy donkey seems an
intelligent animal. Frank himself is a sort of
handy man, who gives operatic selections on a
one-stringed instrument that was born of a ’cello
father and violin mother—and gives them very
well. His trombone solo seems to have an ex-
hilarating effect on the donkey and the audience.
All things considered, he is a valuable man to
cheer up the desert places between drinks.
** # *
Caliban, the knife-grinder, is still danc-
ing attendance on the ladies at Melbourne Royal,
and the gruesome chuckle of that brick-red citizen
is a principal support of “Woman and Wine.”
Arthur Styan—as The Bulletin forgot to men-
tion last week—resumes the matted, ruddy mane
and the vindictive hunch of old friend Caliban.
Also he wears the same blue blouse and blood-
shot eye that distinguished him a while ago,
but the deformity in his off hind leg looks
wickeder than ever, and his capacity for pro-
moting hatred, murder, and sudden death seems
to be further developed. Altogether, Styan is a
delightfully genuine fiend. Walter Baker’s lapses
into liquor, and his stern determination to make
things unpleasant for the woman who broke him
up, are likewise convincing. Crowded houses
have seemingly worked a considerable improve-
ment in the Bland Holt co. Miss Harrie Ireland
lets herself go vigorously in the character of
the Woman with a Marble Heart, and though
her marbleness is a little too palpable and
her walk off the stage sometimes suggests
springs in her boots, Miss Harrie promises to
play Miss Elizabeth Watson’s parts with capital
effect before her next birthday, whenever that
may be. The Bland Holts, together with their
live baby, and the Japanese ballet, the steeple-
chase and a number of other matters, will tend to
maintain “Women and Wine” in a state of
affluence till further notice, notwithstanding the
drama’s preposterous latter end. Most dramas
terminate in a blaze of absurdity, but the climax
of Bland Holt’s latest entertainment is received
with exceptional scorn.
** * *
“Melbourne Bijou, the late domicile of
Rickards’ frivolities, has been confided to the
direction of the Dampiers, and on Saturday night
the new season opened with “ The Power of
Wealth,” which odd, funereal drama happened
first in Sydney in August last. It is more or less
adapted from Marie Corelli’s story of an Italian
count, who got interred alive, and subsequently
shoved his way through his various coffin lids,
and made a burglarious escape from the
family vault, and returned, under a noble alias,
to the palace where his widow was fooling
with another fellow. The Count’s hair grew
white in the awful night when he was pushing
and clawing himself back to everyday life; there-
fore the lady doesn’t quite recognise him as the
husband she had planted. Nobody quite remem-
bers him, although he speaks in the unmistakable
tones of the original Dampier, and behaves in the
weird, peculiar manner of one who could a tale
impart. Having re-married his widow, and found
her no better than before, the revengeful, white-
haired Lazarus takes the lady down to his vault
and shows her a big box of rubies previous to sen-
tencing her to a life-long agony among the crum-
bling bones and evil smells of that cool retreat.
A severe course of conversation leads up to this
gloomy crisis. The entrapped victim remarks
casually that rubies resemble drops of blood, and
then the word “ blood ” inspires her to knife the
baleful Lazarus, who seemingly dies once more.
At this the lady laughs “Ha! Ha ! ” with great
violence until an earthquake comes along. The
family vault falls in upon her sin and wickedness;
soft pieces of masonry, like puddings, strike the
stage and bounce therefrom, and one long, flabby
strip of earthquake is understood to be crushing
the faithless Nina to death, what time her stabbed
husband recovers his self-possession. In the fifth
act Count Lazarus appears in a monk’s raiment
and talks gloomily of redeeming his soul—the soul
he had pawned in his trance. But a loving cheeild
calls him Father, by which means he acquires a
new interest in life, and his lost spirit troubles
him no more. He lets it rip.
• * * *
There are many moments in this loquacious
drama, and some of them are sufficiently im-
pressive. Dampier’s slow, incisive elocution
achieves a solemn effect at times when he speaks
of death and burial. Also, Miss Lily Dampier is
not afraid to stretch out her arms and express her
feelings in a large buxom manner which has
caught a little fury from Nance O’Neil. In the
minor part of a ruminative clothes-dealer, George
Buller is good, as usual. He curdles his customers’
blood by telling them they are standing in homi-
cidal boots, or sitting on chairs of ill omen. But
the unimpressive portions of “The Power of
Wealth ” are long and frequent, and the limited
audience on Saturday night didn’t promise a
brilliant success.
** # *
At Her Majesty’s (Melb.) there was an
interval to be filled between the departure of
Nance O’Neil and the arrival of Italian Opera on
June Ist, and the management is now marking
time with “ A Royal Divorce.” The present pro-
duction seems rather suggestive of its having
started on a Friday. The four tableaux which
commended the “Divorce” to favor at the
Princess’s are less satisfactory pictures when
they come under closer view. Cecil Ward,
as the little conqueror in the historic great-
coat, likewise suffers somewhat from odious
comparison with a heavier personality ; whilst the
Josephine of Miss Linda Raymond has a more
gay and festive air than circumstances warrant.
Yet, inasmuch as the previous Bonaparte and
his first wife were never taken very seriously, the
difference between them and their successors
doesn’t hurt anybody’s feelings. Lissant,
George Majeroni, Plimmer (as Augereau) and
several other people, including some nice girls
in strange bonnets and an airy French ballet
are gaudy figures in the proceedings. Miss Rose
Musgrove is a conspicuously fresh and clever
Stephanie—so clever that the present scribe, re-
membering the young lady’s quiet, pathetic Mimi
in “The Only Way,” begins to suspect her of
being a born actress. And the show derives a
particular Melbourne interest from the first
appearance on the local stage of Mrs. John
Sandes, wife of a respected Argus verse-writer
and daughter of Graham Berry. Mrs. Sandes
looks handsome in the unpopular character of
Marie Louise, and is likely to earn judicious com-
pliments from this scurrilous journal when rhe
nervousness has abated.
** * *
Rickards’ new Melb. Opera House, built,
it appears, on the lines of the Palace Theatre,
Sydney, came to light last Saturday, without
much preliminary flourish of trumpets, or any sort
of inaugural pomp. A fine, roomy house. The
Eillars that are a decorative nuisance at the Palace
ave been dispensed with in this case, and the
color-scheme is not distracting to the eye.
In short, the Opera House and its rich
furniture and fittings are all that was
expected, likewise more besides. The open-
ing programme makes a new and blessed
departure in the first part. The people who used
to sit around on chairs, gazing coldly and stiffly
at the auditorium, are now required to walk about
and make an animated background whilst the
“turns” are happening. As for the second
Eart, it is naturally all right. Marie
iloyd reveals an astounding hat to Mel-
bourne for the first time. Mme. Bobe beats the
Xylophone as no previous madame or man
has ever castigated that effective instrument
within the memory of the oldest inhabitant.
Rickards sings a song; also his young daughters, in
charming pink raiment, dance very gracefully
and warble a nice little warble in front of a fetch-
ing coon ballet. Moreover, Alec. Hurley, Mary
Lynne and Irene Franklin are there to add im-
portance to the dawn of the new Rickards era.
** * *
Mdlle. Dolores re-appeared at Melb.
Town Hall last Saturday in a white gown with
black fixings, and sang to the hearts of a large
audience, who had mostly loved her before she
changed her name. All other changes in
Dolores, nde Trebelli, are for the better if possible.
She is a beautiful singer and a fascinating artist.
She educates her public in the way they
should think about songs and singing, but the
culchaw imparted by Mdlle. is a kindly, unassuming
influence —not a bit like the culchaw whose voice
breathes o’er Bayreuth, and can’t breathe in any
other direction. Further Dolores concerts, which
include a pleasing pianist, Mr. Newell, in the
entertainment, will be given on dates not yet
specified, and for whatever date the prima-donna
announces a repetition of “The Shadow Song”
she may expect a large-sized crowd to hear her do
it. Always a great vocalist, Trebelli — i.e.,
Dolores—is positively immense in “ The Shadow
Song.”
** * *
Next lime Nance O’Neil goes to London—
say twelve months hence—she will appeal to the
female mind of the metropolis in a spectacular
play written to order by S. A. Mills, of the Bul-
letin. The subject is classical, and the name-
part seems to be better suited to Nance’s bossful
method than any of the characters in which she
challenges comparison with actresses of wider
range. Anyway, her heart is set upon it, whilst
the amusing Rankin vows that he sought in vain
the ideal heroine of this particular ancient story
for 25 years ere Providence introduced him to
Nance. By the way, there is no classical reason
why the heroine aforesaid should turn the scale at
15st.; but, on the other hand, it is open to Nance
to establish a precedent in the matter. The story
dates so far back in the dim past that nobody can
fix a limit to the leading lady’s avoirdupois and
force of declamation.
The M.L. Grovt. is making very stringent
rules under the new Health Act. When a person
suffering from an infectious disease has been living
in a room, the wall-paper must be taken down and
the room thoroughly disinfected. Bodies of those
dying from such infectious diseases are to be
wrapped in sheets dipped in corrosive sublimate,
and buried in quicklime, or, where possible,
cremated.

The Cobar Chesney mine, Cobar, bave lately
placed an order with the Austral Otis Engineering Com-
pany for a pair of powerful winding engines to haul from
2000 feet. These engines are rapidly approaching com-
pletion, and will he delivered about first of next month.
The same firm of engineers have also supplied the Occi-
dental mine, Cobar, with a handsome pair of winding
engines, as well as a boiler. This machinery was delivered
in very quick time and should give great satisfaction.*
* * * *
The Australasian Insurance and Banking
Record of 19th October, 1900, writes regarding the
Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society, Limited : “ The
Society can justifiably point to the adequacy of its
reserves, which are of a magnitude and a character to
command the fullest confidence.”*
* • * *
The Mount Drysdale Gold Mining Co. has
recently installed a rock-drilling plant, which should be
the means of largely increasing the output of this
property. Following the lead set by the Great Cobar
Copper Mining Syndicate, and other mines in the district,
it has adopted the Ingersoll-Sergeant type of drills and
compressor. The boiler is of the most modern return-
tubular pattern, and the whole plant was supplied by
Parke and Lacy Company, Limited, the Ingersoll agents.

Messrs. Plummer Love and Co., 249 George-
street, Sydney, are large suppliers of all mining requi-

* * * *
Most readers of The Bulletin will have heard
that Luhrig Vanners have proved a phenomenal success
on Broken Hill sulphides as also on low grade gold and
copper ores. All Barrier Mines remodelling their mills on
up-to-date lines include a number of them. The South
and Junction companies each have about a dozen, no
slime treatment plant being now considered complete
without these excellent machines. In Queensland tin
concentration is the latest accomplishment, the Irvine-
bank Mill having introduced Luhrig Yanners with success-
ful results, the example having been followed by the well-
known Ooolgarra Tin Mining Co., who are installing a
number of Luhrig Vanners in connection with their new
Tin Dressing Mill. Messrs. W. &J. Lempriere, Melbourne,
are the general agents for the Luhrig Appliances.*
• * * *
Norman Godkin, of 31 Queen-street, Melbourne,
is prepared to purchase or develope, with option of
purchase, “ big prospecting shows ” (either gold or
mineral) in any part of Australia. Properties offered
must be subject to inspection. Cable address: “ Option,”
Melbourne.*
* * * *
Mr. Mephan Ferguson, Engineer, Ironfounder,
&c., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Footscray
works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. -Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts, nuts, forgings, and all sorts of iron-work.*

Curtin-Davis Co. (Tas.) : “Four tribute parties
are working in the levels driven by the co. Occasionally
rich shoots of fahl ore have been met with, but, as a rule,
the veins are short, and cut-out in hard country.” How
refreshing the truth sometimes is, but how seldom it is
told, except of a mine that is being worked on tribute ?
When tributors start to mine, lying for the co. usually
ceases.
* * * *
Hercules (Tas. ): Said to have entered into a
contract to ship 10,000 tons of ore to Dapto, the first con-
signment of which has given a profit of £3 per ton. If
this average profit can be kept up, much better to con-
tinue shipping ore than monkeying with a sulphide pro-
cess which has been long promised to this and the Mt.
Reid mine, and never fulfilled to date. The time will
come when a man with a new sulphide process will not
only carry his life in his hand but will do a public
service by losing it occasionally.
* * * *
The old Durham mine, on the Etheridge, so
much messed up by the Bull co. that mismanaged it, is to
have another chance. This time some real good Croydon
and Georgetown mining men are taking a hand, amongst
them being Karl Karius (the Etheridge king), Fred Cuth-
bert (the lucky and plucky Croydon mining magnate),
and Peter Jackson (a canny Croydon Scot, likewise store-
keeper). A development co. is to be formed to open up
and prospect the property.

As compared with last year (when expenses were swelled

officers) the rate shows a large reduction. But even in its
worst years the A.M.P. remains, without exception, the
most cheaply-managed office in Australasia.
* • • *
Interest receipts, on the other hand, show that
the percentage realised on the investments is still grow-
ing less, which is also the case with all other Australasian
offices. Interest earnings for 1900 are £752,922—equal to £4
10s. lid. per cent, on the average amount of the life
assurance funds, or £4 9s. 7d. per cent, on the total assets
(life assurance fund and reserve for depreciation of securi-
ties). This compares with some previous rates as follows :
Interest Bealised on Average Funds.
1893 .. £5 13 6 per cent. 1897 .. £4 17 0 per cent.
1894 .. 511 10 „ 1898 .. 414 10
1895 .. 5 5 2 „ 1899 .. 411 10 „
1896 .. 5 0 5 „ 1900 .. 4 9 7 „
It is certainly a great slump, though it looks worse than
it really is. If the A.M.P. instead of keeping a reserve of
£245,264 to meet “possible depreciation of securi-
ties,” and keeping, presumably, that amount of in-
flated values standing among its assets, would write
down its assets by £245,264, its interest earnings as men-
tioned above, would be Is. 4d. per cent, better than they
are. Meanwhile, the A.M.P. makes good preparation for
facing the drop. Its estimates are now based on the
assumption that its investments will yield £3 10s. per
cent., so it has really £1 os. lid. per cent, margin. And,
with a caution which can’t be too much commended,
£90,255 is set aside as a provision towards reducing the
interest estimates to 3 per cent. Even after doing this,
the surplus available for division reaches the splendid
figure of £537,895 —the largest sum which the A-M.P. has
divided in any one year up to date.
* * * *
Most of the changes observable among the
assets are distinctly for the better. The item, “ Proper-
ties acquired by foreclosure, £604,100,” is £20,000 less
than it was a year ago, so it would appear that the dead
stock of land and houses is becoming saleable at a
better figure. The Society’s holding of State securities
(£2,533,231) shows an increase of over £300,000, and an
increase in this eminently safe form of investment is al-
ways commendable. If Australia goes on borrowing as
it has been doing, the time will certainly come when
these securities will not be eminently safe, but The
Bulletin still hopes that a lucid interval will arrive in
this country’s politics. And there are no “ deferred in-
stalments of annual premiums ” or like dubious assets on
the list. Taking everything into account the A.M.P.’s
balance-sheet for 1900 is the best in its record, and the
progress shown is most gratifying.
* « * •
The Equitable Permanent Benefit, Building,
Land and Savings Institution (Sydney) publishes another
of those painfully indefinite balance-sheets for which it is
remarkable. So far as all available evidence goes, the in-
stitution with the very long name is perfectly sound, but
its mode of presenting its accounts is exasperating. It
habitually pays 9 per cent, per annum to paid up and 8
pey cent, to contributing shareholders. The balance-sheet
never, by any chance, shows the amount of profit earned.
This year it is mentioned casually in the report that the
net profits were £7556, and as the balance carried forward
is £3392 it may be inferred that the dividends come to
£4164. Even this scanty amount of information, how-
ever, isn’t always available. The balance-sheet states
that the year began with a balance of £2209, and ended
with one of £3392, after paying, among other things,
£9839 as “ interest to depositors, shareholders and bank,”
and £35 “credited on investing shares.” Interest to
depositors and bank is an item of working expenses, and
after it has been debited the profit should be clearly
shown, and the payments to shareholders should come
out of that profit. The most definite estimate that can
be made of the position of the E.P.8.8.L. and S.I. is em-
bodied in the following statement of its total reserves at
different dates after payment of dividends
Total Total

March 1895 .. £29,003 ’ March, 1899 .. £28,339
1896 .. 30,438 „ 1900 .. 27,068
” 1897 .. 28,254 „ 1901 .. 26,682
” 1898 .. 27,313
The Institution, in addition to its substantial dividend,
pays considerable bonuses now and then, and between
dividends and bonuses it has contrived to reduce its
reserves considerably since 1895. It has now total share-
holders’ funds amounting to £97,629 (capital £70,947 and
reserves £26,6821, and, on the other hand, it owes £95,283
to depositors, which is a pretty heavy load to carry, all
things considered, for an institution with practically no
liquid assets.
* » * •
Thus Magistrate Beetham (M.L.): “It is in
the interest of the public to keep sharebroking as clean
as can be. If we don’t take care we shall soon have it in
the same condition as horse-racing.” Beetham isn’t an
up-to-date man. Some of the dredging swindles lately
perpetrated in Southern M.L. were far more atrocious
than the greatest turf ramps ever chronicled in that pro-
vince. The fear is that horse-racing may yet degenerate
to the level of sharebroking—not the other way about.
• * * •
The Critic (London) recently delivered a
“ Lenten Sermon ” on financial journalism of the day,
and selected the Financial News and London Truth for
some vigorous English. When Hooley was on the high
tide, the public did not expect to discover that the
owner of the Financial News had actually been in part-
nership with him at the time he glorified Hooley’s wares.
Also, it was found that, after “ Moses Moss ” had given
rotten concerns columns of puffs in Truth, he received
from the promoters big cheques for “ services rendered.”
The Critic says the public has been defrauded for years
bv these parasites. Of Labouchere the Critic says he
was a wealthy man before he founded Truth and added
to his fortune bv swindling the public, first through the
city column of the World and afterwards through
Truth. By his partner “Moses Moss” promoters, it is
openly declared, are blackmailed right and left, and the
paper’s readers are put into swindles by the score. The
cheque-books of Whitaker Wright, Frank Gardner and
other promoters could probably unfold a tale which would
cause a sensation greater even than that of the Hooley
disclosures.
* m • •
Dear Bulletin. —For pure cussedness and
malice aforethought, I opine the Australian coastal-
steamer about takes the cake. Lately had an interest in
the S.S. Blank, of the Blanky Dash line, which, leaving
Adelaide on Wednesday was timed to reach Sydney on
Monday. Bang up the local office Sunday afternoon and
was told she was expected “ sometime Monday morning.”
In Sydney D.T. on Monday I found under “ Melbourne
Shipping” : “ S.S. Blank sailed Saturday.” Then,
happening to look higher up the same par, I saw : “S.S.
Blank arrived Sunday.” Having thus learned that the
ship had departed the day before she arrived, I rang up
again on Monday afternoon, and was told to expect her
early Tuesday morning. More ’phone Tuesday afternoon,
answered that she’d be in first thing Wednesday morning.
Wednesday afternoon —again no. news of vessel, which
ultimately turned up on Thursday morning—2£ days late
in a 5-days’ trip. No stress of weather either. Quite the
regular thing. Apropos, when intending to leave Sydney
by a boat of this line, billed to depart, say, at 12.30 p.m.,
I generally stroll down between 5 and 6 p.m., and never
missed a boat yet! Bearing all of which in mind, is there
any doubt about the transcontinental railway being a
brilliant success ?
* * * *
“Alia”: Re the paper manufactory it is pro-
posed to establish in N.S.W. if a suitable site can be
found. Considering the great variety of raw material in
the way of fibres, grasses, wood, etc., used in the modern
making of pulp, what is wrong with a site on the Clarence
Biver ? Every year thousands of tons of corn-stalks and
husks and cane-trash are burnt off or ploughed in. This
could be obtained cheaply for working up into paper,
while the farmer would have a small return for what is
now practically a waste-product. Also, on the river
banks in the innumerable adjoining lagoons and lakes,
there are enormous quantities of reedy grasses that
should be capable of being worked up into the rougher
makes of paper. There is also the advantage of cheap
water-carriage both for the raw material and manufactured
article.
* * * *
Eleanora (Hillgrove) N.S.W. : “This scheme
for treating the co.’s 100,000 tons of tailings is on an ex-
tensive scale. These tailings are known to he worth £1
per ton as they lie in the dump.” It has been the prac-
tice to lie about them for many years. Possibly their
metal contents average £1 per ton. The fact that they
are base because of the percentage of antimony is always
carefully concealed, though it is well-known to those who
have hitherto made attempts to treat them, and to others
who have sampled the dump and then abandoned the
idea. There may be some way of making sdwt. antimony
tailings pay, but it is not known to the writer. Many pro-
cesses have been tried, but all have failed, and it is
almost hopeless to expect any system but concentrating
to succeed. Many thousands of tons of Eleanora tailings
have already been concentrated, and as a result the
dump has been impoverished.
Pambula Mines (a N. S. W. Bull-owned concern):
“ Cleaned up last night a crushing of 140 tons for 330 z.
gold. Tailings assay 28dwt. per ton, and are being kept
for cyanide treatment. Will start on Monday crushing a
parcel of 10 tons from Belle mine.” The taking out of
less than sdwt. and leaving 28dwt. behind is an interest-
ing feature. And when will the battery finish crushing
that 10 tons ?

G-irilambone Copper - mine (N.S.W.): Still
being worked in a hand-to-mouth way, producing less
than 100 tons of ore per week and shipping away occa-
sionally small lots of concentrates and picked ore. Much
said about prospects, but the question as to whether the
mine is paying or not remains unanswered.

application for suspension of labor - conditions for six

that if the co. was not prepared to work the mine it
should remove the machinery and let others do so.

To find out how the wholly-unsatisfactory con-
ditions surrounding opal-getting and selling at White
Cliffs can be improved, N.S.W. Govt, has agreed to
appoint a Boyal Commission —of three salaried officers of
the Mines Department. These individuals have to arrive
at an unbiassed opinion about how their own Department
and their own bosses have muddled things at White
Cliffs, or else they have to acquit themselves without a
stain on their own characters, and find that there is noth-
ing wrong, and that their own management of things has
been Al.
• * » *
Cobar (N.S.W.): First news for a long time is
that the Phoenix mine (once called the Berribungie) has
struck pyrites ore, some samples going 20 per cent., and
the average over 5 per cent. Why this dearth of news in
respect to the Cobar mines ? That camp produces far
more copper than the rest of N.S.W., and even more gold
than any other N.S.W. mining field. Its production to-
day in both metals is probably less than half what it will
be within the next year or two ; and less is said and
known about it than about any other N.S.W. camp. For
all the public know to the contrary, both the Occidental
and Mt. Drysdale mines may be shut down, instead of
which both are working and paying well. Cobar Gold
Mines, a Bull-owned co., keeps remarkably silent when it
is doing well. Believed to have recently started a large
filter-pressing plant on its slimes, and success has been
established by a high extraction at a low cost. How
many N.S.W. men realise that the Oobar copper-mine is a
bigger and more profitable mine than Mt. Lyell, and that
the auriferous main lode of Cobar is the biggest in Aus-
tralasia ?
* * * *
Five or six years ago what was known as the
Banfield process of sulphide treatment was referred to
here. The principle of the thing was that, as Broken
Hill sulphides consist of gangue and metals in mechanical
and not chemical combination, fine crushing is the first
requisite. For this purpose the Juggernaut mill was in-
troduced. The inventor of the process was an old
moneyless Cornish ore-dresser ; and he had for partners
in the enterprise two or three others of strictly limited
means. These men, like most enthusiasts, became pretty
generally known as cranks ; and as they had no big co.
behind them, and as their primitive appliances were
sneered at by the “ big ” people, the process soon ruined
them without getting a fair trial. Its failure then has
kept everybody off it since ; and with lead at high
figure nobody felt the want of it. Now, however,
a demand for something has arisen; and present
writer, who hasn’t and never had a shred of
interest in the concern, believes that some desperate co.
might do a great deal worse than test this poor-men’s
process. It seems ridiculous that, when two or three
mines that ought to employ 500 men among them, and
which actually did so until recently, are shut down, a
process which unquestionably gave good mechanical
results should not get a fair trial, simply because its in-
ventor was an uninfluential working man and his only
backers deplorably bad business men.

* * * »
The results obtained by the Wetherill (magnetic
separation) process from Broken Hill zinc-bearing “ tail-
ings” were recently communicated (it would seem
officially) to the International Institute of Mines at Paris.
It is claimed that from 80 to 90 per cent, of the zinc was
recovered (besides the bulk of the residue of silver and
lead), at a cost of under ss. per ton. The tailings contain
on the average 25 to 30 per cent. zinc. Notwithstanding
all which, the works erected by the co. at Broken Hill,
shut down four months ago, remain idle.
* • * *
Another system of concentration is suggested
in connection with B.H. sulphides and the zinc “ tailings.”
This is the Elmore oil process. It seemed too fantastic
to be worth much consideration until the issue of a report
of the test of a 700-ton lot of ore at a Welsh gold-mine—
the Glasdir, near Dolgelly. The recovery here was 80 per
cent, against 141 per cent., the best obtained by any other
process. The claim is that carbonate of iron, all earthy,
silicious and aluminous matters, and zinc blende pass
through the oil; while copper pyrites, iron pyrites, lead,
silver and gold are suspended in the oil. If this is
demonstrated, the process is applicable to the B. H.
material—which the ordinary gravity-processes only treat
with a very modified success. Some preliminary tests
have, however, not given much hope.

Ivanhoe and Boulder Junction (W.A.): Having
proved its own mine to be a hopeless duffer, this co. has
acquired an option over another called the King Edward,
in the Mulwarrie district, North Kalgoorlie. “ This dis-
trict is well spoken of by experts as one of very great
promise as a gold-producer.” Writer imagines that he
has heard something like this before. If foolish people
deserve luck, the Ivanhoe and Boulder Junction crowd
ought to achieve it in their new venture, for their folly in
spending tens of thousands on such a property is as bound-
less as it is incomprehensible.
• • • *
Ivanhoe Junction is quite another mine at the
same place. “ The development continues to contain the
value expected of- it. The lode is a large body of stuff,
and samples give every indication of permanency.’ Rot
—unmitigated and unadulterated —but rot nevertheless.
Perhaps, after all, it is only natural that rot should come
from a rotten mine such as the Ivanhoe Junction un-
doubtedly is. More than a million has been wasted m
hopeless prospecting around the big mines of Kalgoorlie
without finding one other mine.

Boulder Half-Mile South (W.A.): “In places
the ore-body is 4ft. wide, and so far as explored it aver-
ages 2ft. 6in. The seams of quartz carry visible free gold,
returning 1 up to 3oz. per ton. The bulk is of low grade.
Driving on the course of the lode should give better
results.” Why should it? Why should drives, winzes,
and all other workings, improve as they go ahead ? Some-
times they do ; often they don’t —but the latter is never
anticipated. If the seams of quartz went 30oz. it would
be of little use if, as is admitted, the bulk is of low grade.
The B.H.M.S. is a hopeless “ wild-cat,” known as such to
the school-children of Boulder City, and laughed at by all
men who have seen it and who know the difference be-
tween a mine and a lime-kiln.
* • * *
Hannan’s Brown Hill: 4538 tons, 56090 z. Ton-
nage greater and average considerably less than last
month, but quite good enough if it could be kept up.
Sooner or later that must be admitted as
impossibility. Shareholders would have known it
long ago if they were not kept absolutely in the
dark. The oxidised ores have long been exhausted,
and the end of the sulphides will be reached before
the end of this year, unless some new discovery is
made in the mine. It has been so pierced with diamond-
drill holes running in every direction as to make this
hope a very remote contingency. As a last resource, a
proposal was recently made to amalgamate with the
Northern Associated on terms which showed the dilemma
the Brown Hill was in. Both mines have been examined
and the verdict is that the Brown Hill has plenty of
machinery and no mine, and the Northern Associated is a
splendid mine without plant. So hopeless is the position
of the Brown Hill mine that the terms of amalgamation,
favorable as they seemed at first sight, have been con-
demned as not good enough for the Northern Associated
under the circumstances. A few months more and the
end of the Brown Hill will be reached. In the face of
this palpable and deplorable fact, it still, during the
recent share - boom, had a valuation of over half a
million—shares 90s. In the collapse last year the
directors only issued a circular explaining the true
position after it was so well-known that further
denial was useless. History will probably repeat
itself. When the last good crushing has been arrived at,
another circular may be expected, intimating with great
regret that the sulphides are exhausted, that the direc-
tors had been unable to amalgamate with the Northern
Associated, and that the game of buying mines at extor-
tionate prices only for the purpose of giving valueless
area to the Brown Hill is played right out. About
time Bull shareholders pondered over the fact that more
than half of the shares in the Brown Hill Co. have been
paid for adjoining leases from which no gold has been
obtained.
* • * *
Northern Associated: Another 500-ton parcel
of rich ore going to the Fremantle smelting works, and
the mine is developing remarkably well in the sulphide
zone. The deeper the mine the better the ore. Remark-
able that this mine should be so poor in its oxide zone.
The oxides seem to have been shed into the Brown Hill
in some way or other. That mine paid nearly all its divs.,
and big ones they were, from the oxide zone, and the
little sulphides it' has left are dangerously close to the
Northern Associated boundary. A well-known mining
engineer who recently examined the Northern Asso-
ciated declared that shipments of remarkably rich
ore could be kept up without robbing the mine
of its eyes, and that long after shipping has ceased these
rich leases would serve to keep up an average as high as,
if not higher than, any other the field.
The Great Western Railway promoters have
rushed their first venture on the market with the flota-
tion of the Barn Bluff discovery. Writer is intimate
enough with the district to believe that the Barn Bluff
copper (or whatever it is) mine will ultimately prove to
be a repetition of the Lake Dora show—that “magnifi-
cent” and “inexhaustible” property which is now as
dead as Grandfather Adam. The G.W. syndicate has
frequently spouted about the numerous Mount Lyells
existing carelessly around and within its dominions,
but if the Barn Bluff show is one of them, writer knows
of quite a number of neglected iron outcrops that look
equally promising. These remarks do not mean that the
Lake Dora and Barn Bluff discoveries are not promising
prospecting shows, but the G.W. syndicate ought to know
better than to rush on to the public a concern which has
been only a little more than scratched on the surface.
The Government official’s report, following on the violent
skite of the mine manager, a few weeks back, is enough
to damn a very decent-looking outcrop.
* * * *
Barn Bluff (Tasmania), the vicinity .of the latest
mineral “ blow,” is a sharp-looking peak on the Cradle
range, standing about 35 miles east in a direct line from
the Emu Bay railway at the Mackintosh crossing ; and 30
miles north from its nearest point to the .Great Western
Survey. During three months of the year it is quite
possible to experience a fine day at Barn Bluff, and at
times it is necessary to take your coat off ; the remaining
nine are passed in fog, rain, hail, and snow—mostly the
latter. A friend of writer’s spent two months there in
mid-summer, and the greater part of his time was passed
in severing the ropes of fog off his heels with a sheath-
knife. New-ehum, and old-chum, explorers and prospec-
tors are herewith advised to paste this on their hats:
Never leave camp, on the finest day, or any other day,
without compass, matches, tucker, and a rug. Never rely
on following a strange creek in the Bluff country, more
especially toward the south-east ; it will invariably, in
level country, lead you to a small lake, connected with
another lake, and another—there are hundreds. Writer
was once three days in a fog, traversing the shores of
small lagoons, and didn’t progress five miles in a straight
course. And unless you have lungs of leather, legs of
steel, a dog’s stomach, a clear head, and a good heart —
don’t go there at all. A few weeks ago a man named
Connolly started from Rosebery to the Barn Bluff coun-
try. His swag and tucker have been found untouched,
but their owner has apparently vanished from the earth’s
surface. The searchers have now abandoned hope, and
Connolly’s disappearance is probably another of the many
Tas. cases of lost confidence.
• * * »
Western Mine (Tas.): In a bad way because of
low-grade ore and a lower-grade lead-market. Last six
months, produced 439 tons less ore, and £7400 less value.
Re-arrangement of surplus-plant completed, and pre-
paring to carry down the mam shaft from 600 ft. to 800 ft.
For the first time in the history of the co. it is found
necessary to make calls for additional plant and develop-
ment, and, unless an improvement takes place in the
lead-market, or a big improvement in the mine, it cannot
be worked to pay.
• • • ♦
Mt. Lyell: Further improvements announced
in newspapers. In the north drive No. 6 tunnel, stoping
ore 35ft. wide of high grade. At No. 7 level the new find
has developed splendid sulphide and fahl ore. Assays up
to 20 per cent, copper and 15oz. silver. The market has
not budged, showing that people have long wearied of
these newspaper yarns, which, even if true, would not be
believed. Short-lived mines often reap the benefit of
newspaper lying, and long-living mines, like Mt. Lyell,
have to suffer for it.

Melb. University students are the only
people, so far, who have treated the Jookoyork
with cheerful and friendly irreverence. On Com-
mencement Day, when H.R.H. entered the
“ wi° n Dali, he was met by a stentorian chorus,
What ho! George, how goes it?” He was
cheered, as a matter of course, and he was also
openly congratulated on possessing a missus o'
whom the students could approve. In fact,
their trank .assurances to Mr. York that Mrs.
York wasn t half bad were almost embarrassing'
He was further treated to a great deal of advice
regarding the rearing of infants from the medical
side ot the hall, and was instructed to send the
same advice along to Pa-in-Stocking-Feet who
was doubtless having a hard time walking the
flow with the kids at Windsor Castle. George
didn t seem to enjoy the chaff at all ;■ but as
A WISE MAN.
CURIONUS CUSTOMER : “How many children you, John?”
C HOW: “No savee. My missy got seben.”
George never seems to enjoy anything, there was
no way of telling whether he was more miserable
than usual or quite happy. The dailies kept an
awful silence about these irreverences. They de-
tailed how Barton was importuned for a speech in
a set chorus, and how Peacock resisted a similar
invitation to laugh, but the jollity of the students
was neither denounced as a disgraceful affair
which would require another contingent to wipe
out, nor was it gently slapped on the back as evi-
dence of the good nature and high spirits of the
sons of the Naicest. There was no precedent to
go on, no previous royalty having been joyfully
“barracked” in Australia, and the sup-Press
evidently couldn’t decide in time whether to be
shocked or not.
* * * *
Bridget Dunn’s fate should be a warning
to all womenkind. She was a bright - eyed
and joyous domestic at Adelaide, and the way
she shined the pots and pans of a gratified mistress
marked her out as the future treasure-wife of a
happy husband. But in an evil moment the ad-
mirable maid consulted a fortune-teller, and when
she left the establishment she came away without
her gaiety. She never smiled again. In three
weeks she was “found drowned”; verdict, suicide.
Apparently she had struck a low-spirited fortune-
teller who foretold eerie things, and Bridget
Dunn took her fortune-teller seriously. Strangely
enough, a month earlier, another Adelaide girl
committed suicide after interviewing a fortune-
teller. If it was the same one it would seem that
that prophet or prophetess requires to be sup-
pressed. The ordinary kind who tells the client
for 2s. fid. that she will marry a peer and live in a
marble tower is only a cheap fraud. The pessi-
mistic kind is not only a fraud, but also a funeral.
A promising youth, who will presently be
reaping a healthy crop of wild oats at the age
when most men sow them, admitted in Colling-
wood (Vic.) court recently that he had been
drunk six times in as many months ; also that he
had surrounded four long si ee vers during the pre-
vious evening without much subsequent discom-
fort. As the child was just 14 he probably belongs
to the Early-Drinking Association.
* * * *
“B.”: The discreditable feature of the
celebrations in Melbourne was the wild, mad,
food rushes. Nothing so indecent has been
seen for years. There was almost no organ-
isation. Those who charged the refreshment-
tables first scooped everything; and the quiet
people, who couldn’t or wouldn’t trample on
their human relations, got nothing. And to
get nothing, under the circumstances, was
a terrible ordeal for hundreds of folks. They left
home early to get a place ; they sat many hours
waiting for proceedings to begin; and if they
tried to get a meal at a restaurant they found
every place crammed. It is stated that the Vic-
torian Govt, paid £35,000 for “refreshing” its
guests. A strict inquiry ought to be made as to
how this money was spent and why only the
strong, the impudent, the rude, the pushful and
the Hebrews could get anything.

Still another cry of rage :
If the arrangements for the reception of the Juke in
other capitals are to be as thorough as in Melbourne,
curses both loud and deep are likely to follow that placid
nonentity all round the continent. Everything went a'
smoothly as a spendthrift down the road to destruction
m Melbourne-for his royal nibs, but the people who
paid for, and in some cases themselves constituted, the
entertainment, got precious little consideration The
police restriction of traffic was so close and blundersome
that it was often impossible to get across Bourke or
Collins-streets for hours together. The tramway service
was utterly disorganised, though the co. didn’t lose
much, as it early acquired a sweet habit of collecting the
full fare and then dumping the passenger down about a
mile from the city and telling him the cars “weren’t
running any further just now.” The State school kiddies
gave one of the prettiest shows to the visitors -and were .
themselves kept waiting about on the day, unfed and un- .
howH f ° r ' 2? th f Ul " l \ t of the Domain fireworks, a
how luif,, maddened crowd was kept penned on Prince’s

HARD LUCK.
While shooting at Kilmany Park (Vic )
last week the Dookoyork saw a snake crawling in
Ins direction, and raising his gun to his rov-d
shoulder immediately took aim and blew it' to
pieces with great gallantry. t 0
Within a Gippsland brake,
Last week, with courage grim,
The Look he mat a snake
That crawled in front of him.
It did not want at all
To use the Dook for food ;
It merely wished to crawl,
As Nature meant it should.
The Look he had no sword
His dress not needing one—■
Its crawling to reward
And so he used a gun,
And blew it, as was fit,
To blazes, as you see,
Instead of making it,
Perchance, K.C.M.G.
C.R.
LOVE.
“ Love never dies,” says joyous youth,
“ Why argue such a patent truth ? ”
“ Nay, Love must die,” says middle-age,
“No matter how one’s heart may rage.”
The old and wise say, with a sigh,
“ What never lived can never die.”
H.E.S.

The medal presented to the returned con-
tingenters by the Jookoyork is about as
poor a sample of silversmith-work as even
a churchwarden could devise. One side is a
kind of dubious half-crown, with a profile of the
late Queen, and on the other a great struggle has
been made to crowd a panorama into the space of
an inch and a-half. A commonplace Britannia
extends a laurel-wreath, under which can be seen
in the distance a crowd of British soldiers storm-
ing a kopje, while behind her are a cluster of war-
ships m harbor. What awful taste the authorities
who arrange these things must have.. A two-
guinea prize offered in any art publication would
bring a score of far better designs from students
in their teens.

A returned first contingenter from the
Flyspeck intended joining the last squad of Boer-
huntars, but prior to the swearing-in ceremony he
demanded the sum of £lB odd due to him for the
first trip. Money was not forthcoming, neither
was the soldier. Writer saw him slinging
sawn stuff out of a timber craft’s innards, the
other day, while saying disrespectful things about
the State for holding on to his hard-earned £lB.

Talking of Marcus Clarke, his ancient
rhyme of “Bill Jenks ” often attributed
to Lindsay Gordon —cropped up at that cham-
pagne and smoke yabber-yabber which the Barton
Ministry lavished upon the Celebration Press last
week. “ Bill Jenks ”is the invariable recitation
of Goorge Jenkins, o*6, on such f«Uve tat
weighty occasions. Jenkins, C.M.G., ot tie
flowing beard, has a sort of historical right to do
this thing over the flowing bowl, because the
verses were written at his house, one Sunday m
the ’6o’s, when poor Marcus needed a humorous
outlet for distressful thoughts. Hence the title
“ Bill Jenks.” The author would have made it
“George Jenks,” but the longer word wasn t so
suitable as ‘‘ Bill.” Also, he foresaw a future time
when the dignity of his host might shrink fiom
the pointed allusion. The career of a Parlia-
mentary Jenkins casts its unctuous shadow before.
The most beautiful creatures in the Duke’s
entourage are the footmen, grooms, and outriders.
These are evidently all selected for their beauty,
and are tall and shapely and wonderfully shaven.
They turn out at a function as lovely as a band ot
male ballet girls, heavily painted and powdered
with lips incarnadine, and eyes bright with
the mysteries of make-up. _ The rouge and powder
are too painfully evident in the glare of day, and
give the observer that weary, unreal feeling which
comes of meeting a painted girl in tights walking
down the street in the sunlight. When frescoed
grooms are necessary to the maintenance of Koval
dignity it is time to wonder if, after all, the whole
business is as effective as a pantomime.

Premier Peacock’s wasteful expenditure
of a grovel souvenir on the Jook was to all intents
of speech denounced as foolishness by the recipient
when he said : “It would not need this very hand-
some gift, which you have been good enough to
present to me, to remind me of our visit to your
State, because I never could possibly forget, &e.,
&c. But, although no such gift was necessary as
a reminder of our visit, I shall always retain it as
a pleasing memento,” &c., &c. After which the
Jook passed the casket of blackyvood “bearing
specimens of Vic. gold and Prince of Wales’
feathers in N.S.W. diamonds ” along to somebody
who put it among the Royal baggage, and Pea-
cock’s grovel was duly rewarded with autographed
photos of the Jook and Jukess.

One big Vic. butter-making firm presented
H.R.H. of York with a beautiful casket made like
a butter-box, painted with the co.’s trade-mark,
and containing 501 b. of the company’s unparalleled
cow-grease. And then during the royal stay a
big shop-window in the city was loaded up with
butter, topped with a shrieking label which stated
that it was of the same churning as the royal
sample. This looks very like having royalty on
toast.
* * * *
“ One shall be taken and the other left.”
A Melbourne man was escorting his best girl
home the other evening when, as they crossed the
South Melb. railway-line arm in arm, a light
engine dashed by, snatched the girl away, and
went on leaving the man absolutely untouched.
The body was found on the rails a chain away.
*• * * *
The church still appears to take its conse-
crated bone-yard quite seriously. A suicide was
buried recently in the Church of England ceme-
tery at Birregurra, Victoria ; he somehow got
there without the knowledge of the clerical
authorities. When they heard of him, which was
in itself a profoundly foolish thing to do, they had
the remains dug up and ejected in great haste.
Where the bones got to after they were ejected no
one seems to know. The incident tends mainly
to show the church’s inability to keep its mouth
shut at the right time. A wiser organisation
would never have heard that the body was there.
A judicious wink in one eye and a judicious blob
of wool in one ear are calculated to keep many a
church out of shocking trouble.

“Via” : Be lady barbers. There’s one
at Cunnamulla, Western Queensland, though how
she got out there is a mystery. Cunnamulla is a
place where it threatens to rain sometimes.
Points on domestic economy and
useful hints for the home can always be
picked up in court during the hearing of
divorce or maintenance cases. In a recent
Vic. wife-desertion case an excellent hint
to housewives was given. Defendant ad-
mitted that he “drank a little.” It was
shown that it was not the little he drank,
but the frequency with which he drank it
that led to the trouble. He had sold a
business on one occasion, and he drank a
little, and went home with a big roll of
notes in his pocket. His wife got at that
roll in the stilly watches of the night,
abstracted five £5 notes, singed the edges
of the rest at the candle, and returned them
to hubby’s “kick.” When the good man
awoke next morning he had a “head, ’
and, finding £25 missing from his roll, he
was smitten with horror and remorse, con-
cluding in his haste that as those remain-
ing were singed he must have burnt the
missing documents in his blind, drunken
folly. When, long after, he discovered the
trick that had been played upon him, he
packed his bag, and went to be a consola-
tion and a comfort to another woman.
S.” : Was amazed at the greeting ex-
changed by two British man-o’-warsmen at the
entrance to Flinders-st. station, Melbourne, one
night last week.' One, coming from the station,
met another entering. They shook hands; and
then, before the crowd, fell upon each others
necks and kissed. The men were a little drunk, X
admit. This form of salutation is not unusual
amongst Russian sailors, but the sight of two
A.B.’s of the British navy mugging was not good
for a bilious man.
* * * *
The municipal health officer of Townsville
reported recently, that he had paid a visit to the
Chinese quarter of the sultry town and discovered
that the Chows stored their vegetables and fruit
in neat little rooms measuring seven feet by four.
Also, that the Chow habitations admitted of little
or no ventilation ; that the floors were rotted by
damp, decaying vegetable matter j and that a
“thousand odours rose from the sewage-soaked
floor.” Added, in conclusion, that he had seen
nothing worse in the East End of London.
“Mors”: Went to a funeral in a A
bush township the other day, and to my sur-
prise the 0. of E. minister who read the ser-
vice was an elocutionist. Had always before
heard the words droned-out in dismal flesh-
creeping tones; but this performer marched
gaily up the cemetery walk with head thrown
back and radiant face, chanting the service
like a song of joy. As the paean of victory
from the magnificent words of the Book of Job
rang around, delightful mental pictures arose
of a happy release from the cares of life, and
of a glorious triumph over death itself.
Realised that I had heard the service pro-
perly read for the first time, and now think
that people ought to be buried to the inspirit-
ing strains of a band of music, whose tones
should enforce the real happiness of the occa-
sion ; because, after all, when a man is once
dead it is the most beautiful thing that could have
happened to him.
Some time ago a rumour went round Perth
(W.A.) about a plague-case in Chinatown, and a
search was made for the patient. He couldn’t be
found, and ©very Ling 1 and Lung and Ting and
Tong and Tang in the Chow quarter was quite
sure that the report was groundless. The
authorities reckoned, they were altogether too
unanimous, but nothing could be proved against
them. It now transpires that the Chinamen—
who didn’t want to be quarantined and white-
washed and aggravated—drove their plague-
smitten brother into the bush and left him there.
Then, to save time, the cart out of which he had
been dropped was used to carry back a load of
vegetables and pork for sale. Presumably, the
late Chow’s death was hastened through being
thus thrown out, and probably other people were
killed through the infected pork and vegetables,
but the responsibility rests among some 20 men
who look exactly alike and who seem to exchange
names every five minutes, so little can be done.

“ Melb.” : This photoing is downright
ridiculous. Last Sunday morning, when the
Duke and Duchess went to church, the photo-ers
outside were like a swarm of mosquitoes, or daddy
longlegses, not counting the shoals of kodaks.
Every camera is a little maxim for the destruction
of royalty. Wonder they don’t see it. The
Lama of Thibet, in his seclusion, is the wisest
monarch. Ho visible monarch is half so tall or
majestic as the invisible one.

The most conspicuous new banner in
Melb. Eight Hours’ procession was that of the
Clerks’ Union. They also had a tableau contrast-
ing the ancient “scrivener” with the modern
typewriter—latter suggestively worked by the
cheap small boy who is shunting his father out of
the pen-and-ink market. But nary a one of the
lily-handed quill-drivers followed their flag.
« * » *
Brisbane has been, and still is, using bitter
language about the Dook. The fact that that
depressed, wrigglesome little man takes no real
interest in the plague city is too apparent to be dis-
guised. He went there with a reduced suite. The
fleet didn’t go up and make a display among the
sandbanks opposing the meat works ; and the
original programme was curtailed. The Brisbane
papers said publicly and bitterly that this was the
Last Straw, and certainly Queensland is so bare of
vegetation just now that the statement seems
quite probable.

The parson-managers of Brisbane Tele-
graph, who advocate the benevolent asylum and
the soup kitchen in preference to Old Age Pen-
sions, will no doubt be disgusted to learn that
Melbourne Benevolent Asylum, which, previous
to the starting of the pension system, was ever
unable to find room for the numerous applicants,
now has 130 vacancies, and the old folks are still
leaving faster than they enter. And the cost to
the State is just about the same in both cases

A WOMANS LETTER.
_ . Sydney, May 21, 1901.
My Dear Arini,—
Saturday’s races brought out the few Anybodies
who didn’t go Duking to Melbourne, a large
number of dramatic persons, some pretty frocks
and some frantic frocks. Miss Palotta’s raiment
of pastel-green frieze was well set off by the green
grass over which the skirt trained ; as was her face
by the big, black-feathered hat that overshadowed
it at an angle of 45 degrees. What a noble thing
it is to have a complexion that can stand such
brilliant, uncompromising daylight as Saturday’s.
Miss Palotta’s skin (do you recollect that cour-
teous German who, wishing to compliment a lady
on her fine cuticle, told her she had a beautiful
“ leather ?”)—well, Miss Palotta’s leather and
the leathers of some of the “ Floradora ”
sextette can stand a combination of micro-
scope and Australian sunlight. The fair
Grace, by the way, waggles at the
hips in walking. Her waggle is a modification—
a very modified modification—of the Maoriland
wahme’s waggle. Marie Lloyd has the same de-
fect ; even the cat-cum-butterfly grace of Madame
Darto is marred by it. Perhaps the tight fit of
the skirt round the hips has something to do with
the discord of the grace note.
Some of the Australian born “leathers”
can’t be excelled. Mrs. Trench and her sis-
ter, Miss Gox, for instance. Former lady was
dressed in a beautiful shade of heliotrope,
but the style of the gown came under the
heading of Frantic. Its zouave made the
wearer hunch-backed and pigeon-breasted, and
her wide, flat hat was fastened down at the back
close to the nape of the neck in a fashion that
would suggest covering a bald spot at the base of the
brain. Mrs. Mackenzie Fairfax’s little figure was
quietly dressed, and the wide fur on her short
black sable coat was off some expensive animal
but it made her look very broad. One or
two beautiful white gowns were on the turf, and
seemed quite in season, so warm was the ’day
Friezes were plenty. I liked best a close-fitting
coat and skirt of a bright shade of navy-blue. It
was worn with a fur and floral toque, and the
wearer’s face was round as a rose is round, and
pink withal.
The choux ot ribbon or chiffon on the neck
just in front of the left ear was worn by a few
women. This fashion, it is said by English
authorities, has a strikingly softening effect on the
face, especially the lean-jawed face. If that be
so, is it not better to have two choux, one below
each jaw ? If you can get something of little Red
Riding Hood’s expression into the whole of your
face, why leave half of it reminiscent of the wolf ?
Where do the artists who run the tea pavilion at
Randwick buy their tea ? Or do they buy it at all ’
Is it grown on the sand hills round Randwick?
I have lapped better tea at a Poley Cow Creek
race-meeting—a place that is 90 miles from a rail-
way station, and whose grand-stand is a post-and-
rail fence, and whose Mayor disinherited his son
for “bloomin’ womanishness” because the lad
bought a nail-brush.
A musical evening was held at Pott’s Point, the
other day, but Sydney’s best was travelling Mel-
bournewards, so that, out of the 40 invited, only
one turned up. “What did you do with the
supper ? ” asked a lady friend of the hospitable
hostess. Oh, we just ate it ourselves, 93 answered
the dame, nonchalantly.
The Ballarat lady who presented fish-scale
flowers to the royal children will have many
imitators among that type of woman who believes
that genius is shown by making one article do
duty as something entirely different. Sydney
sassiety is busy at present making its husband
wear a dog-collar on his forehead and stand with
a plush tablecloth round his manly form, while,
inspired by these attributes of royalty, the soon-to-
be-presented wife practises her presentation curt-
sey in front of him. This is the only time on
record when a Sydney Society lady has bowed
before her own husband. Several Sydney dames
are indignant because George dismissed them un-
handshaken—or should it be unshook—from Mel-
bourne, telling them to apply again in their own
harbor-city. George hasn’t brought enough hand-
shakes with him to go twice round the same
people.
Presentation mirror-frame to the travel-worn
Princess is probably to be supplemented by a fan
of ostrich-feathers. There’s an ostrich-farm out
on the South Head Road, and the Bird-in-Chief
offers to supply fluffy part of fan for much love
(to Royalty) and a very little money. Committee
has close on £l7O, so the poor Duchess won’t
need to use a palm-leaf fan, or to make a breeze
on herself with a newspaper, any longer. Jewel-
lers who have the contract for May’s frame
are sad because a wonderful piece of
water-worn gold bought by them from ex-
plorer T. Kerry didn’t reach them two days
sooner. They think the committee would have
chosen it in place of a thing made by hands. It
resembles a half-wreath of delicately and minutely
carved flowers, and' is of perfectly pure gold.
Mr. Kerry is alleged to have picked it up on a
lone South Sea islet.
Concerning Melbourne evening dressing : Syd-
neysiders down to see the Mighty Atom and his
Duchess have come back with the remains of the
blush of shocked modesty yet on their cheeks.
They say Melbourne dames wear their evening
gowns very dec-ollette. One man from here said
that he never before saw so much of the other
man’s wife as he saw in one evening leaning
over the back of the dress-circle at the Princess’s.
Mr. Julian Ashton, back from Melbourne,
where he was sketching and Yorking, has a few
opinions on the carnival. The scheme of illumi-
nations at Prince’s Bridge was beauty itself. .

Madame is wedded these eight years to an

needed tinkering.

When the French Spy came on the Criterion’s
stage he discovered to the audience some delicate
and desirable dresses. Miss Watts Phillips’ first
frock is a white muslin with black lace
insertion over pink silk slip, wide belt of
pink satin, straw hat trimmed with black
velvet, and many-colored roses. Later, she gets
into a grey check coat and green velvet skirt, and
a big hat of turquoise-blue silk and chiffon, with
red roses and tulle strings. The burlesque dress
leaves a misty memory of gold braid, spangled net,
jewels, and a long cloak of white satin. Miss Ida
C resham s are first, a white satin with flounces of
embroidered net-work trimmed with rows of satin
ribbon. Her next is a pink, of mirrored velvet,
patterned with lace embroidery. The third gown
is of pale blue duchesse satin, with green chiffon
spangles and satin.
It is enough to make men sing a Te Deum ’
One of him has scored on the Oppressor Woman.
A decent old tradesman in Adelaide was awarded
a verdict of £25 against an elderly widow for
breach of promise. In summing up, the S M
put as one question to the jury : “ Did defendant
C( ? ntra ° fc deliver herself up body and soul to
plaintiff? And the jury said “Yes” to the
extraordinary query. Yet, after all, is this deli-
very of a soul desirable for either party ? It would
leave the woman with no soul while the man
would have two, and he finds that it takes him all
he knows to lose one. And if the lady was will-
ing to carry out her contract, how do you deliver
a soul ? You can t take it out and hand it over on
a fork.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:
“ The dressing at all the recent Federal shivoos
was mournful and ineffective. One black gown is
like another black gown, and so women who
wanted to shine took it out in jewellery The
Duchess paralysed all by the numerous ‘ sparkle
crowns she displayed, each one bigger and
glitterier than the other. Her Ex. showed some
glittering diamonds and pearls, so did the Queens-
land and West Australian Governoresses Lady
lennyson wearing a meek, unaggressive tiara at
all shows. Both J anet Lady and her daughter-in-
law ran the vice-regalities very close in the matter
of gems. Miss Concord Walker wears some good
diamonds, but, being a spinster, she hasn’t the
right to sport ’em unlimited as a matron has. Of
Sydney visitors, Miss Whitney played the role of
Sw- f ea \ Spir \ fc ; M -T. R. Bavin and
Miss Winchcombe showed fine frocks. The
official ladies mostly run to black brocade and
unremarkable lace. Mrs. W. H. Freehill had a
pretty mauve gown. Melbourne maidens wore
white satin chiefly for evening gowns. Mrs
Claude Macdonald showed up well at the conver-
sazione in a wonderful cloak. Mrs. H M'Quade
also sported some nice toggery on that occasion
dome of the hair fixings were weird. A girl at
'Hoiise had a large transparent Katherine-
wheel of white gauze the size of a dinner-plate
stuck over her forehead. Many women tie white
tulle in their hair, and look as if they had their
heads bandaged. Perhaps the best-frocked woman
throughout the whole week was Mrs. Brookman,
?7 fe of the Boulder man, and Mayoress of Perth,
one had a dress of point lace that raised the envy
of every other woman present.
" v^ ie mi B ht y have been tumbling off their little
perches all the time in a fashion most refreshing
to the common, vulgah people, who acquired that
exhausted feeling through seeing the same little
tin-gods running every Melbourne show for years,
i ederation has brought about a great shaking-up
of the whole bag-of-tricks. The little cliques of
local Somebodies that used to be the ruling tone-
givers in each State capital have been welded to-
gether—petty coteries flattened out of recognition.
Snubs were freely dealt out by the Royal High-
nesses to many a pushful person. Melbourne’s
very Best People suffered some agonising
moments during Commonwealth week. After
practising bob - curtseys before the looking-
glass for weeks, even having their dress-maker
insert an elastic strap in front of the knee among
the folds of the dress to act as an aid to a graceful
duck, their genuflections were cruelly cut short by
Duchess Cornwall, who seemed wearied by over
much ceremony. At an afternoon reception a
wealthy widow and her daughter sidled up to the
much-stared-at group on the lawn and gave way
at the knees every time they caught the royal
optic. The Duch. was talking to somebody else,
and after three bobs and a grovel all the widow
received was a royal hand-grip lasting exactly one
half-second and a cool stare over the top of her hat.
Another lydy on the same day pushed up to be
introduced to a lady of the royal ‘sweet,’ but all
she got was a faint ‘ howde doo,’ an upward tilt
of an aristocratic Roman nose, and the cold
shoulder.
“ The appearance of the Ducal pair and the
Parly couple at the State Reception, when they
stood on the dais in the Queen’s Hall, struck every-
one. The Duke positively trembled with nervous-
ness, and his small, frail figure seemed to wilt in
the scorching light of many eyes. Lord Hopetoun
was as nervous. His face twitched convulsively
and his hands kneaded the air. On the other
hand the Duchess stood calmly bowing and
smiling to all around, and the Countess of Hope-
toun appeared cold and unmoved. Old women are
fond of saying there is a good, motherly look in
the face of the Duchess. This was never more
apparent than at the Public School display on
Saturday, when she beat time with a friendly and
well-meant gamp to the band music, nodded ap-
proval at the various turns the well-drilled kiddies
did, and earnestly battered her hands together
after every number. Apparently, she really en-
J?yed herself then ; anyhow, she and her frail
little Duke remained an Lour and a half instead of
the 20 minutes arranged.
Ihe Duchess was very sea-sick on her way
out, especially in the Bay of Biscay. The Times
said so. But the statement gave offence, in con-
sequence of which the Times, in a subsequent
article, stated that it didn’t really mean the lady
was ill; all it meant was that, from the roughness
of the sea, it was inferred that in all probability
the Duchess would be ill. You can’t be too
careful how you speak about royalty.
“Fagged-out celebrators feel that it’s not all
beer and skittles dining with Royalty. For when
Royalty stands nobody may sit. On one of the
fireworks nights, after the State dinner at Govt.
House, Duchess May felt inclined to stand at a
window to see the fun, and until all the rockets
and Roman candles had burned out the whole
party had wearily to hang about first on one foot
then on the other. They were all dead-tired, too,
after the functions of the day. At last the eldest
waiting-lady approached her mistress and hinted
that a chair would be a boon, and then the whole
fatigued company could flop likewise. H.R.H.
graciously gave the lady permission to go into
another room. And the party went on standing
on its last legs.
“ There is one memorable occasion of the Ducal
douche of glories on which the laughing Premier
failed to live up to his reputation. When leaving
for the landing procession at St. Hilda, someone
ottered the Peacock m his swagger Windsor plum-
a£.e f to °k ]t ’ an d stuck it absent-
mindedly in his tinselled coat. On being presented
to the Royal Highness—a most critical moment—
the cigar fell at the Royal feet. ‘You have
dropped something,’ remarked the Duchess
pointing to the fatal cigar. ‘ Thank you, your
Royal Highness,’ murmured the blushing Pre-
mier, losing his precious minute of presentation
over the worthless weed. And though everybody
else laughed, Peacock was as serious as the grave.
