“ A Toorak matron paid a visit to the Russian
man-of-war on its arrival in our waters, and the
following Sunday had a return call from ten
officers, who completely upset her equilibrium bv
kneeling and kissing her hand, one after another
on arriving. When she could conveniently escape
she made a dash for the telephone and unselfishly
rang up every nice girl in the neighborhood, telling
her to come and be kissed also. For all-round
generosity I think that would be hard to beat. In
truth the hand-kissing of the foreign warriors has
sent the nice girls of Melbourne off their heads
Also button-collecting has grown to be an alarming
craze. One German officer cut the gold crown off
his cuff at the Wednesday-night reception, and
presented it ‘on bended knee.’ The favored
beauty wore it in her dress—as a brooch —on
Saturday night. What better scalps could any-
one desire !
“The crowd that went uninvited on tha
Brooklyn last Thursday, when the Yankee *At
Home' was on, scared other foreign hosts, and
the German Consul sent telephonic message to
those invited to the ‘ Hansa’ At Home that cards
of invitation were not to admit wholesale contin-
gents.
“Marshall-Hall, sac-coated and soft-halted,
was a conspicuous figure at the ‘ Hansa ’ social
event. So was Mrs. Eccles, in a black frock, with
a transparent vest letting in the light of day on a
very white neck. Mrs. George Chirnside was one
of the daintiest of figures in all cream. Mrs.
C. Yallange and Miss Ruby Madden—who pre-
sented a study in still life on the deck—were both
garbed in black. The suave, substantial Germans
looked with wonder on the ludicrously lean figure
of Captain Vallange in all his scarlet trappings.
“Prime-Minister Barton, like the Madden
family, was conveyed to the ‘ Hansa ’ in a special
launch—a gaily bedecked hooded one. Mr. Barton
carried the Duke’s present—the diamond snuff-
box—about with him, and facetiously offered
brother politicians a pinch. Mrs. Barton added
bunches of violets to a smart black outfit. Mrs.
Isaacs was a limp and languid figure in an
apathetic couldn’t-help-it black frock, and an out-
of-mourning blue toque.
“The foreign vessel At Homes, with dancing
from 3 to 5 o’clock, kept the Melbourne girls tired
all last week. They didn’t want to refuse the
nautical darlings a dance, and yet the conven-
tions cried out against whirling about the decks in
tailor-made skirts. Lena Middell-Stanley, one
of the prettiest members of the Madden clan,
accepted a whirl every time, and was rushed
by Dutch, German, Russian, and Yankee officers
at all the At Homes. Byron Moore’s suite of
girls were also without scruples as to the look of
things and had ‘ a real good time ’—as Uncle
Sam s boys put it.
‘‘The most extraordinary-looking distinguished
visitor in the celebration pen was Admiral .Tessen’s
daughter—the picture of a back-country Phyllis,
with bright pink cheeks, wide eyes, in which was
a placid inquiring, ruminating expression, and
brushed-back convent head-dress.
“We needn’t talk about poor Hopeys little
tempers when a certain foreign Admiral is in
port. You never saw such a pepper-pot and so
hungry for his rights. On Public Schools’ Prize
day the fire-eater was placed with his ladies on
the guest dais of honor,' but only in the second
row. So he tackled the officials and with terrible
shoulder-shrugs pointed to the dome, in illnstra-
tKin of the ladies’ hats blocking his front view.
He and his charges were then stationed in front]
but on the ground floor, as the first row of the
dais was full. When the boys crowded there to
hear the Royal Speech, the foreign group became
engulfed. Janet Lady Clarke laughingly helped
the ladies up on the dais and wanted to do as
much for their Protector, but Himself was in no
mood for conciliation. He squared his shoulders
and flung himself back against the boys with a
face like a turkey-cock. The youngsters who
were lustily bawling ‘For he’s a jolly good
fellow! forgot to look who was pushing, but
well for them they were in their own backyard.
Otherwise they might suddenly have waked to
the fact that they were being knouted as ‘ revolu-
tionary students ’ or sich.
What hollow farces are enacted under the
name of official etiquette. When the Dutch
cruiser Noord Brabant arrived in Port Phillip,
the old Cerberus desired to salute her, but found
she had not on board a Dutch flag to hoist in
accordance with the recognised compliments of
the navy. The commander accordingly des-
patched a boat to the Noord Brabant, asking for
a Joan of one of its jacks, and when it returned to
the Cerberus the Dutchman was gravely saluted
with his own borrowed banner.
“ Lady Mary Lygon didn’t look her best when
here with Royalty. The refined effect of those
classic features of hers was marred by a coat of
red-brown sunburn—healthy, but not becoming.
“ Throughout the celebrations champagne flowed
like water, but only shamelessly-early greedy
birds ever arrived in time to pick up anything to
speak of in the shape of eatables or soft drinks.
This was rough on tired women who, after hours
of standing, yearned for a cup of hot coffee and a
morsel of cake. The average quiet-going she
lacks the pluck to toss off unaccustomed fizz minus
such precautionary ballast as a biscuit or a
sandwich. Some heroines took the risk. One
nice-looking old lady, for instance, rapped scribe
on the knuckles with a picked-bare turkey-bone
and invited her to share a cab home on the co-
operative system. When scribe declined on the
plea of prior arrangements, the ancient melted
into tears and thanked God she was not as Mel-
bourne women are. ‘ln Tasmania,’ she sobbed
Melbourne ladies are famed for loud vulgarity
no doubt their inheritance from old Gold Rush
days! ’
“Here and there, women in hats and sack-
ulsters ate jelly with their fingers, and a State
Minister is said to have spied a rural constituent
trekking home with bottle-necks sticking out of
his tail-pockets. But never mind ; we don’t kill
a pig every day.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Tom Brooker, S.A.’s new Education
Minister, runs a wood-yard in an Adelaide suburb.
Cardinal Cullen, uncle of Cardinal Moran,
was born in prison. His mother was the wife of
one of the rebels of the time.
Fate is indeed capricious. One generation
gets a trinket to swing at the end of the chain,
though the preceding generation had to do its own
swinging.
“Melb.’ : In the Carlton police-court
recently, a lawyer called another an “offensive
scoundrel.” A few minutes afterwards he called
him “ My friend.”
Eph. Walsh, one of the little party that
went out in search of the men who got astray
from Stockdale’s Kimberley to Fort Darwin ex-
pedition in ’B4, was killed lately while serving
with Kitchener’s scouts in S’Africa.
Fred. J. Broomfield, the well-known
Sydney journalist, was the writer who supplied
the historical sketch of the States of Australasia,
which fills 222 pages (pages 11 to 232) of the latest
issue of the “Seven Colonies.” It is probably the
best condensed history of Australasia at present
in print.

Cabled that one Hugh Watt has been
mulcted in damages as a co-respondent in a high-
toned English divorce-case. Watt is the gentle-
man who interviewed Kingston, when the latter
was S.A. Premier, re some Northern Territory
•oncessions, and made a wild and precipitate
flight from the S.A. Premier’s office. He may not
have been a long-distance champion, but his time
over 100yds. on that occasion was pretty good,
considering that both he and his pursuer were out
of training.
A bitterly disappointed man is Mason, the
long, gaunt official with the vague whiskers, who
has twice been elected Vic. Speaker. A K.C.M.G.-
ship from the Juke was reckoned a certainty for the
lank Gippslander, but, like most other people, he
failed to catch the Royal eye, and now makes no
effort to hide his chagrin.
The Duke to Daniel O’Connor :—“ I re-
member you, Mr. O’Connor,” and public men
within hearing turned green with envy. The
Duke remembers Daniel, and the Duke, on his
way to Brisbane, fled past Daniel’s city without
stopping even for a drink. He evidently remem-
bered Daniel right enough.
Mrs. Nation, the Kansas lady who goes
about smashing liquor bars with her little tommy-
axe, lost her first husband by d.t. She was
arrested five times one Sunday, bailed out five
times, and employed the intervals in more
destruction at saloons. Kansas is a nominally
prohibition State, and Mrs. Nation is its prac-
tical prohibitor.
Victorian Premier Peacock (in Windsor
uniform) turned on his laugh during the dreary
wait at the Dook’s State Concert. When the
crowd applauded wildly he stopped short and
grew scarlet. Mrs. Peacock was at his side, and
the story goes that Alexander of the Shriek only
won her on his solemn promise to leave off that
deafening smile of his.
When the highly favored, and subse-
quently frustrated Fegan was promised the
Under-Secretaryship to the Lyne Department,
Newcastle (N.S.W.) admirers subscribed to a
testimonial. It is now announced that “in view
of subsequent events it was decided that the
amount collected be refunded to the subscribers.”
When Fegan had a good billet and a big salary
his friends were anxious to give him a cash
donation. Now that he has no billet they won’t
give him anything.
“Boko”: It is usual for the Moderator
of the Presbyterian Assembly to wear “ breeches”
during his term of office ; but, of course, this prac-
tise is optional. At one of the meetings of present
Assembly a member got up and objected to ex-
Moderator Dill-Mackv still continuing to assume
the pants of office, and Dill Macky rose excitedly
to his feet; “ I wore breeches before I was Mode-
rator,” he said, “and I’m not going to take them
off now for anybody.”
Old W. A. B. Gellibrand, whom the slowly
awakening electors of Tasmania threw out at the
late Derwent election, has worn out the seats of
the Legislative Council chamber at Hobart for 30
weary consecutive years. He slept on behalf of
the Derwent constituency all that time, and said
“No ” in his dreams to every proposal for upset-
ting the Mouldy Order of Things. The first time
old man Cellibrand has been really awake since
’7l was when his constituents the other day put a
new man (Ellis Dean) in his place by 53 majority.
Writes an S.A. lawyer :
It is certain that if little Way secures promotion, Bou-
caut will be the next Chief Justice of S.A. He is nearly
70 years of age, but still strong-brained and vigorous.
Boucaut spent nearly 20 years in politics, and was twice
Premier. His name will always be associated with the
proposal to defray interest on loans by levying land values
taxation. Parliament decided to spend the money, but
rejected the taxation proposals. This was in 1875, and
Parliament was sorry for it afterwards. Boucaut is a
yachting enthusiast, but his great hobby in recent years
has been horse-breeding. As a Judge he is distinguished
for his strict fairness towards prisoners, especially when
undefended.
' One woman describes another :
The Duchess of York—-if not a duchess -would be fairly
described as a strapping wench, long-limbed, solidly-
built, and somewhat goodly to look upon, though by no
means beautiful. She has a long neck and fine shoulders
for an evening-dress setting ; a shapely head thatched
with light brown hair, and pretty arms and hands. Like-
wise, don’t forget to look at her teeth when she laughs,
as she does freely. (The duke can’t laugh as his wife can,
he just titters and pulls his moustache nervously.) Her
face has a sulky aspect in reposp, but her laugh is win-
some. Suppose she were not a royal Duchess what would
she be ? Well, she would be a credit to the front row of a
comic opera chorus.
Dr. Lemon, who died recently in his 67th
year, was associated with the M.L. Telegraph
Department for over 30 years, retiring from the
superintendency in ’94. One of his first enter-
prises, in ’67, was the adjustment of the mileage
tariff for telegrams. Something of the kind was
badly needed ; at that time it cost 6s. Bd. to send
10 words from Charleston to Napier, and lOd. for
10 words from Auckland to Onehunga. There
were all manner of intermediate rates, and the
why of these amazing discrepancies no one knew.
It was on Dr. Lemon’s advice that Premier Hall
made the ’phone a State monopoly in Maoriland.
Melbourne is never quite free from
anxiety about Jenkins. The latest anxiety is that
Jenkins, who has a good ornamental billet in the
State Parliament, will I}B due for a pension after
another 12 months’ service—wants the pension,
naturally—doesn’t want to put in the 12 months’
service, but to get a Federal billet —and can’t find
out for certain whether his State pension will fol-
low him to his proposed Federal billet. The
necessity for providing for Jenkins being obvi-
ous, there have been proposals to give Jenkins 12
months’ leave of absence, so that he can go into
the Federal service and still remain in the State
service, and be in two places at once without being
a bird.

General, Treasurer, Works Minister, Chief

Secretary and Minister of Education. With all

“G.” : Those who watched the Jook care-
fully throughout the Melbourne celebrations
agree that that exalted personage’s attitude in
private life is that of Princess May’s husband.
The assertion that the Jook is an extremely robust
individual suffering from temporary ill-health is
belied by his appearance. It is a sorrowful thing
to inspect the nervous, hesitating, weak-looking
little fellow, and remember all the wild flapdoodle
which was written about his hilarious, devil-may-
care rowdyism in the days when he was a mid-
shipman or sub-lieutenant. The only practical
joke he seems likely to rise to now is to call
“ 800 ! ” in a weak voice through a doorway, and
then trot gradually round the corner.
Robert Homburg, who succeeds Sudden
Solomon as leader of S.A. Tory party, is a dull
ballast politician of German descent, who has
been a great dead-weight in Parliament for 15
years without doing anything wonderful. He has
all the mental lightness of a bison. Hoinburg
represents a country constituency in S. A. Assem-
bly, and two-thirds of his electors are Germans.
Many of them cannot speak English, but they
always record Tory votes. He was Attorney-
General in 1892 in Downer’s Ministry. Homburg
is a man with violent prejudices—a bounceable,
breakable kind of man—and his capacity for acci-
dentally putting his hind foot on things while
trying to turn round will probably make the Ass
party regret its choice.
N.S.W. Opposition-leader Lee is a painful
apparition on the platform. At Sydney Pro-
testant Hall last week, the State became ac-
quainted, for the first time, with its future hope
and joy, and the vision was heartrending. Lee
read most of his speech, and gave the impression
that he had never heard of it before. He held the
huge block of foolscap up to the light with his left
hand, held his eye-glass before him with his right,
and lost his place regularly. About every tenth
word he would come upon something absolutely
unintelligible. Then he would ''give up the
manuscript as a bad job and wait a few seconds
till be could think of a word that would fill the
gap—by which time he had lost the thread of the
argument, and would start the whole paragraph
over again. By the time he got back to the gap
he would get mixed between the word that was
there and the one he intended to interpolate.
When he got through the manuscript, about 9.45,
he put it down with an expression of relief and
spoke with some slight approach to fluency ; but
he couldn’t round off his periods, and they were
left ragged and untidy. His party was so glad
to hear the last of him, apparently, that it started
cheering wildly before lie showed any signs of
getting to a finish. Altogether, Lee was a figure
to move a statue of Moloch to tears of pity.
The prisoner was charged with robbing a
lodge. Defending co .n il was urging that it
hadn’t been proved that there was any such lodge.
A witness had said that he knew the head-office
and had seen the name on it. “ There you are,”
Mr. Blank, snapped ’Sonah, “we must believe
the evidence of our eyes. I look out of the Court-
house door yonder, and see the sign, the Bkiga-
LONG Bunyip, and so I know that a paper called
the Beigalong Bunyip is published there.” But
the court simply flew on to the next point when
counsel explained that the Brigalong Bunyip had
expired two or three years before ! However,
’Sonah bore no malice—he directed an acquittal.
“ Barnacle ”on a one-time royal'y :
The Yorkess is stated to be of a somewhat stand-off
disposition ; this was a failing of her dad’s. I was on a
ship, where Teck occasionally dined, and one day, when
ashore with the launch, His Itoyal nibs, who was leaning
on a pile of railway-sleepers near the wharf chatting
with a high official, notified his intention of going off
with me. 1 told him we were going in a quarter of an
hour, and intimated when the time had passed. He took
no notice, and then, being young and inflated with ideas
of my own importance, I got angry, and stated my orders
were to leave at a given time, and if he didn’t trot along,
the boat would have to go without him. Then he came
with a look of ccld glassy astonishment in his eye that
was worth seeing. I wonder if lam the only person in
Australia who has told a member of the British Royal
family to hurry up or he’d dashed well get left?
“ H.” : Died in England, James barker,
Salvation Army colonel. His brother is the
harpist in Musgrove’s operatic orchestra. James,
by his own account, was a reckless young fellow
when General Booth laid his bony hands on him
and the shadow of Booth’s vast nose fell on his
path. Barker was picked to open fipte in Mel-
bourne. He married Captain Alice Collis, and
they started next day for these Antipodes. Their
first pitch was Collingwood Flat. In view of the
disagreeables that arose, it must be remembered
that, while Barker evinced pretty large ability,
he had General Booth’s prestige behind him.
Anyhow, in 1884, when he had been here
a year, the General decided that lie was
going too fast. The sanguine fashion of
Barker threatened to pile up financial em-
barrassments, and Booth loathed to see cash
dissipated. So Ballington Booth and Major
Howard were sent out to look after him. Balling-
ton ordered Barker to England. Barker refused
to go, entrenching himself behind a threat not to
give up the properties. A lot of his friends
wanted him to start on his own hook, but he was
loyal to the cause, and had a pathetic faith in his
pawnbroking prophet. Ultimately he was per-
suaded to go to London and see the General. An
arrangement, under which Barker’s Rescue work
was to be kept separate from the big-drum part of
the business, was come to, but did not work well.
General Booth sent a peremptory order for Barker
to take a final farewell of Australia, and return to
England. He went, broken-hearted. Thence-
forward he was in the shade. His Melbourne
popularity had been too great, and he was never
forgiven.
TO PREMIER SEE.
(For his Album.)
The bitter irony of circumstance—
Not you—has lost this everlasting chance.
The chance—good Lord, we shudder as we write —
To be a chrismed and sacrosancted knight.
You did your best; you put your hopes in pawn,
You spoke to Royal Highness on the Lawn.
You spoke so well, to put them at their ease,
These modest, young, retiring Royalties.
You spoke so well the Duke said, smiling grim,
“ I have no title big enough for him.”
The Other Bounders, sons of sin and wrath,
Met him mid-sea —and caught him in his bath.
They took from him his gewgaws—and, God knows,
The wonder is, they did not take his clothes.
There was, in times gone by, a Mister Pitt,
Not undevoid of wisdom or of wit ;
He held all Europe in his small, white hand;
For years and years he plotted, schemed and planned.
His little finger raised made King and Khan
Tremble to know there was on earth —a Man.
Kings, Highnesses, Transparencies and such
Small purple cattle wilted at his touch.
This common Mister Pitt, in days bygone,
Strangled the First and Great Napoleon.
Therefore be comforted, O plain John See —
Misters, not knights, have immortality.
C.R.

To Contributors. --Always give, in ethnological and
natural-history items etc., exact date and exact
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
“ Did I ever tell yer ’ow I was taken-down

hadn’t. “Well, I don’t mind tellin’ you, as [

an’ all that sorter thing—makes upter me, an'
offers ter show me round. We got into a pub.

as knew my friend. Well, I asted ’im ter ’ave a
drink, but ’e wouldn’t ’ear of it. ’E wanted ter
shout, as ’e was so glad ter meet me. Then we
sat yarnin’ for awhile, an’ th’ last-comer pulls out
a pocket-book, an’ begins huntin’ about in it for a
jooty-stamp. There was none, an’ ’e asted th’
landlord if ’e could oblige. ’E couldn’t. Then ’e
said ’e’d slip down ter th* P.O. an’ get one. I’m
sugared if ’e didn’t leave th’ pocket-book on th’
table, wide open. My friend took it up, an’
started lookin’ through it. ‘What’s in it?’ I
asted. Well, ’e went through th’ thing, an’ found
fifty notes (oners), a couple of fivers, four sovs.,
an’ 6s. 3d. in silver. ’E said ’e was naterally a
’onest man, but th’ temptation was too much for
even ’im. ‘ Tell, yer what! ’ said ’e, ‘ you give
me a tenner, an’ take th’ lot. ’ E’s got plenty,
an’ll never miss it; besides, ’e’ll never mind you,
as you leave by th’ boat ter-night.’ Th’ temptation
was too great for me. I ’ad £lO on me, an’ I
’anded it over, took th’ book, an’ sneaked. I
thought it was great biz. Wasn’t I savage when
I looked through it? Th’ 6s. 3d. was right

“ Spare-me-days ” writes:—
Bulletin Bed Page (11/5/’Oli, in reviewing Brim ton
Stephens’s “Convict Once,” wants to know what Mag-
dalen Power could do to be transported at 16. This
scribbler can tell him. Once saw an old convict register
in which was recorded the history of some hundreds of
female convicts. Amongst them was a girl of 16,
described as “ nursery governess,” who was sentenced to
seven years’ transportation for wearing at an evening
party an article of dress belonging to her mistress.
Another case was that of a girl of 15, transported for
seven years for stealing apples from an orchard. And
there were plenty more like instances. The English girl
of the early part of this century could do plenty of things
whereby to get herself transported at 16. There wasn’t
the least difficulty on that score.

Casey wanted a windmill to irrigate his
little wheat-field, and poolan offered to sell him
one of the two on his (Doolan’s) land. “And
why do you want to sell one of yours ? ” inquired
Casey— “are they not a success?” “To be sure
they are,” said Doolan; “but you see there is
only wind enough on this small selection of mine
to drive one windmill.” Explanation accepted,
and sale effected.
“ Alpar ” : That colored picture of “ An
Australian Dairymaid ” did make me laugh. Who
ever saw a girl go into a dirty stockyard with
uamty shoes and stockings and'a nice little cap
decked with ribbons ? Fancy said cap and rib-
bons up against a dirty cow ! The cows are not
groomed in this beastly country. Occasionally,
too, the cow switches her long, filthy tail —which
is mostly secured in a craqk in a convenient rail
and catches the maid under the ear, or around the
swan-like neck. And, fancy the little shoes
buried m the muck when the girl is bailing-up,
and the old cow is being chased around the yard
by a couple of dogs ! Far as I’ve seen—and I’ve
seen a few —dairymaids are dirtier than dairymen,
and that’s saying a lot. Let us have a picture of
a brawny, red-faced “piece,” with blucher boots
minus the laces, nature’s stockings, a dirty
‘ pinny, ’ one of the boy’s old coats, and the old
man’s hat with the crown gone ! Let us have
icality, at any price.

“J. W. 0.” concerning rabbits on the
Queensland border :
_I saw rabbits on Bulloo Downs in ’B5 ; and when, in
’B7, I was erecting 40 miles of the border rabbit-proof
fence and had (owing to the difficulties I had to contend
with) to delay completion until Dec., ’O9, the rabbits were
going through the gaps in the fence in thousands. The
rabbit-wave, in fact, had reached the Q. border early in
’BB. “Ne’er-do-well,” in reply to “Clio. F.” (B. l/9/’OO),
ho.wever, is wrong about the young rabbits not being able
to squeeze through a smaller mesh than lfin. ; as, often,
when I have gone into the trap-yard (made of lfin. mesh)
the young rabbits have skedaddled through the mesh like
water through a sieve. They do not seem to try to get
out until they are frightened. In ’BB there were thou-
sands of rabbits near Hungerford after the fence was
completed ; but the fact was suppressed by the inspectors.
In fact, one station I could name had men poisoning rab-
bits in ’B7.
“ P.D.” : lie beaching'ships at
Port Darwin (B. 10/2/’01). In ’92,
the E. and A. Co.’s s.s. Menmuir
lost a propeller-blade, and was laid-
up on a beach at high tide near the
local hospital. A new blade was
fixed, and the vessel floated off next
day without shifting a ton of cargo.
Also, during the construction of the
Darwin-to-Pine Creek railway, s.s.
Euphrates, with 2000 tons of steel
rails, was beached at spring - tide,
rails unloaded, and steamer taken
off. She had, through meandering
on to a rock when entering the har-
bor, been in a sinking condition when
put ashore. The local tide range is
27ft.
“Deebly”: A characteristically
Australian institution is that of the
itinerant youthful prostitute. Along
many of the chief roads going West
these girls may be met tramping
in twos and threes —many of them
little more than 16 years old. They
are always accompanied by as many
“blokes” of about their own age,
as a rule, who are distinguishable
from ordinary “travellers” by the
diminutive swags they carry. The
girls carry nothing but a flippant grin
—“ without which none are genuine.”
They sing the latest “hall” songs
as they go, and make impudent
remarks about those they meet. Oc-
casionally they “spell” under a tree,
and have a swig of tea, or of beer, if
there is any left. Arrived at a
town, a calico “ fly ” is pitched on the
town-reserve, a fire made—and there
you are, all ready for business. And
there they stay until some night, as
the result of too much beer, wild
screams pierce the dead of night,
followed by language that brings
blushes to the cheeks of the honest
bullock-driver, camped in his virtu-
ous bunk hard-by. Then the local
bobby (reluctantly in most cases) is
compelled to give them “ the office
to git.” I have never, out of Aus-
tralia, met with this phase of the
“ oldest profession.”
Shearers’ cooks on the Darling
are mostly fair pugs, and after their
first spread often invite any dis-
satisfied partakers to “ come out-
side.” At Wayback one year, the
cook, a sparse, middle-aged man,
issued the usual challenge, and, in
response, six men filed out. The
tucker was all right, but to refuse
fight would have been unprincipled. The cook
bashed the lot, and the food was voted “ A 1 ” ever
afterwards.
“ Uloola ” : Along N.S.W. coast lands
after a hot day, if the white ants fly it is a sure
sign that a southerly wind is coming. The insects
swarm in thousands from old posts, stumps, &0.,
and after flying a little time their wings drop off.
* * * *
“8.5.”: I question if forest-destruction
be as disastrous as predicted. I know a district
which, when heavily-timbered, was practically
waterless in summer, but which, since being
cleared, has developed what look like permanent
springs. I also know of a creek which carries
more water now than when its vicinity was tim-
bered. Is this the effect of clearing, and a com-
pensation therefor ?
• * * *
According to N.S,W. Botanist Turner,
who has been urging on the N.S.W. Western
Lands Commission the importance of a better
conservation of indigenous grasses and scrub, one
Sydney firm lately supplied an Argentine order
for 50001 b. of saltbush seed. He cited this a 3
evidence of the high value placed elsewhere on
the native shrubs of the droughty inlands.

“ Via ” : Saw two black “ dead marines ”
hanging from either end of a wayside railway
station verandah. Thought it was a hint; but
inquiry showed that the suspended bottle is a
certain means of keeping the swallow and sparrow
from resting on and defiling the eaves. Sort of
Australian bete noir.

I was looking for the township of Cotne-
by-Chance, and I consulted a gentleman of the
road-mending persuasion. This is how the dia-
logue went
“ Can you direct me to Oome-by-Chance ? ”
"Oican. Do yez see that old thrack that used to be
there? Well, follow that, kapin’ the sun in yer roight
shoulder till ye come to a lignum shwamp. Pass that
lignum shwamp, an’ ye’ll come to some brumbies’ thraeks.
Don’t follow the thraeks, but kape the way ye was goin’
till ye come to another lignum shwamp. Go through
that lignum shwamp, an’ ye’ll come acrost the brumbies’
thraeks agin gallopin’. Kape to the roight of the thraeks
an’ ye’ll make another lignum shwamp. Bear to the left
of that lignum shwamp an’ go as far as ye can without
hittin’ a thrack, an’ ye’ll see some tall trees with some
jackasses (lyin’. Kape the bir-rds on the left an’ the sun
anyhow ye loike.”
“ And where will I go then ? ”

“ Trotter ” : On Mourilyan plantation
(Q.) recently, a row took place between the Japs
and Kanakas (something like 400 niggers em-
ployed there), and on the arrival of the police
from Geraldton the Japs simply defied them. No
wonder—with about 300 whites in the district to
about 4000 Chows, Japs, Malays and Kanakas.
The press, so far, has said nothing about this little
trouble.

land of Potted Meat, whilst the slayer
has been re-named “ The Fenian.”

“ Werrimee ” : Houses on wheels
are becoming common about Broken
Hill. Know a teamster whose family
travels up and down the roads from
here in a neatly-built two-roomed house
with glass windows; and t’other day I
saw two 4-roomed houses passing down
the street to the auction mart. Many
of the houses in the Silver City are
built on leases, and, when sold, are re-
moved bodily on low 4-wheeled trucks.
Nothing unusual to see three or four
cottages standing to-day on what was
vacant ground yesterday, or to see vacant
land at night where neat little homes
stood in the morning. It’s just as well
here, where houses have such a habit of
disappearing, to chain your mansion up
before going out.

cookie if be might submit his (the cookie’s) name,
with a recommendation, &c. Cookie asked the
member if he were a J.P. “ No,” said the mem-
ber. “ Then,” replied the cookie, “you can leave
me out. It’s a bad enough thing to get from any
man; but, coming from you. I take it as au in-
sult ! ”

According to a St. Arnaud (Vic.) paper
four cows were recently poisoned near Broadford
through eating poisoned rabbits—four rabbits
being found in the stomach of one defunct cow.
“It appears that the cattle allow the rabbits,
which are poisoned with phosphorised pollard, to
become very much decomposed, and then devour
them. Some think it is the phosphorus which
attracts the cattle, especially in the dry weather.
They instance the eagerness with which milking
cows will eat bone dust or phosphatic manures.”
“Iota”: Let the champion mean
woman have a show. A traveller called
at the house of a lady noted for her “near-
ness,” and asked not for food, which
would have been promptly refused with
—-“Be off, or I’ll set the dogs on you
but merely the time of day, as he wished
to know how long it would take him to
reach the next town. “I don’t
he was told. “My clock has stopped.
And right then the clock struck twelve !

It is vouched for on various assorted
oaths by the whalers of those parts that
the seal—before his winter migration
southward from Behring—takes in a cargo
of “ stones.” There is no evidence as to
how he gets rid of the consignment on
arrival. Is it done to increase the weight
of said seal, obviate the necessity of mus-
cular effort to remain under water, and
thereby conserve all his strength for pro-
pulsion ?

* * * - *
lie estimate of cost of bushman’s keep.
The writer has had survey-camp in various parts
of the State, and his practice was to make each
man pay his own share of the bills ; much more
satisfactory than charging them a stated sum per
week. Only once it ran up to 12s. (id. per week,
and the tucker was poor. After the cook had
left hurriedly without waiting for his cheque, it
transpired that he had endeavored to ingratiate
himself with the handsome daughters of a selector,
who lived handy, by boarding their father’s pigs
at our expense, and quite regardless of cost. His
sudden exit was due to the unseemly ardor of bis
conduct with one of the insufficiently mashed
damsels, and to the fact that the old woman re-
sented his behaviour with a small tree Average
cost was 7s. (id. to Bs. per week, including tea at
2s. per pound, white sugar, jam, condensed milk ;
puddings practically every day, &c. The average
employer who gives so much a week and board has
a habit of estimating board as being worth 15s. per
week. It isn't.

The only barber in a Far Northern Q.
town was Ah Fong, who beguiled with opium the
intervals of business. One day a big hairy-faced
Irishman who had been tackling Q. rum at close
range, entered, and prowling through the back-
premises, unearthed the barber snoozing off the
effects of his drug. “Come out o’ that, ye lazy
haythen, an’ cut me hair,” shouted Pat, violently
MADAM VAN DER VERE GREEN.
shaking Ah Fong. “ All li, all li—me come bime-
bye,” drowsily murmured the Chow; and the
hairy one, returning to the shop, floundered into
the operating-chair and promptly fell asleep. He
was still slumbering when Ah Fong had finished
with him and had resumed his interrupted
dreams. Presently Pat awoke and gazed incredu-
lously at the dreadful apparition confronting him
in the mirror. “Howly Moses! phwat’s this,”
he shrieked, as he saw reflected a hairless face,
the deadly white shorn parts contrasting hideously
with the rest of its mud-colored surface, while a
fearful nine-inch gash, surmounted by an upper
lip straight and hard as a paving-stone, seemed to
merge into a pair of enormous ears standing out
from a scalp clean shaven, save for a tuft left at
the back of the crown. All Fong bad taken his
victim for a fellow-countryman. [And this, ye
gods ! is how The Bulletin gets its own old-time
lie sent back to it. Edward Dyson told the story
of Pat and Fong in this paper many years ago.J

WATSON’S WHISKY.
many that grate on one’s ears at workshops,
mines, schools, street-comers, etc. Thus :
“ Itsynay oysbay erethay omecay ethay eacher-
tay ; utpay emthay incesquay intna ourya ockets-
pay.” Translation: —“Nitsy, boys, here comes
the teacher ; put them quinces in your pockets.”
One gutter-snipe will perhaps say to his mate :
“ Givesy mevesy thavesy bumpvesy-ervesy
whevesy youvesy arevesy dovesy wivesy ivesy.”
And what he means is only—“ Give me that bum-
per (cigar or cigarette stump) when you are done
with it.” Members of cliques and pushes, such as
the “ Kilkennies,” the “Rag Tag,” the “ Blood
and Tiiunder,” make night hideous with their
bawliugs. “Gerriger worriger. Gerriger ariger
biriger origer liriger lariger.” All they are really
saying is : “ Get work. Get a bit of light
labor.” Then there is a lingo which has a
special alphabet, and every word is spelt.
The alphabet runs—A. Bub, Cung, Dud, E,
Fuf, Gug, Huh, I, Juh, Kuk, Lul, Mum, Nun,
O, Pup, Quh, Rus, Sus, Tut, U, Vuv, Wuh, X
(pronounced ux), Yuh, Zuz. F’rinstance that
popular idiotic screech, “What Ho, She Bumps!”
would start “Wuh Huh A Tut,” &c._ Another
gibberish is formed by the transposition of the
initial letters of the principal words, or of a per-
son’s name, in a sentence. Thus, a beefsteak
becomes a‘ ‘ steef bake, ” a glass of beer—a ‘ ‘ blass
of geer,” and Bob Jones becomes “Job Bones.”
All of which are understandable when the prin-
ciple is known, but when rapidly gabbled off they
are an unintelligible medley. “P.K.’s” lingo
consists of inserting “ arp,” “ arpe,” and “ yarp ”
before the vowel sound of each syllable, and ocea-
sionallv, as in “blopoke” (bloke), inserting “p”
after the vowel sound. It is perhaps suggested
by one of the oldest English methods of canting,
mentioned in the oldest slang dictionaries, that of
“inserting a consonant betwixt each syllable;
thus taking G —“ How do you do? ’’ would be
“ Howg dog youg dog ? ” Some of the gibberishes
here mentioned are only remarkable for their
extreme silliness, and are strong evidences of
gradual brain-softening in the inventors.

“ A lick ” ; Consider the lot of the South
Njoast cookie. I know a farmer in Illawarra dis-
trict who gets up in the morning somewhere about
midnight to extract the juice from his kine. This
he carts two miles to the factory, where it tops
the scales at 421bs. Milk is 3d. per gallon at that
factory, and out of the enormous income thus de-
rived he is supposed to find £ll2 per annum rent,
pay a laborer and buy h igh collars for his aristo-
cratic son. Again, a widow in Shoalhaven sent a
truck of pumpkins and onions to Sydney, and
received from the auctioneer as payment a penny
stamp ! Also, three farmers in same district sent
up two trucks of like produce and at settling-up
found themselves 11s. to the-bad.

“ Ilevvi ” ; Resided for a time at a small
N.S.W. township on the Northern line—a great
pumpkin depdt— and daily saw truck-loads of
pumpkins consigned to down-country jam-fac-
tories. The railway officials told me that the
factories took the bulk of the pumpkins railed
from that station.
* * * *
Bill’s carefully thought-out sys-
tem of boozing was to split his cheque,
“ blowing ” a pound here, thirty shil-
lings there, and so on, getting at each
place as much “on the nod” as he
could. His last spree wound up at
the “ Forty-Mile,” the host of which
was a stranger from “further out,”
where pub. “points” are a fine art.
By most people Murphy was
promptly recognised as an individual
upon whom no flies enjoyed their
siesta, but Bill conceitedly thought
that he had forgotten more than
Murphy ever knew. And under this
impression (his cheque being no more)
he proceeded to flatter Murphy on his
and his wife’s appearance, the fine
growth of his children, the sagacity
of his dog, and the staunchness of his
draught horse. All this proving use-
less, Bill started to take fits—having
first emphatically impressed on Mur-
phy that nothing relieved him but
rum—“ plenty of rum.” This scheme,
however, was also a failure, Bill being
allowed to tear up about a dray-load
of gravel whilst Murphy kept his
eye carefully focused on a spotted
cow that was contentedly chewing
her way through the contents of Bill’s
swag on the bank of the creek. So
William had to recover “ on his own ”
and retire to the “dead-house”, to
think things over. Evening falling
and no “shouters ” having appeared
to supply his keen-felt want, Bill
emerged from his retreat to play his
last card. Murphy was smoking his
evening pipe on the verandah when
Bill, with rolling eyes and twitching
hands, accosted him with “Murphy !
Murphy ! Give it ter me. I must
do it. They’re tellin’ me to ! Give
it ter me, quick— quick J ther gun—
ther nun/’’ Murphy calmly went
behind the bar, and returned with a
double-barrelled breeoh-loader, which
lie handed to Bill, saying, “ Yer
won’t want both barrels, will yer?
The nearest side un’s choke fuller slugs an’ I’ve
killed pigs with ’er afore; so she ought ter
do you! But see—get roun’ ther back —I don’t
want no blanky mess ’ere!” Then William did
have a fit —of “ language.”
* * * *
“T.N.” : In Taranaki (M.L.) 20 years
ago, when the Tote first came into vogue, coin
was scarce and produce was often used instead. I
remember the “divvy” to the winner of a race
being 10 sacks fungus, 2 calves, and a waggon-load
of pumpkins.
“ P.L.” : Be the cunning
of animals —birds in particular.
Writer observed (in N.W.
Queensland) a hawk making
various attempts to rob a hen
of one of her chickens —but all
in vain. The mother was too
alert and wary. After a while
the bird of prey came circling
over the brood again with some-
thing in its claws. A piece of
meat suddenly dropped from
the air into a corner of the
yard. The poor hen immedi-
ately rushed off to secure the
prize, forgetting the chicks for
a moment. When she returned
the brood was one short, and
the hawk already far in • the
distance.

N. S. W. Land Appeal
Court decided a much-disputed
point lately. When a selector
has applied for land, some land
boards have allowed the squat-
ter, whose country has been
taken, to appear and take part
in inquiring into the man’s
bona jides ; others have shut
him out. The Appeal Court
decides that the squatter has a
standing, mainly because he is
entitled to the country a 3
against everybody but a settler bona-fide.
Charters Towers in slump-time ! Oh, the
nightmare ! Well, even the Peculiar Doctor has
left; so you can judge how bad things are. And
there’s some £2OOO of “Patriotic Fund’’locked
up in the bank; don’t the subscribers wish they
had it out. The “patriotic” hollowaremerchants
pause as they pass that bank, and gaze sadly —so
sadly. If pathetic glances could melt a strong-
room door !
“Prac.” : A middle-aged woman came to
my surgery and said she was suffering from
“laths.” Her husband was a lath-splitter, and I
was somewhat puzzled, until I found it was a case
of “ shingles ” ( Herpes zoster ). “ Well,” said the
patient, when I told her, “I knew it was some-
thing to do with wood / ”
“ Nurse ” : If all the Victorian magistrates
who adjudicate pension claims are like the one in
my suburb, they’re a pretty rough lot. He looked
over my papers and questioned me till he knew
more than I did myself, then says he —“Can’t you
do any work?” I’m 71 and have worked all my
life, and find old age beginning to tell. So I told
him I was very old, and had bad legs besides.
And there, in that court, I had to pull down my
stockings and show them my legs—and two great,
hulking policemen looking on. Then he says,
“Can’t you do sewing?” I told him my eyes
were none too good. He hums and haws, and
blurts out, “There’s a bit more work left in you
yet. We’ll allow you 6s. 6d. a week.” Ten
shillings is little enough : six-and six is only mak-
ing a fool of a woman.
WALTER LES THETRES, taking his baby-sister for a ride.
One of the first things that greeted the
Dookoyork the morning after his arrival was a silly
Age par stating that, on her landing, the Duchess
had made careful inquiries from Hopetoun re the
tree planted by the Dookoclarence in Ballarat
when he was out here long ago. The par described
how delighted the Duchess was to find that the tree
(which was said to be quite dead) had flourished.
This was too muoh for York, and the paragraph
was violently repudiated. It was worse than
having the photo. “of my first darling husband ”
put up over the bed of the newly-married widow.

John Stanley : Reid protected most thoroughly (by levy-
ing a high duty and no excise) dried apples, biscuits,
candied fruits, confectionery, preserved, boiled, and
dried fruits, jams and jellies, sugar, molasses, &c. By
means of a light excise and heavy customs duty he also
protected beer, cigars, cigarettes, and tobacco. The chief
English trusts are the English Sewing Cotton, Cotton-
Spinners and Doublers, Thread, Indigo Scarlet and
Cotton Dyers, Bradford Coal Trust, Yorkshire Wool-
Combers, United Indigo and Chemicals, Calico Printers,
Wall-Paper Makers, United Velvet Cutters, British Cotton
and Wool-Dyers, Yorkshire Soap Makers, Portland
Cement Makers, The (-alt Union, J. and P. Coates
Limited, Armstrong-Whitworth-Garton-Anderson Com-
bine, The Linoleum Combine, two lace trusts, lace-mak-
ing combination, Lancashire Bleachers Association, Not-
tingham Lace-Curtain Combine, British shipping ring.
These trusts represent a capital of £50,000,000. N.S.W.
duty on sugar is 3s. per cwt. on raw, 4s. per
cwt. on refined, and Id. per lib. on candy. English and
Canadian prices of reapers and binders not available.. ..

to 4 o’clock yesterday afternoon. Four miles from town, on the Goonoo Gcwnoo
State, Mr G. B. G. King, general manager, had a flock of 12,000 sheep mustered
for the Royal party to view, but for some reason the train did not stop.

A Letter from Paddington.
8 Victoria-street, Paddington, N.S.W.,
July 28th, 1900.
Dear Sirs,
Some years ago I was under medical treatment
for severe pains in the back; in fact, I was com-
pletely prostrated. The doctors then wanted me
to undergo the operation of crushing a stone in
my bladder. I did not like the idea of this al-
though the pain I suffered was severe. A friend
advised me to try Warner’s Safe Cure, and, after
taking 3or 4 bottles, I began to feel better. I
continued the course and am now, thanks to this
medicine, free from any trouble whatever.
Yours truly,
T. J. CASTNER.
Messrs. H. H. Warner and Co., Limited.
Warner’s Safe Cure cures all diseases of the
kidneys and liver and disorders arising therefrom,
such as Indigestion, Rheumatism, Biliousness,
Impure Blood, Anajniia, Gravel, Gout, Neuralgia.
Debility, and Bright’s Disease.*
The old inhabitant has his uses, after all.
A man, replacing old posts near Mt. Barker
(8.A.), t’other day, found a lump of gold sticking
to the bottom of one of them ; and the local wise
men began to discuss the theories of “ the genesis
of gold,” when the ancient person limped along
with his stick. And he, being told of the find,
“minded that 30 years or so agone Mrs. Blank
was upset out o’ her cart and lost her false teeth
through the horse shying at some fencing, old
Johnny Brown (or somebody) was putting up,
and when they come back to look for the teeth
they couldn’t sight ’em nowheres.” The explana-
tion was accepted ; the teeth, with the solid gold
plate of the olden time, had apparently rolled
into an open post-hole, and Johnny Brown (or
somebody) had squashed a post on top and buried
them.
Whatever may be the future of the auto-
car, the gee-gee-car is showing great variety of
design and finish, and will make a good fight to
live. Witness the exhibit of the N.S.W. Car Co.,
of Cleveland-st., at late Show, when, out of 11
vehicles shown, nine were awarded prizes—many
of them firsts. Such a record would be hard to
beat.*
Many a yearning for Royal gift or title
must of necessity remain an aching void : The
giving of Gifts is a distribution of disappointment.
But consolation follows in the steps of sorrow.
Better than a Royal snuff-box or autograph photo,
or a C.M.G., is that great dispenser of delight, the
D.C.L. whisky. The D.C.L. gives comfort in the
highest degree. It maketh glad the heart of man
who has failed to shake hands with the “ Duke.”
It blesseth him that gives (or sells) and him that
takes—but especially him that takes.*
A large manufacturing rubber mill, with a
ground floor area of 42,000 ft., is being erected at
Montague, Melbourne, by the Dunlop Tyre Co.
The mill, which will cost £lO,OOO, will manufac-
ture not only tyres but rubber goods of all de-
scriptions, and a very experienced rubber expert
is on his way from Indiana (U.S.A.) to assist the
company.*
The Wentworth Hotel, Church Hill (near
Wynyard Square), Sydney, is now in the hands of
Mr. D. Maclurclan, late of the Queen’s Hotel,
Townsville, Q. For quietness of situation, ampli-
tude of accommodation, perfection of arrange-
ments, and reasonableness of tariff the Went-
worth, as now managed, would be hard to beat.
Two specially notable features in connection with
the establishment are the splendid harbor view
from the roof-promenade and the superintendence
of the culinary department by Mrs. Maclurcan, of
cookery-book fame.*

A Voice from the North.
“The Wreck” writes from the Herbert River
(N.Q.) —where the Jap-man, and the Java-man,
and the China-man flourish, and the White-man
is growing so comparatively scarce that you will
soon be able to put him in the Colonial Sugar
Co.’s museum as a curiosity, with the label—
“ White Man. Found on Victoria plantation,
Herbert River, N.Q. And these are “The
Wreck’s” doleful remarks, punctuated by some
remarks from The Bulletin :
Throughout the whole of this White versus Mottled
Australia controversy you Southerners have adopted a
tone signifying that Queensland is the principal importer
of aliens, of whom the Kanaka is the most dangerous to
the Commonweal. Moreover, your general attitude in-
sinuates that we are lacking in various economic virtues
of which the South has a superfluity, and the which, out
of its superior civilisation, it will graciously and gene-
rously bestow on us. Wherefore this scribe, now that
you have had time to sober up, turns over in his bunk to
make a few remarks.
* » * * *
The Bulletin is a Southerner bv location ; but
it is- by principle Northerner, Easterner, and
Westerner as well—that is to say, it is Australian,
and it puts Australia Always First. It isn’t
responsible for other people’s talk ; and, as far as
its own talk goes, it has always represented
the Kanaka as the least dangerous of aliens.
That Queensland is the principal impor-
ter of aliens at the present time is be
yond doubt, seeing that Japanese and
Javanese are being brought in by hundreds —
hardly a month passes without a fresh shipment
being reported. The 1891 census showed Queens-
land to have a larger propoi tion of colored aliens
than any other State. This year’s census will
undoubtedly show that Queensland’s coloured
population is increasing much faster than any
other State’s —both positively and proportion-
ately to whites. “The Wreck” evidently lost
his compass before he started drifting.
• * • *
In proportion to the wealth of the workers in North
and South, the South has the larger number of aliens. In
other words, the proportion of sordid poverty and desti-
tution makes the Southern alien a more superfluous
industrial factor and his competition with the whites
more bitter.
“ The sordid poverty and destitution ” of tho
Southerner is a myth. There are more poor
Southerners because there are more Southerners.
Savings Bank figures supply one fair test of
workers’ wealth ; and in 1899-1900 there were in
Queensland fewer Savings Bank depositors, per
100 of population, than in any other State ; while
the average amount deposited, per head of popu-
lation, was lower than in N.S. W., Victoria, West-
ralia, S.A., or Maoriland.
• • • *
Also, your aliens intermix with the whites in a more
baneful degree than do ours. The howl about com-
petition with Queensland nigger-grown sugar is bare-
faced hypocrisy, for Kanakas and coolies are extensively
employed in chipping and trashing on the N.S. VY. fields.
Comparison between sugar-lands for white labor north
and south of the tropic is absurd, and to say they are
similar betrays stupidity or bare-faced lying.
There are few Kanakas in N.S. W.— only the drift-
ings from Queensland. There are probably be-
tween 500 and 600 Hindoos; and The Bulletin
has objected just as strongly to the Hindoo as to
the Kanaka. Only it has objected more often to
the Kanaka, because there are more of him
between 8000 and 9000 in Q., besides the Asiatic
brigade. The Bulletin never said that N.S.W.
sugar-conditions were similar to N.Q. sugar-condi-
tions. The Wreck drifts about too promiscu-
ously.
* » * »
The All-White crank is either insincere or wanting in
courage, else a start would be made on his own pet alien
who grows vegetables and supplies opium to the Sydney
and Melbourne women, or hawks cheap finery to the
selectors’ womenfolk and frequently does worse when the
men are away.
The Bulletin started on the Southern Mongrel 20
years ago, and has kept going ever since. But the
Queensland Asiatic population is increasing by
upwards of 1000 ' per annum. The Bulletin
wants first to stop the rubbish from falling in, and
then to clean out the tank.
Your Southern-made Ministry is not honest or courage-
ous enough to put the matter to the people in the only
way in which they can realise its importance—the way in
which Laurier put prohibition to the Canadians. Dare
they appeal to the individual thus : “ Are you in favor of
the abolition of the alien from the Q. sugar industry ? If
so, will you pay a cash tax of £ —, that being your share
of the subsidy necessary to enable that industry to con-
tinue under white labor ? ” Also.: “ Are you in favor of
the expulsion of all Asiatics and Polynesians from Aus-
tralia. If so, will you pay £— as your share of the pas-
sage-money and compensation ? And will you actively,
and by force of arms, if necessary, back up this country
in any difficulty which may arise with the Imperial, or
any foreign government?” Of course it would have to
be stipulated that in the latter event there would be no
4s. 6d. per day. But, of course, you don’t propose to
abolish the alien—merely to remove the local regulation
of him, as did the Northern States in the ’6o’s —as Auck-
land did with its alleged “ social evil,” and as you’ve done
with your own nigger. The alien is to remain in the land
and live on air or liberty, as does the N.S.W. niggei - , who
seems to thrive without being employed 1
Certainly the Barton Ministry will dare to put
those questions to the people—as far as they are
necessary. If, to secure a White Australia, we
have to further protect Australian sugar, and if in
consequence the price goes up (which it won’t),
white Southerners will cheerfully paly to
make sugar-growing a white man’s industry.
When Bundaberg sugar-growers clear £l6 10s. per
acre over 20,000 acres—when W. T. Munro can be
reported from Cairns this year as having
made £2500 profit from 196 acres of cane,
employing white labor only —then the industry
can pay white wages without raising the
price of sugar. As to expulsion, we haven’t
talked very much about expulsion yet: time
enough for that when we’ve stopped the hordes
coming in. In any case, the Q. planter is bound
by agreement or regulation to send most of his
colored laborers home without asking for Southern
help. Yes, we do propose to abolish the alien;
we propose to get rid of the black drop and the
yellow drop altogether; and to preserve the
White Breed pure.
• « * *
The whole anti-kanaka agitation seems due to the
ghost of Mrs. Beecher Stowe’s hysterical sentimentalism.
It is fostered by the cant which condemns feudalism and
glorifies factoryism. That a man should starve or turn
criminal because he can’t make a decent living by honest
(sic) toil is nothing to worry about, but that he should
have no right to vote is a disgrace to civilisation and
demands armed interference. Is the “ free ” Rhodesian
nigger better fed than was his Transvaal brother, the
starving Indian of to-day than the fast-diminishing ryot
of pre-company days ? Is the colored loafer of the New
Orleans levee' preferable to his bonded parents—the
slumdom of New York to the serfdom of the plantation—
and will the free “ walk-about ” Kanaka be a greater
blessing than the present bonded nigger? And so you
yearn to civilise us, to make us a little three-bob-a-week-
half-fed-cocky-laborer, ora fifteen-bob-a-week-and-go-on-
the-street-to-'“ make-it-up’’-community, like unto your-
selves. You are quite disinterested, having “ most to
lose.” So you have—dirt, destitution, and degradation.
Civilization'? Shrivelization !
“ The Wreck ” is breaking up fast; and it isn’t
possible to catch every fragment of him. The
anti-kanaka agitation, to put the matter briefly,
is a protest against modified slavery ; it is a pro-
test against mixing of breeds ; it is a protest
against cheap labor, which tends inevitably to
extend its boundaries and swallow up well-
paid labor ; and it is a protest against
welcoming brown savages into a civilised white
community. The Rhodesian nigger and the
Transvaal nigger don’t attend Australia’s funeral;
the Q. nigger does. We don’t want an equivalent
of the New Orleans colored loafer here ; so we’ll
stop him from coming by excluding his parents.
• * * *
Well, the pastoral industry is wrecked, and its thou-
sands of workers only kept from becoming aggressively
evident by a judicious distribution of Govt, relief. And
you have hysterically decided to wreck an industry which
will add a few rilore thousands to the number of workers
who are degenerating into professional loafers. The sub-
lime ignorance and apathy bred (as Pitt intended it
should be) of indirect taxation, has decided that it is
preferable for an acre to lie idle rather than it should
employ one white and two Kanakas. That there is no
practicable alternative does not worry you—you are all
r 'K h t.
And you will build up big cities, with art galleries and
theatres and banquet halls for the favored few—and, as
for the others —will there not be the gutters, and plenty
of room in the dead west and north. And factoryism will
take root in the land and flourish exceedingly, and Aus-
tralia will be the most civilised country on earth, and
every starving man will have a vote. Selah 1
* * * •
“The Wreck ” has floated out of sight, and his
rhetoric can follow him. But as this correspon-
dent likes questions, The Bulletin puts three
questions to him : 1. What is your name ? If
those are your views, defend them publicly, don’t
hide. 2. What’s your relation to the Kanaka and
the Jap. ? Do you employ them ? Do they em-
ploy you? Or are you a Col. Sugar Co. mill-
parasite or field-boss. 3. How else did the tar get
into your cocoanut !
At Poverty Point.
(Continued.)
Coppin, as a young man, was in the employ of Daven-
port, the original Vincent Crummies, and taught the
Phenomenon to play the violin.
“Major ” Winchell, of the deathless Harmy, has been
unfolding to a ’Murkan newspaper reporter his scheme
for weaning sinners from theatres “ where the plays are
bad.” The Harmy will start a Grand Salvation Theatre
of its own on sound commercial principles. Everybody
concerned in the performances, including the band, will
give services gratis, so the only charge on the receipts
will be for rent, lighting and stage fittings. The item of
author’s fees will be avoided by the Major's simple device
of writing all the dramas himself. Thus far he has two
shows in active preparation—a six-act drama entitled,
“ Jonah and the Whale," and “ The Prize Fight of Life ”
in “ what he calls 10 rounds,” the last round concluding
with the presentation of a Crown of Glory to the hero
who lias knocked the devil out. These are “ great,” says
the author. They include strong scenes for the leading-
lassie and the principal man, likewise lots of fat for the
low-comedian-lieutenant, and the Major anticipates a
long hallelujah run for both pieces, especially “ The
Whale.”
A recent discussion at a London theatrical club (the
0.P.) brought out the opinion of one Mr. Montague Davis
that “ the club should not be bothered by the personal
quarrels of actors and critics.” What sort of criticism is
to be expected from men who expose themselves to the.
possibility of personal quarrels with mummers whom they
ought not to know off the stage !
Major Pond, the Lecture Engineer, in bis “ Recollec-
tions,” gives a strange instance of the callousness of
Bamum, on whom, however, he has a palpable edge of
animus. Pond was sitting by Barn urn’s side at the
Hippodrome when a lady was killed in the Chariot Race.
Pond: “This is shocking.” Barnum : “It will be a
benefit.”
“ H.” : I do insist that Oscar Wilde’s “ Woman of No
Importance ” is a right noble play—the finest, in point of
moral, written for a great many years. It made a deep
impression on me, as acted by Titheradge and Mrs.
Brough. The idea is from the “ Natural Son,” of Dumas,
dealt with in a robust, manly fashion. There is the
woman, anxious to shield her son from the contaminating-
influence of his father, and succeeding. The treatment is
from the highest point of principle.
The escapade of Georgia Hodson, afterwards Mrs. W. S.
Lyster, is detailed in Mrs. Gilbert’s Reminiscences, last
Scribner. She bolted shortly before a New York per-
formance of “ Pocahontas,” in which she had the chief
part. The audience knew the piece by heart, and that
was the prime joke in Brougham and Walcot gagging
without her. Brougham, as Powhatan, spoke her lines
with, “As Poky would say.” At last, when he had to
say to Capt. John Smith, “ Take her, and be happy,” he
handed him a broom.
Mrs. Gilbert, in her entertaining Reminiscences, now
being published, has something new about Wilkes Booth,
Lincoln’s assassin. He was the best Romeo she ever saw,
and a man of the most winning personality. Junius
Brutus Booth had three sons—J. 8., Edwin, and Wilkes,
all leading tragedians. “The Corsican Brothers” was
founded on a real case of fraternal sympathy—as it were,
Siamese Twins without any visible bond. Such a sym-
pathy there was between Wilkes and his mother. Start-
ling instances are current of her second-sight regarding
him, and the warning letters she would send.
Our old friend, Charles Cartwright, the strong, “ hard ”
actor, with the strident tones, has been playing Charles
II in one of the recent Nell Gwynne dramas. “Truth to
tell, the part does not sit well on him,” said the Mornino
Post. Which means, of course, that C. 0., as the giddy,
over-dressed royal masher, was about as convincing as
Charles Arnold would be in a performance of Oliver Crom-
well.
O. H. Taylor and Ella Carrington have made a hit,
touring Japan, with “ Rip Van Winkle.” A Japanese
legend is oddly similar.
The new Bishop of London, who promises to disturb
the alleged “convictions” of Anglican churchism, was
nearly married to Lady Ulrica Duncombe a while
ago. The engagement was broken off, otherwise
the alliance would have formed a notable link between
Alice Leamar and the Bench of Bishops. Sweet Alice’s
sister married a brother or uncle of Lady Duncombe
aforesaid, and although the Hon. Mrs. Duncombe was
subsequently divorced by her husband, the connection
between the two families still holds good in the sight
of Heaven, where marriages are made.
“ Pukaki” : In Auckland once, when a variety troupe
was performing at the City Hall, I used to look in there
fairly often, and noticed that when Evie Williams, a girl
of 16 or so, came on to do her turn, a youthful admirer in
the front row invariably threw her a miserable little
bunch of flowers, about the size of a buttonhole. I de-
termined to play him a trick which I thought at the time
was funny, but which I see now was merely “ low-down.”
I spent a few shillings on a really decent bouquet, which
I meant to throw to Evie as soon as her young
adorer had thrown his, and I grinned at the thought
of how he would be taken down. That night
I took the only vacant seat in the front row —next to a
young man in corduroy pants, evidently from the
country, half asleep, and exhaling a delicate odor of
whisky. I sat down and stowed my flowers under the
seat. He continued to doze, and at the interval I
went out, met a friend, and wasn’t in a hurry to get
back, as I knew Evie didn’t come on until near the end.
But when I did stroll back, after about 20 minutes,
another girl was finishing a song, and to my horror I saw
my drowsy friend rise unsteadily, and hand her up my
flowers, amid tremendous applause ! It served me right I
Died, lately in Berwickshire, Scotland, Lady John Scott,
who composed the best-known air to “ Annie Laurie.”
Far from Fashion’s feast and riot,
Where her maelstrom never whirled,
You have wrought in queenly quiet
Golden chains to bind the world ;
And tho’ none may know your story,
And tho’ few have heard your name,
Every bar of “ Annie Laurie ”
Is a wreath of deathless fame.
Fame that nought of wealth discovers,
Nor the laurel-wreath that dies,
But the thanks of laughing lovers
With the rose-light in their eyes ;
Not for you the rocking steeple,
Nor the rainbow flags unfurled—
But the hearts of happy people
Bear your love-song round the world.
And the air of “ Annie Laurie ”
Will ring on across the years ;
Men will hear the threadbare story
Smiling, some, and some with tears ;
And a-down the drifting ages
Through the wide world East and West,
All the stars on all the stages
Sing the old song “ by request.”
WILL OGILLVIZ.

is solicited.

Stories,” by Albert Dorrington (“Alba Dorion ”)

Australia. The S. Daily Telegraph recognises

says the volume “ contains dramatic pictures
which the best of our artists in words might well
envy.” A handsome cloth-bound volume, 350 pp.,
post-free from Bulletin office for 4s. 6d.
“ Gunner ” on an army subject :
Ed. Bulletin. —“ Scotty W.” (8., 13/4/01) doesn’t seem
to see all the bearings of alleged treatment of “ colonial ”
officers in British regiments. How many of said c.o.’s can
stand the regimental racket—can pay mess, band, polo,
and other subscriptions, and put up their share of the
cost of entertainments ? A British regiment is, in theory,
a club or a family, all members of which are expected to
live after a pattern which is generally set by the richest.
The result is that men avoid, if possible, regiments
affected by rich men, unless they can go the regimental
pace. The abolition of purchase also has operated against
the impecunious man, who used to drive a profitable trade
in changes. This system in its ultimate result was simply
a device forgetting seniors to retire, and thus quicken-
ing promotion. The principle was applied in the
“ Officers’ Benefit ” Funds of the E.T.O. regiments, which
had no purchase. If a field officer happened to be stop-
ping promotion he would be offered so much out of the
Benefit Fund—to which every officer in the regiment con-
tributed —to retire, and he generally took the offer.
There is one very serious ground of complaint against the
“colonial” officer. All vacancies in the British Line
regiments are supposed to be filled up, either
regiment-ally from the militia 3rd battalions
of the regiments, or by cadets who have
“passed” out of Sandhurst. Almost every officer
in the regiment will be more or less interested in candi-
dates pf one or both classes. The “colonial” man—per-
haps a person of mature years —joins as junior-subaltern,
instead of the lad of 20 or so. His character is formed,
and his habits are fixed by years of civil employment.
There is something very false for such a man in the posi-
tion of the junior-subaltern—ordered about by all the
other officers, checked for small faults by the adjutant,
patronised by the sergeants, and perhaps even
prompted in an under-tone by the men in
the ranks. In all this there is probably not
the least ill - will, only a half - contemptuous pity
for the bearded man who is willing to take a boy’s place.
The “ colonial,” even if he knows his drill, is necessarily
ignorant of the petty details of regimental duty ; and
when it comes to his turn to attend to these, the orderly-
sergeant at his elbow will be perpetually saying, “ Beg
pardon, sir, we do it this way ” ; or, “ Sir, sir—the Colonel
won’t have it done that way ” —and so on, till the poor
man doesn’t know “ where he are.” Then the adjutant
or captain of the day is continually “ getting-on-to ” him
about some unnoticed trifle. All this—which would
matter little to the boy-subaltern—is a constant worry to
the older man, who "cannot quite reconcile himself to
being found fault with for trifles which he cannot realise
as part of a system. The “ranker” is in a different
position. He knows all the ins and outs of the system,
and never makes small mistakes; nor does he put
on airs with other officers, as it is to be feared the
“ colonial ” sometimes does. The whole regimental sys-
tem is based on “ seniority,” not of age, but of rank, and
seniors will keep juniors in their places. Hence the
trouble.

POLITICAL
POINTS
“N. : Battersea, England, decides to
nan.e a street after Joubert. Who would dare
propose such a thing in Australia ?
In the Westraliau elections the Laborites
won five goldfields seats and only one other—
Subiaco and Leederville, which was collared by
Dagleish, formerly chief clerk in Melb. Detective
office.
“ We should have been stronger, richer,
and higher in the world’s esteem had our South
African possessions never passed beyond the
range of the guns of our men-of-war.”— Conan
Doyle.
In the Westralian State elections last
month no less than five candidates, in the one
goldfield electorate of Kanowna, failing to get a
fifth of the successful one’s total, forfeited their
£25 deposits. Hastie, a Laborite, won the seat.
Abraham, M.P., the Welsh collier mem-
ber, pleading in the Commons for time to deal
with a Miners’Eight Hour Bill: “Fifteen days
you have devoted to discussing amendments to
kill man ; not one day will you give for a bill to
save men’s lives.”
It works ! London County Council,
prodded by Maekinnon Wood, turns its attention
to the municipalising of London docks—a scheme
that will mop up, say, £30,000,000 for a start.
Wood has been so successful in similar agitations
before, that this may go through before long.
N.S.W. is again making a great effort to
“find work for the unemployed.” Minister
O’Sullivan is putting many hundreds on at scrub-
cutting, forest-thinning, ring-barking, sand-shift-
ing, and all the other stereotyped jobs, and the
good old loan covers the wage-bill as usual. Then
the loan makes more interest to pay, and that
makes more money going out of the country, and
that makes more poverty, and poverty makes
more unemployed, and they mean more scrub-
outting and sand-shifting and other relief works,
and these mean more loans, and so on ad
infinitum.
NO WAY TO TREAT A LADY.
J.P. (to drunk and disorderly): “Fifty
shillings or seven days.”
THE LADY: “ Well, I was never more insulted in my life !”
“ W.J.” : Melb. barrister Irvine clearly
showed, at the recent Federal election, that
the habit of being ignorant is growing on him.
Questioned as to a White Australia, he stubbornly
refused to know anything about the idea, and
finally summed-up the term as a “ political catch-
word.”
Ebenezer Ward, ex-actor, journalist and
squatter, is trying to succeed to Playford’s seat in
S.A. Assembly, He represented Gumeraeha, the
constituency he i 3 now canvassing, over 30 years
ago, and Gumeracha still remembers him. This
is bad for his chances, and defeat _is practically
certain.
The cable tells of the raising ~of the six
months’ siege of Hoopstad, whose garrison is
stated to have endured greater hardships than the
defenders of Mafeking. And there are no bon-
fires, no congratulatory cables, no big drunks. In
fact, nobody seems to be aware that there is any
Hoopstad.
The Daily Telegraph printed the See
manifesto and the Lee ditto in parallel columns.
This is how it begins to bash its Opposition Joss.
The awkward fact was that See came out with his
programme first, and then Lee came out with
exactly the same programme, and the Telegraph
rubbed it in.
Most likely the Laborites will find a
political resting-place for Donald Macdonell—who,
all things considered, made a good fight for a
N.S.W. Senate seat—as State Assembly Minister
for Cobar, the seat lately occupied by W. G.
Spence. Next to the Barrier seats, Cobar seems
about the safest M.L.A.’s job in N.S.W.

From a letter by Isitt, the athletic M.L.
parson and Prohibitionist, now in England:—
Of one thing you may rest assured. Right or wrong, the
anti-war man is coming out on top. lam puzzled over
some phases of the question, and say little and hear all I
can, but already the evidence of a reaction is every-
where, and J. Chamberlain is riding surely to the fall
Labouchere and others so ardently desire for him.
Premier Seddon promises a new Local
Government Bill for Maoriland this session. If
it does something to cut down the numberless
County Councils, Road Boards, Town Boards,
Drainage Boards, Sheep Boards and River Boards
that now infest the country, it will be a boon and
a blessing to everyone. Almost every place in
Maoriland has six Boards on top of it, and the
country looks like a mental sawmill.
“ Democracy is aroused over there,” says
King O’Malley, and it really begins to look like
it. Gellibrand, the hardest fossil in Tasmania’s
Ancient House of Tombstones, has just been
opposed and beaten for his seat. The Mercuky
weeps sadly over the fact that a tried and reliable
sleeper of Mr. Gellibrand’s calibre has, after 30
(boo-hoo) years service, been passed over for a
new (boo-hoo) man who has had no (boo-hoo) ex-
(sob) perience (snuffle) whatever.
A letter from Westralia :
Saw poor old Premier Throssell and party a few days
before the State elections being driven in big style up
Bayley-street, Coolgardie. Although it was a Ministerial
visit, and the street was fairly crowded, poor old George
looked from side to side for an encouraging cheer, or a
wave of the hand, all in vain. Little knots of rude
men guffawed as the carriage passed, or made unkind re-
marks, or spat across the road. There is no sympathy
between the wrecked Bigjohn Ministry and the people on
the fields, no matter what anybody says to the contrary.
The Westralian Labor-party has pegged
out its six seats on the Opposition benches, so the
Throssell Government is now considered quite
dead. Another sign of the collapse of the old
Forrest influence is the fact that Bigjohn’s little
brother David was defeated at the Gascoyne.
Time was when the idea of the smallest Forrest
being defeated anywhere in W.A. was beyond
imagining. Now a Forrest is only an ordinary
man. And the time may yet come when he will
be only a bloke.
The report comes down from N. Queens-
land that Abdul Wade, the Bourke camel
man, is taking up 100 camels to carry
dressed ore, &c., from the outlying
Chillagoe mines to the railway. With
him goes, no doubt, the Afghan driver ;
and so N. Queensland grows more
Mongrelia than ever.
About the new S.A. Ministry :
Jenkins, S. A.’s new Premier, is believed to have had
some trouble in forming his Ministry. There was a sur-
plus of eligibles, and there were other difficulties.
Butler, who becomes Treasurer, is the only man available
with any ideas of finance, save Grainger, who has other
qualities which seem to keep him out of every Ministry.
Laborites are greatly chagrined at the omission of their
leader, Tom Price ; they expected a representative in
place of the resigning Batchelor, who was Labor leader
when Holder took him in. Brooker, ex-Govt. whip, is
Minister of Education. He has been an honest and con-
sistent democrat during his II years of politics. Jenkins
has got together about the best available crowd in the
democratic ranks, but almost any crowd is good if it has
a good leader, or bad if it has a bad one. John Greeley
Jenkins has been a first-class subordinate, anyhow.

Good Samuel Mauger, Victorian temper-
ance boss and Pleasant Sunday Afternooner, was
much shocked by what lie saw during his visit to
N. Queensland. Chinese and Japanese swarming
everywhere, half-castes more than frequent, white
women living with Kanakas, Japanese prostitutes
openly countenanced because the hordes of niggers
are not expected to be celibates, and so on. “ The
moral degradation of the State is awful. For in-
stance, in one recent shipload of Islanders were
eight women, all diseased on arrival, but all freely
admitted.”
The usual returned Contingenter, signing
himself “Disgusted,” writes to Sydney Evening
News to say that he has been back four months,
that none of the loud, rich Jingoes who promised
the departing Boer-shooters their country’s eter-
nal gratitude will give him a job of-any kind, that
he is ill as the result of hardships in S’Africa, and
has to pay for medical attendance out of his own
scanty means, that he is £l5O to the bad, and that
Boer-shooting is all dust and ashes. And the
moral is : “ Put not your faith in Jingoes.” The
Bulletin said so over a year ago.
In educational matters the country people
of Western Queensland have a very serious griev-
ance. Townships that muster 100 children have
wooden, tin-roofed schools erected to accommo-
date 40, while the big towns, already well pro-
vided with educational facilities, are being daily
supplied with the best of teachers and apparatus.
The secret lies in local log-rolling. The big cities
can muster a deputation of members of Parlia-
ment at a few minutes’ notice, while sandy, dusty,
blear-eyed God-forgotten catches a flying glimpse
of its member, perhaps once in three years.
A reverend gentleman writes from Mel-
bourne :
An item in the reception of the D. and D. of O. and Y.
was suppressed by the Melbourne press. After the civic
functionaries had welcomed the visitors at Princes’s Bridge,
Mr. Fleming and the unemployed, with red flag and cross
bones, attempted to present them with an address. They
had proceeded so far as to have gained the ear of the
Duke when the leader was unceremoniously seized and
felled to the ground by a plain clothes officer. So ended
this part, and the Royal procession passed on. The Melb.
press was more reticent than the tomb on this matter,
and only those in the immediate vicinity realised t
had happened. This is a land of Free Speech !
Sydney Telegraph lamentation : V*
The Barton Ministry is a Protectionist Ministry, and
only wants the opportunity to tax the people to the last
limit of their endurance for the support of industries
which, without such subvention, could not be carried on.
And the still worse offence is that the Barton
Ministry doesn’t see its way to further tax the
people of N.S. W. for the support of the Freetrade
Telegraph, which has hitherto truculently de-
manded and regularly enjoyed 50 per cent. Pro-
tection under the postal regulations, and been
pauperised by public subsidies until it has for-
gotten how to carry on without them.
Dear Bulletin.— Re N.S. W. Full Court’s
extraordinary leniency in the Heydon case. A
few months back a certain solicitor, at the dicta-
tion of another solicitor, wrote a letter (for which
he was paid, on his own statement, six guineas),
which he admitted in the witness-box had not one
word of truth in it from beginning to end. Quoth
C.J. Darley at the time—“ During 45 years of
Bar and Bench, never have I heard a solicitor
make such an admission.” Yet we have heard
nothing more of this case. Why ? Some explana-
tion is wanted from his Honor, or some action on
the part of the Law Institute.— Qilerens.
