“ W.P.D.” on the Federal capital :
lie “ J.B.’s ” par. (8., 4/5/’l) about Federal capital water
resources. The cost of water conservation at the Bom-
bala site would be nothing for a long time to come, so
that of the Carcoar-Garland site couldn’t be much less.
“ J. 8.” hasn’t read Water-Engineer Blomfield’s evidence
m Mr. Oliver’s report. Blomfield says : “ A storage reser-
voir would not be necessary for some considerable time •
but, if required, was obtainable at an elevation of 60ft.’
above Lord’s Hill, where an inexpensive weir could be
erected 30ft. high and 600 ft. long, with good foundations.”
(Report, p. 72.) Mr. Blomfield’s “considerable time”
may be taken from the context of his evidence to mean
till the city has a population of 250,000, and surely that
is time enough. Bombala is a town with a swift, clear
good-sized river flowing right through it, and that is
something to start with in the way of water.
“ Trotter ” sends a yarn from the remote
North:—
When you have trotted around Queensland and come
to Thursday Island you would imagine you had struck
the Malay Peninsula. This place contains samples of
almost every conceivable colored race and clan and sept
under the sun. Work in hotels and billiard-rooms, boat-
building’, laundry work, boot-making, store-keeping and
every kind of labor is done by the dark man. White men
working m the boats for a well-known “ white ” shell-
getting firm receive the enormous salary of £2 10s per
month with rations of bread and meat included—milk
jam, butter, &e., they have to buy themselves. While
here I “ dined ” at a second-rate pub., and at one end of
the table a nondescript medley of colored people were
congregated. As I was in a hurry I asked the girl to
attend to me, when she replied, “ Wait till I’ve served'
these gentlemen.” And every white person at Thursday
waits till the colored gentleman is served—more or less.
Sargood takes his defeat for Presidency of
the Senate badly. When it was known that
Holder would be Speaker it seemed highly improb-
able that another South Australian would get the-
Presidency, and Sargood was considered a dead-
bird. A consoling theory advanced on Softgoods’
behalf is that S. A. men get both billets through
an unfair combination of “small States,” but if
there had been any such combination it is almost
certain that the small States would have demanded
a better division of the spoils. The simple ex-
planation seems to be that Baker’s only two rivals-
for the Senatorial Presidency were Tory members
of the unspeakably-Tory Victorian House of 48
Rich Landlords, and the Senate wasn’t taking any
Victorian Bich Landlord. In other words, that
Sargood and Zeal lost on their own demerits.
During the last two years there has been
a grievous slump in Queensland’s sugar produc-
tion. That State turned out 163,734 tons of sugar
in 1898, 123,289 tons in 1899, and only 92,554 tons
in 1900. The drop in two years amounts to no
less than 71,180 tons—nearly one-half of the out-
put. When the Queensland sugar product in-
creased by only several thousand tons during part
of the time when Griffith was restricting the impor-
tation of Kanaka labor, it was set down by all the
Black Labor papers as conclusive proof that the
industry could not get on without its Kanaka.
The smallness of the increase was held as proof
that the business was lost, ruined, blighted. The
output increased very largely before the end of
the Kanaka restriction period, but these few years
are still the subject of loud hollering in the nigger
press, which calls them an evidence of the impossi-
bility of dispensing with Thomas Tanna. Now
with abundance of nigger-labor, a huge and per-
sistent slump has happened—and it is set down to
the weather.

Some scraps from an enormously long
letter left behind by a recent Launceston (Tas.)
suicide :—
“ Before reading further, get a firm grip of a post and
hold your breath while I break it gently. Now, ready:
hold tight, it’s coming. In the words of the Dutchman,
I am going to ‘ bore a leedle hole mit mine head in mit a
revolver.’ There, the terrible tale is told. Now, if you
haven’t fainted, you can leave go the post. ... If one
has an income of three or four thousand a year he can
make life worth living ; but with less it is only an exist-
ence. In my opinion, this world is a world of imperfec-
tions. The one and only perfect thing in the whole
universe is death. ... I want no glory. I just want to
be dead. Merely that and nothing more. Don’t you run
away with the idea that this is a terrible tragedy ; nothing
of the kind. Simply one of life’s little comedies in three
acts. Act 1—Dead, and like Paul Jones, doing - well. Act
2—Inquest; verdict, serve him right. Act 3—Buried,
and left alone in his glory. I have thought of every
death imaginable, but threw them all out except drown-
ing or shooting. I couldn’t make up my mind which
would be the surer death, so tossed up. Heads, shoot;
tails, drown. She came up heads, so shoot it is. It’s
very funny when you have only a few days to live ; when
{mu are doing or seeing this, that, or the other for the
ast time, especially when you forget about the shuffling
off, and get planning things for the future. . . . To-mor-
row is my last day, and I am going to make the most of
it. First thing, I will have a good go at the piano, play
all the old favorites for the last time ; then I’ll take the
dogs for a run, and have a look round the old town for
the last time, and I think I’ll invite myself up to have
tea with you and Nance, and finish up the day with a
little revolver practice—take pot shots at myself. I’ll
just give you a description of my death ; I think I could
do it in a little poem ; it will be very pathetic, but weep
not, tears are rain. Here goes :
The verse was very bad, but it was characteristic
of the Australian’s incurable tendency to dash off
a little thing of his own in all times and circum-
stances. The jury had never met a suicide like
this before, and was so shaken out of its ordinary
rut that it didn’t even bring in the usual verdict
of “ temporary insanity.”
The Dookoyork’s honor list in Victoria
was a Crushing Blow. Many who were rabid
loyalists two months ago are ready to bite your
head off if the name of the midget prince is men-
tioned to them. It was believed that honors were
to be shed around like feathers in the moulting
season. Their friends had already begun to call
Peacock ‘‘Sir Alexander,” and M’Culloch (the
chairman of the Celebrations Committee) “ Sir
William,” and Speaker Mason “ Sir Francis.”
Isaac A. Isaacs nearly got a permanent squint
looking round the corner for a knighthood. The
dailies gravely announced that the investiture
had been held back a few days in order that the
Federal Speaker might be included, but Holder
never got the refusal. People are now diligently
hunting about to find out whether there is to be
another list before the Dook leaves.
A special session of Tasmania’s Parliament
is to be summoned for the purpose of further re-
newing the concession of that dreadfully slow
public worm—the Great Western Railway Syndi-
cate. This will make the second renewal, the
syndicate having twice undertaken to get its job
done by a certain date, and having barely made a
commencement after all. It will also probably
be the last renewal; when the co. comes round
next time it is quite likely that railway construc-
tion will be a Federal matter, and it is equally
possible that the Commonwealth Parliament will
tell the co. that it is a fraud and an incompetent
delusion, and send it about its business. By way
of making it look as if there was some public busi-
ness on hand, Parliament is also to pass an address
to the Jookoyork and make some remarks about
an insect that is alleged to have got into the local
apple-trees.
The police-court Bench has ordered Police-
man X to hand over to witness Exton the famous
Coningham necklet. Presumably the case ends
there, though it is hard to say. The Coningham.
difficulty has broken out in so many new places
already that it may develope another fresh crater
any day.
A WONPCRFUL CUR€.
VITADATIO
-A-G-A.USJ*-
Suffered from Cancerous Growth for
14 Years.
THREE LEADING SYDNEY DOCTORS PAIL
TO CURE HER.
Mr. S. A. PALMER.
Woollahra, Sept. 11,1900.
Dear Sir, —I feel it my duty in the interests of humanity
to let you know what VITADATIO has done, and is
doing, for me. I have been a martyr to suffering for the
the last fourteen years with a Cancerous Growth and
Ulcerated Stomach, and Bleeding Piles, and Swellings in
the Legs and Feet. Nine years ago I was examined by a
leading Sydney doctor. He told my mother that I was
suffering from a Growth in the Abdomen, and would
never be any different. He operated upon me, and in
three months I was as bad as ever. He again operated,
with the same result. Then I entered the Prince Alfred
Hospital, and was there for six weeks, and the doctors all
told me that they could do nothing for me whatever, and
that I was suffering from Gastric Ulcer in the Stomach.
I left there incurable, and in a very weak state. Then I
interviewed another leading Sydney doctor. After ex-
amination he said I was suffering from a Cancerous
Growth, and he could do nothing for me. Not being
satisfied I called to see another doctor. He put me under
a powerful galvanic battery until I was black, and said it
was done to shake the growth down, and it was some
time before I was brought round to my senses again. He
sent me home in a cab, and told me if I had to call in a
doctor not to send for him. Then I was for twelve
months under the treatment of a well-known Sydney
herbalist. I received some benefit, but the treatment
did not effect a cure. Of late years I have suffered
terrible agony, and my body swelled to an en ormous size.
I gave up all hopes of ever being cured. I have spent
hundreds of pounds in medicines and doctors, to no
benefit. A few weeks ago a particular friend of mine ad-
vised me to take VITADATIO, saying that it would cure
me. I took his advice, and I am very glad that I did so,
for, to my surprise, after taking three large bottles, pieces
of white skin began to come away from me like whites of
eggs, and have continued to come away ever since. I
I have lost 121 b. in weight since the Cancer began to come
away. I can now go about and walk any distance with-
out any trouble, which I was unable to do previously to
taking VITADATIO. J
I consider that it is a wonderful medicine, and in my
opinion is worth £1 per bottle, and cannot be made too
widely known.
I shall be glad to give any further information re my
case on application for my address at the Institute, 184
Pitt-street, Sydney, or at the Paddington Agency, 377 .
Oxford-street, Paddington.
[Note.—The writer of the above wonderful testimonial

higher pay—which is hardly surprising, consider-
ng that the salary of some of them is equivalent
io but £6O per annum.
So-called “ Paris-made” gowns are mostly
turned-out by Bohemian establishments; the
Viennese coming next, and the English being
well represented. Very little of such work is
really done in France.

her own food, she is dependent upon pro-Boers for :

Britain is parasitic in this sense, that she can j
only exist for six week's without sustenance :
from outside. ... If each man, woman, J
and child in the United Kingdom were j
strong as Sandow, and could shoot as straight as
the Queen’s prize-man, the prowess of the whole
forty million hungry Sandows would be absolutely
useless for British defence if the British fleet were
beaten at sea, or if a naval disaster were to occur j
similar to one of the three defeats which Lord j
Roberts was sent out to redeem. They would
have nothing to eat.” — Aknold White.

He Took a Notion.
Better to take a notion than anything belonging
to another man. A notion will do you good if it
be of the right kind. There are good notions and
bad notions, but Mr. Creed, of Mayfield-street,
Drake, N.S.W., had a good notion —a notion, in
fact, which probably saved his life. What better
notion can any man have ? Read what Mr. Creed
writes to us:—“For a long time I had a very
violent cold, such a cold which leads to consump-
tion, or something equally as bad, and had a most
uncomfortable rasping cough, which used to shake
my whole system, and seemed to break me up.
I got a notion that Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills
would relieve me. I took them, and the result is
that they cured me.” To show his good faith,
Mr. Creed has got Mr. James Allison, proprietor
of the Mount Pleasant Hotel, Drake, to witness
his signature to the above statement, which we
have received. Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills
cure such cases, and are a positive cure for Sick-
Headache, Biliousness, Indigestion, Dyspepsia,
Constipation, Liver Complaint, Kidney' and Fe-
male Troubles. Sold by chemists and store-
keepers, price Is. per bottle, or six bottles for
6s. 6d., or same will be mailed, post paid, upon
receipt of price. Sole proprietors, The W. H.
Comstock Co., Ltd. (Australasian Depdt), 58 Pitt-
street, Sydney.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Sydney Tatt’s club-meet at Randwick on
Saturday produced a bad accident. In Tatt’s Stakes,
Called Back came down beyond the 6-furlong post, bring-
ing Pebble and Laurel with him, while Speechless (well-
backed and thought a “good thing”) floundered to his
knees, throwing jockey Godby out of the saddle. None
of the riders were maimed. Called Back, though carrying
a broken leg, struggled 200yds. further, and was subse-
quently shot. Happening is attributed to Called Back
galloping on the heels of the ultimate winner, Caravel
and this is borne out by the raw appearance of Caravel’s
hind legs. Called Back, bred in N.S.W. by Messrs. F.
and A. Martell, was by the Grand Flaneur nag Aureus
from Charming by The Drummer, and promised well
• • * *
Deaths of crack pony jocks Cohn and Allen
through accident at Rosebery Park (Sydney) course last
week recalls that other riders—Rooke, Clayton
M Laughlin, and Elliot met their ends somewhere
about the same spot. Why is no effort made to render
the turn safer, or why does not someone in authority
move the authorities to make the effort ? That particular
place is generally looked upon as dangerous, to put the
thing mildly.
* * » »
Sydney-trainer H. Raynor’s diminutive appren-
tice, F. Richardson, hc.d the unusual honor of winning the
first race he rode in, when bringing Edna Lyall “ home,”
at Rosehill, t’other day. ’Twas a hot finish, too. Young
Richardson has only had six months of stable-life, but he
comes from a good stock. His brother was up when
Clean Sweep won last Melb. and San Fran last Sydney
Cup. J J
* * • •
Cornstalk State nags, which had been looked
upon as owning the programme, had a bad time at
Q.T.C. meeting on opening day—Saturday. Queen’s
Plate (ljm.) seemed a moral for Kelso’s champion Dandy,
at w.f.a., but, though heavily supported, he ran absolutely
last, and Bris des Nuits won. Description of the race
makes it appear that something akin to the bursting of
that blood-vessel in the head months since affected him.
Lord Rudolph—thought to have a mortgage on the Leger
—finished unplaced in a field of six. Ex-Sydney nag
Melodian appropriated Hamilton Handicap, but as he is
locally owned his win doesn’t count to the Cornstalk
State.
* » • »
Inferiority of N.S.W. “leppers,” as compared
with those of Victoria, is generally ascribed to absence of
encouragement. “ Bill ” Long once contended that the
class of jumping nag in the Cornstalk State wasn’t worth
catering for, and, judging by the response to Sydney Turf
Club’s offer of £l5O for ’chasers, the A. J.O.’s whilom chair-
man wasn’t far out. Only eight entries came along. Most
of those nominated have been with us for a big stretch
and are close up done. The A.J.O. offers £2OO for an
event next month, but the prospects of a good line-up,
unless some come from ayont the Murray, are very poor.
Remedy lies with owners generally. Why not try some
of the numerous unsuccessful flat-racers across country ?
Instances are plentiful where rank failures in one depart-
ment have been big successes in the other, and many
have shaped brilliantly at both games—Australian Star is
the latest case in point.
• * * »
“H.”: Wasat one of Adelaide’s big race-meetings
the other day. Near one of the main entrances, and inside
the track, I saw three knots of men, about 20 in each
knot. At intervals small objects went into the air,
scintillated a moment in the sunlight, then dropped. I
went over and found they were headin’ ’em. Nobody
seemed to mind, not even the police. Near by “ Yankee
Sweat” was doing a rare “ biz.” A little further on I
found that I could get on to a “ bob ” tote or a straight-
out bet at every 10 yards. All these things are illegal in
S. A.
• * « «
There is no mistake about M.L. turfite, Mr.
G. C. Stead’s luck. He has owned the biggest 2-year-old
winner eight times in the last nine years. His latest
(Menschikoff) has gathered in £2555 this season, which
has never been topped by any 2-year-old in Maoriland—
bar Multiform, with a record of £2762.
» » » *
Turf luck comes along to some people six feet
thick. A M.L. punter lately had a golden afternoon.
Possessing an unlucky bundle of 13 notes he put the
bakers’ dozen on a nag with a bookie, stipulating that
winnings, if any, were to be “ all up” on another gee
later on. First prad paid the “limit” (£10), and the
£l3O went on to the second moke, who paid £6 3s. ; total
(less investment), £786 10s. The same man had pre-
viously forwarded £1 to be put on a horse at another
meet on same day, and it returned £76 odd !
* • m m
Comparatively easy to nett a thousand or so
over a trumpery hack-race in M.L., if the nag pounced
upon is an outsider, is paying a tenner, and is able to win.
Money wired away from the course lengthens the totali-
sator odds, but if sent too early suspecting bookies will
slip most of it back to the machine. ’Tis when they
haven’t a chance of doing this that they bellow when
“struck.” But there are always plenty of waiters for a
“limit” price, and when three or four “morals” meet
in one race, well-backed by owners unknown to each
other, some very strong observations follow the result,
and the books have a chance of balancing things.
* * » »
Re par herein (18/5/01), announcing demise of
Tas. sport, Mr. Ohas. Agnew. ’Tis said that, years since,
he and the late Alec Robertson were discussing the death
of a cross-country jock. Robertson expressed a wish that
when his time came it would be as sudden, and Agnew
echoed the feeling. Robertson was killed, at Randwick,
while riding Silvermine— a nag that Agnew was wrapped-
up in, and which carried heaps of his coin on at least two
occasions. Mr. Agnew went off suddenly while crossing
from Launceston to Melbourne.
* * * *
Late ’Ome mail news tells that M.L. sport
Spencer Gollan made a nice haul when N.S.W.-bred
Australian Star landed the London Oup last month.
Prize was 1564 sovs. and a 100-guinea trophy. Twelve
started and ultimate winner, humping 8.11, went out at
11 to 2. Rate was run in mud and proved the softest of
soft things. At Kempton Park (Eng.) Easter Monday
ancient gelding Oban from this section, having 11.12 up,
literally “ walked in ” by eight lengths for 2-mile hurdles.
■* * « •
Worth a line that at Belmore (Sydney) Coursing
Club’s Derby and Oaks meeting, t’other day, pups (Row-
land Rowdy and Lancashire Lass) from one litter (Black
Douglas—Lenos) won the three big stakes, and another
(Black Duchess) of the same lot was runner-up in St.
George Stake to Coo-ee, also by Black Douglas, but out of
Benalla Nellie. This is surely a record ?
• * * *
It was first intended to send the little Jook on
the shoot at Manifold’s, but neither there nor at Ruperts-
wood or Werribee is there any game save bunny. When
Cornwall and York got among the quail, the grovellous
Melb. dailies were all broken-up with admiration of his
splendid shooting, because he brought down 32 brace in
four hours, with plenty of pointers and a loader. Bill
Adams, of Melb. Commercial Travellers’ Club, and Joe
Bannister, of Maffra, with a spaniel and a half-spaniel
half-mongrel, accounted for 230 brace of snipe, in one day,
in ’9B, over ground that was freely shot, not preserved for
a select party. And Jack Allan (bowler Frank Allan’s
brother) and Bannister have put up an even better record.
The York record was really about fifth-rate or thereabout.
* * * *
N.S.W. Rugby rep. team, which is expected to
leave Sydney about middle of August, will find Maori-
landers harder to beat than ever this season. More
attention to gymnastics is being insisted on in the
various centres, result being “ conditioned ” players and
fast and furious games. Visitors will probably play
Auckland, Taranaki, Wellington, Canterbury, Otago,
Southland, and an all M.L. team.

* * * •
Maoriland athletic and cycling bodies now want
spoon-feeding, and intend asking Govt, to subsidise
championship meetings, which the unappreciative public
don t patronise to the extent of making them paying pas-
times. One reason for Govt, cheques is that “ such
sports materially assist in building up the country, and
consequently deserve encouragement.” The cool cheeki-
ness of the application will make Premier Seddon smile.
Govt, already subsidises swimming—an art in which
young M.L. should be proficient; but to demand the
country’s cash towards pap-feeding oft-time cronk bike
pedallers and providing tin-pannikins for lily-white
amateur runners is gall indeed.
* * * *
“ Crab:” .Looks as if the new thing in boats
that Oxford eight won with this year, and which it is said
was designed by and built to the order of Dr. Warr, of
Eton, will prove to have been thought out in Sydney
by the crack builder Ohris. Neilsen, especially as Aus-
tralian Tom Sullivan’s name is mentioned in connection
with Dr. Warr’s idea. Close on two years since Neilson
had instructions from the Mercantile R.O. (Sydney) to
make an eight on lines akin to those of his sculling boats,
the chief principle being to have the craft on top of
instead of in the water. Details of this production were
sent to Tom Sullivan by Sydney’s rowing enthusiast Mr.
“ Jack” Blackman after the boat was launched. The
dimensions and general description of Dr. Warr’s
“notion” fits Ohris. Neilsen’s to the letter. The short
sculling boat was introduced in England by Sullivan as
“ a little idea of his own,” but it is certain that Hunter
River sculler Towns and 0. Smith gave him the lines of
the contrivance.
* * * *
Recent case at Wollongong (N.S.W.) of a stake-
holder paying over to the winner, though warned not to
do so by the loser, and being mulct in the full amount of
the protesting party’s bet and exs., recalls a similar
episode years back wherein the late big sport George Hill
suffered. He held £2OO a-side in connection with a
champion boat-race on the Parramatta. The loser kicked
against the umpire’s decision and demanded the restora-
tion of his bet, but Mr. Hill handed it to the winner.
Subsequent Supreme Court action, however, compelled
him to refund the full sum and pay long lawyers’ bills.
Some pugs are given to this sort of thing but are often
baffled by an arrangement under which the stakes are
handed to a certain individual, who appoints a stake-
holder and passes the money along to him. No one but
the temporary bailee knows who the stake-holder is.
Therefore it is impossible for the loser to serve said stake-
holder with notice not to pay over.
* » * *
“ Spare-me-days ” : Under “ Sporting Notions ”
(Bulletin, May 18) “ Well-known Sportsman ” “ talks
through his neck ” when he gives the deaths of Jim and
George Clark, Pat Anderson, and Ted Barton as instances
of the evils of hard rowing. Good old “Jim” died of
cancer, and George died for the want of vigorous exercise,
for as soon as he left off rowing he commenced to pile on
fat to an alarming extent “Well-known Sportsman”
neglects to state that “Bob” Clark, the brother who
rowed as hard races as any of them (writer recollects
him fainting at the finish of a race against
the Mercantiles) still wears his hat jauntily on
three hairs, as in the old days. M. A. H. Fitzhardinge
and his brother, the Judge, look good for a century each ;
Q. Deloitte, who some 30 years ago was a champion, is to-
day as well-set-up a man of his age as Sydney can pro-
duce. Clarence Hannell, the Newcastle giant, who holds
the reputation of having pulled the poppet-heads out of a
skiff, must now be nearer 70 than 60. Tom Strange,
Jack Wilkinson, Jacky Thomson, Jim Brodie, Dave Mid-
dleton, Bill Bull, Nick Johnson, George Brett, Jim
M‘Kensey, George Seale, Bill Benson, Ted Simpson, Pros.
Williams, Pal. Simpson, Ted and Gerald Kennedy, Charley
Bros, and a host of other old-time amateur rowing men
are to-day very much alive. Chumley, who rowed in the
Victorian boat this year, is over 40 years of age, and has
pulled in seven winning inter-State eights. And then
the professionals. Dick Green, the father of them all, is
as lively as a spring chicken ; Laycock, Trickett, Rush,
“ Footy ” Pearce, George Solomon, Stanbury, Bill Beach,
all magnificent specimens of middle-aged manhood— in
fact the only dead one the writer can recall to mind is
poor Searle, who pegged out through fever whilst almost
a lad.
„ * * * «
“Hermes”: Concerning confidence-men. My
half-Chow spieling friend scooped in the trick and
chuckled softly: “You newspaper men amuse me with
all your balderdash about the poor plucked pigeons. The
birds we take down are not pigeons—they’re hawks and
crows. The pigeons we can’t get at. The other sort
think they are brilliantly cunning, and they always start
out to relieve the flash man with the roll of dummy notes
(two good ones on the outside) of the whole roll, and any
more he may have about him. And they usually fall in.
Sometimes we do the falling, but it’s mostly the other
way. The clever mug plays with nothing but good stuff,
and the innocent spieler plays with 90 per cent, bad stuff,
and that alone tells in the long run. On the other hand,
it is only a question of time till we get pinched. Any-
how, if you will carefully read the reports of the next
half-dozen pigeon-pluckings, you will see that the pigeon
who played up his stuff did it with a sinister design of
getting Very Much for Very Little or Nothing At All—
and, verily, they will no doubt get their reward
Clubs are trumps 1 ”
* * • a
Bruising struggle ’twixt ancient negro Starlight
and brawny muscle-bound Armidale (N.S.W.) laborer
M'Coll at Sydney Golden Gate A. 0., Saturday night, was
all hug and rough from end to end of five rounds. Star-
light evaded easily while he remained strong, which
wasn’t many minutes, and then M'Coll had the game to
himself, for after every swoop and bull-at-a-gate rush in-
dulged in by the up-country pug. the black would be
discovered floundering on the boards, carried there by the
bigger and much more powerful man’s desperate on-
slaughts. It wasn’t interesting at any stage, and only
exciting once or twice. Starlight’s “wipe” went up in
token of capitulation at the end of the fifth 3min. The
black has been in a good many fights of this brand lately,
and takes ’em on as fast as they come, irrespective of
persons or reputation. Same description generally applies
to all. Writer has a notion that “Star” is on a similar
lay to that of the Oalifo rnian Polish Jew Choynski, who
has been fighting anybody and everybody for years with-
out troubling much which way the thing goes so long as
half the money offered is guaranteed him. Most big
fights in Yankeedom are arranged on this understanding
nowadays, though the world is told the winner takes all,
or 75 per cent, with the balance to the loser

Sailor Ball and Maori Ruenalf (matched to “box
the best of 20 rounds” at Gaiety A. 0., Sydney, last week)
sprawled, scrambled, and floundered over each other, like
two escaped lunatics. Half-a-dozen times Ball was only
stopped from going through the ropes on to the floor,
some feet below, by hands from the crowd, raised in self-
Protection. Ball hung on to Ruenalf like a catamount,
ki ( I latter a^e ged) bit and chewed his shoulder, bringing
blood freely. Ruenalf tripped the crazy sailor once, and
then sat on his chest, thumping him, while the crowd
looking on swayed and went wild with excitement and
laughter. The referee—a newcomer, from Yankeeland—
was at his wit’s-end what to do ; the thing beat him
badly. Once he tried to draw the mauling pair asunder,
but Ball resented the interference by landing the
only straight punch delivered throughout the
s^ a ™ble, fair on the referee’s jaw, and that
official immediately squared up for a scrap. By-
and-bye, the police took a hand, and the wild and
woolly engagement was stopped, whereupon the
referee gave Ruenalf the decision because of the other’s
continued fouling, and declared all bets off. This scribe
is sorely puzzled as to why Ball ever gets a match. He
always acts the same way, and hasn’t the ghost of a
notion how to fight. The first time he showed in
Sydney was when he met the ebon-hued dude Felix, at
Seale’s Gym., and then he did nothing but rush head
down into the black’s stomach, under his legs, over his
shoulders, or through the ropes—when he wasn’t rolling
on and mangling the lathy negro on the floor.
* » » *
Whilom lightweight premier pug of this conti-
nent, Peter Murphy, was never in it with Jack Willis in
their match at Gaiety A.C. (Sydney), Monday night.
Money made Murphy' favorite, but after a few exchanges
there was a rush to hedge at an.y price almost. Willis
carried fullv a stone more brawn, and was perfectly' fit,
while Murphy might have been a deal better attuned.
But even if he had been it wouldn’t have availed him
much, for Willis has improved “ out of sight ” since he
came from Victoria, not many months ago. Willis
proved too husky, too tricky, and too hard a
hitter for his opponent, who has about reached
the end of his tether at the game, and should try
some lighter employ'ment—albeit he is yet in the twen-
ties. Things were lively from the jump, and both mixed
punches as if their very lives hinged upon the outcome.
In the second round Murphy was going, having been on
the floor thrice. The third brought the end. Murphy
came into violent contact with the boards early and
stayed there Ssecs., but had no sooner assumed the per-
pendicular than another right-hand smash sent him prone,
and out beyond recall within the allotted lOsec. Murphy
never had a chance at any stage of the disagreement.

All accounts agree that the world’s “light ” and
“feather” champion (“Terrible Terry” M’Govern),
whom Victorian Tim Hegarty' has gone to ’Murka to
meet, is a model and exemplary citizen, which, seeing
that he graduated in the ranks of N’York newsboys, is
somewhat remarkable. Terry is full of sense, and so far
from looking for or submitting to the adulation thousands
of Yankee hero-worshippers are alwa.ys eager to pay him,
he evades and hides from it all he can. Travelling, his
life is as abstemious and unostentatious as it is at home.
He gets with his wife and youngsters as much as possible,
and stays in their company as long as he can. They go
everywhere with him. Yankee writers credit Terry with
being full of manly instincts and a generous friend. Has
M’Govern a parallel in the present annals of pugdom ?

Top-notch middleweight Snowy Sturgeon, who
has been nursing a bad hand since his knock-out of the
Sydney football pug. Jack Oonlon, months back, is
matched to meet a recently-arrived Yankee bruiser giving
the name of “ Kid ” Parker, and will, subsequently,
take on Herb. M‘Kell, lately home from S’Africa.
“Snowy” evidently assumes that the fighter from the
U.S. is a “sure thing” for him, else why enter into
another engagement so soon ; but the man from t’other
side of the Pacific winks one eye and says, “ Wait till it’s
all over.” On appearances, however, writer won’t have
him against Sturgeon, who gave Cribb two such hot tasks
when Cribb was at his best. Apropos , there was talk of
a match between Sturgeon and black Tom Mitchell for
the 11.4 supremacy' of the States, but Mitchell couldn’t be
coaxed to the mark, though clever enough to tackle the
best. He has a fearful dread of Sturgeon since once, at
Werris Greek (N.S. W.), when the snowy one had the black
anyhow, and Mitchell’s supporters rushed the ring, tear-
ing ropes and posts away and ending matters with an all-
round scrap. At least, that’s the way Sturgeon tells the
story.

‘ ‘ Old Timer ”: Principal objection of most
Yankee States to allowing the pending world’s premier
ship struggle between Los Angeles giant Jim Jeffries and
Akron giant Gus Ruhlin to come off in their backyard is
that there’s a probability of one killing the other. This
is bunkum. Deaths in the ring have mostly occurred
among the little ’uns —that affair in London t’other day
(Bulletin par, 11/5/1) to wit. ’Tis hard to find throughout
the annals of the P.R. any instances (bar those of
“ Sandy ” M‘Kay and Simon Byrne) of fatalities wherein
big men were concerned. Which reminds this writer
that two of the biggest pugs that ever contended for
premiership honors were Gully (afterwards M.P. for Pon-
tefract) and Gregson, subsequently the poet of the prize-
ring. Each of these was over 6ft. high, and built in pro-
portion. They met at the beginning of the last century
in Viscount Deerhurst’s Park at Bushey, near Watford.
This was a fight to a finish with a vengeance. Both were
so pumped out towards the close that when sent to their
corners after the last round even money was offered
neither would respond. “ Time ! ” galvanised Gully into
action, and he toed the scratch, but had'hardly done so
before he fainted in his second’s arms. Gregson wasn’t
satisfied, and the same pair met again, with an almost
precisely similar result. Yet there was no death, nor
any thing approaching it.
San Francisco Wasp —a notorious “bor-
rower in its issues of March 16 and 23,
drawings by a former assistant of D. H. Souter’s
—R. G. Russon —which poorly reproduce some of
Souter’s worst mannerisms (mannerisms which he
is now growing Out of). Though Souter has
taken hints from Beardsley and Bradley and
Parrish, lie has merged them in an individual
style of his own ; but Russon seems to be a flag-
rant and incompetent imitator. No decent artist
would deliberately copy another’s style, and take
credit for the copy as original; and no publica-
tion with any sense of artistic honesty would
print such travesties as original work.
Between 600 and 700 ironworkers have
gone on strike at Mort’s Dock, Sydney’s biggest
shipbuilding and engineering institutions, for a
minimum-wage of 7s. per day. The O’Sullivan
yeast is beginning to work. Govt, pays that sum,
whereas the ironworkers of Mort’s Dock have
hitherto been working at 65., and some of them
for ss. per day, for what is practically skilled
work. An engineering co. that cannot afford to
pay its employes 7s. per day has no right to live;
and the fact that a strike has occurred to gain
such a miserable wage from such a wealthy cor-
poration is a mighty argument in favor of a
minimum-wage law.
“ Mahlstick ” : Surfeited with royal paint-
ings, it cost me a little effort to go and see
Tuxen’s Diamond Jubilee, at Melb. Athenaeum.
This is a good piece of work, a cut above the
Royal Nuptials, over which poor Frith and others
wearily moiled. The point of view is from St.
Pauls’ steps, with Queen Victoria looking at you.
Well, it is only about as far from a Tuxen to a
Vernet as from a Vernet to a Velasquez, in deal-
ing with royalty.
“Sister Mary Jane’s top-note” of Jook-
grovel in the Melbourne press was easily reached
by the ’Erald. Reay, its editor, is a teetotaller,
but an editor who had been blind-drunk for a

coming of little Cornwall was
An honor unparalleled in the history of our race ; it is

If the history of the British race included nothing
more honorable than the landing of a young man
and his wife at a suburban pier, then the Empire
is a poor and doddersome thing, and should be
given away with a pound of tea to get rid of it.
The Jook is a gilded fly on the window-pane of a
nation’s history, and the ’Erald is his fly-paper.
Re a portrait which appears in this issue.
The boy riding the bicycle-wheel with his baby
sister on his shoulders is Walter Les Theires, who,
with his two sisters and brother, have recently
been appearing at Sydney Tivoli. They are now
on their way to Maoriland. The eldest is Miss
Ada Les Theires, lfli years old. Walter, whose
picture is given herein, is about a year younger;
Maud is 14, and Charlie, the youngest performer,
12. The baby on Walter’s shoulders is as yet
only an honorary member of the family, being
just about 4 years old. Some of the bike tricks
of these children are really remarkable. Not-
withstanding their Gallic name, the family is quite
plain Australian, and the children are natives
of Brisbane. They were trained by their father,
Mr. Thyer, who in his own youth was connected
with Hutchinson and Tyler’s circus. They first
appeared in Sydney at the Lyceum about six
years ago, under the management of the great J.
C. Williamson. At Commonwealth time they
made quite a hit in Brisbane at a great open-air
performance, when unbelieving Bananalanders
climbed up on to the platform and felt the little
girl who sat on her brother’s shoulders, just to
make sure she wasn’t a doll. She takes a keen
delight in the performance, and Mr. Thyer
proudly swears that David has never had a fall
when riding the single wheel with the little girl
on his shoulders.

The promoters of Barn Bluff—a Tasmanian
much-boomed low-grade, copperish show some 10 miles
off the track the Great Western railway may some day
run—are just now busily cursing Govt. Geologists and
similar interferences with private enterprise. Under-
ground expert Harcourt Palmer, of the 'famous Wait a-
While Gridiron Railway, recently got some Adelaide folk
to put up money to develop Barn Bluff, and on that the
shares went to over £5. Then the Govt. Geologist came
along and said there was nothing to show
that the 8.8. was not quite as good as the
promoters said it was, and on the evidence before him he
had no hesitation in supposing that payable mineral
might be discovered somewhere in the neighbourhood
provided the search was . made deep enough and over a
sufficiently wide area. It was a nice, kind, non-committal
report, which gave the Barn Bluffers such courage that
shares promptly dropped 50 per cent. But, all the same,
there may be something in Palmer’s boast that the B. B.
find is going to build liis beloved gridiron Wait-a-while
railway. One never knows.
• • • •
Copperfield, the once populous little field out-
side Clermont, is to-day a dead town. Most of the houses
are dismantled, in some places only bare posts are stand-
ing to show the traveller where they once were. A few
fossickers still drag out a precarious existence. Mr.
Howard Smith—no connection with the steamer co. —runs
a small store and has a very good private mine there.
Apparently he is the only shareholder in the original
co. who lives beside it. A co. has purchased the right to
the water in the mine and is now busy pumping it out,
having a system of precipitating the copper which gives a
good return for the labor expended. The old mine is a
weird place these times. As one wanders through its old
workings it might be a fairy palace of copper. Every
scrap of iron, every rail, every chain left behind by a past
generation of workers is encrusted with a green deposit
which eats away the original metal, leaving nothing but
a copper shell. It does the same with tin, consequently
old jam and meat tins, after being submerged in the
water for a while, are transformed into rather pretty
ornaments much prized bjr the people thereabouts.
* ■ • *
Morton, in the Port Curtis distriot (Queensland)
is another deserted town. There is a fine police
station and barracks which has never been occupied.
The building was only just finished when the place began
to slip away into “ goldfields that have been.” But there
are a dozen such places in the Gladstone district besides
Morton—Calliope, Crow’s Creek, Tableland, Max Welton,
Deception Creek, Targinnie are but a few, and on every
one there are still a few fossickers. The cyaniders have
stirred up all the old places again in search of tailings.
Wherever there has been an old battery at work within
the last twenty years, you’ll find the cyanider is either
going there, is there, or has just gone. There is hardly a
heap of tailings in Queensland at the present time that
has not been treated, or pegged out for treatment.
**. * *
Clermont (Q.) alluvial is just now dead as Julius
Caesar—no new finds, and old ones doing badly. On the
other hand reefing shows more promise than for many
years—the latest and apparently the best show is at
Western Creek, some 30 miles north of the township, in a
belt of country trending direct towards the old Cape
River diggings and Charters Towers. Ancient Copper-
field, with its copper mine, slumbers peacefully among
the dead.
* » • *
Blair Athol coal-field, 11 miles north-west of
Clermont (Q.), crows vigorously, latest development
being the striking of a 27 foot seam at Bore Hole Pro-
prietary mine. But at present, cartage to Clermont is
12s. per ton, hewing rate 4s. 3d. per ton, and Government
royalty 2d. per ton. Therefore, the find doesn’t amount
to so much as might appear.

“A snug little property,” writes a travelling
Bulletin correspondent, “ is the Silver Spur near Texas
(Q.). Co. has capital of £24,000 —15s. per share paid-up.
It has never made a call. It is down 2'Dft., and its pro-
spects are as good as the proverbially-uncertain lens-
shaped stone will allow. It is working sulphides—of
which a goodly amount is in sight. A year’s supply of
stone has been opened out ready for stoping. Co.’s re-
verberatory furnaces have a capacity of 400 tons. Side
products are copper and lead, and the blast-furnaces for
smelting latter are equal to 1100 tons per month. The
slags from the reverberatory are now being re-treated in
the blast-furnaces. Output of silver 40,0000 z. per year ;
the mine has paid £20,900 in divs., and its average assay
value is 260 z. silver, o‘B9 copper, and 12 per cent, lead to
ton.”
* * * •
Hampden Copper Mines (North Queensland) :
This will be a big mine with railway communication,
which it is bound to have some day. The manager esti-
mates the quantity of ore available for extraction from
the mine at 116,000 tons, worth, on an average, 16 per
cent, copper, representing a gross value of £1,260,000. In
forming this estimate he did not take into account the
pyritic bodies recently discovered below water level, nor
did he include a portion of the mine on which insuffi-
cient prospecting has been done to enable an estimate of
value to be formed. Quantity of ore raised and sampled
on the mine, 5592 tons, worth, in copper, £52,811.
Though the directors regret that the proposal to build a
Normanton-Oloncurry railway was rejected, they antici-
pate that the measure will be re-introduced. The abso-
lute necessity for railway communication with such an
important mineral district must be admitted. Absence
of a railway, and the presence of a drought, almost en-
tirely suspended mining operations for six months. With
a railway, and a high-grade mine, it will probably be
found cheaper to take the ore to the water than fuel and
flux from the water to the mine—in other words, better
to smelt near the seaboard. Proposed to reconstruct the
co., to work the mine on a scale warranted by its value,
for it is generally believed that, with the railway, Olon-
curry will become one of the great copper-producing dis-
tricts of the Commonwealth. Pity equally great things
cannot be said of Chillagoe.
* * • •
Mt. Garnet Freehold : Shareholders determined
to have railway communication. The terms of the con-
tract opposed by one of the directors, J. B. Mackenzie,
who felt so strongly on the matter that he resigned. Re-
ported that, while the railway is building, coke will be
taken in, and matte out, by camels, and that a well-known
Afghan camel-carrier has tendered for the work. If so, it
will probably add one more to the list of appalling blun-
ders committed by the Mt. Garnet management. But the
writer will wait to see if the rumor is true before saying
anything more vigorous about it.

“Shareholder” : The management of Victorian
mining companies may look good on the surface, but it is
tolerably dreadful underneath. For instance, how many
legal managers in Melbourne could take an affidavit that
they neither demand nor receive commissions or bonuses
from merchants to whom their orders are given ? I mean
how many could take it honestly, for some managers
would take almost anything that wasn’t nailed down.
How many could honestly swear that they receive no
consideration from the auctioneer who sells their forfeited
shares ? There is much that doesn’t appear on the surface
in that same forfeited share business. In many cases
forfeited shares are allowed to go at auction at a less cost
than the amount of calls sold for, hence the manager
often holds his own shares in the name of a dummy,
doesn’t pay his calls, gets his own shares forfeited for
non-payment, and buys them back (in the name of
another dummy perhaps) for less than the call. By
judiciously working this device it is possible to hold
shares and pay less than half the calls. Again, some
managers tout amongst shareholders of companies on a
good, solid footing, to get them to use their influence to
dispense with the present manager (though he may be a
competent man) so that he (the' tout) may be appointed
in his place. Where will you find the miner who will ask
a boss to sack a man so that he may get the job ?

Byron Reef (Vic.): “Has sufficient funds in
hand to pay a 6d. div., but the directors have decided to
defer declaring any until Is. can be disbursed.” It would
be much better for them to defer paying any div. until
the mine has been equipped with a stronger winding
plant and heavier battery. Better still, if the mine were
developed another hundred feet to see if the value of the
reef at that level warrants the additional machinery.

Grlenfine South (Vic.): Another horrible drop
in the stock last week, from 80s. to 575., then recovered
to 625. Another fall in the yield— the last 1180 tons for
5630 z., a fraction over Oidwt. The crosscut off the south
drive at 250 ft. level proved a width of 29ft. solid stone.
The manager states that good prospects of gold were ob-
tained at times while driving from wall to wall. But
what does that mean? Anything or nothing. Obviously, the
market has taken it as meaning nothing. A few days later :
“ A full face of stone is being carried, driving south on
the western leg, and the manager states that the pros-
pects are improving.” This is all too indefinite. Vic-
torian mining managers are definite enough when they
can be so without danger, and when they can’t the na-
tural course is to talk of “ good prospects,” “ fine walls,”
“ solid stone,” &c., &c.

• * * *
“ J.C.R.” discourseth about the searching of
miners question :—“The system of searching miners after
‘ putting in their shift ’ below is of old standing. And,
judging from the tales told by miners themselves of eva-
sion of precautionary measures (notably in deep alluvial
claims at Ballarat, where the searching system was
rigorously enforced), I think mine managers are fully jus-
tified in thus safeguarding the shareholders’ property.
Also, as regards reefing-claims, I can cite several convic-
tions for theft of specimens and amalgam. Why should
any honest miner object to be searched, and what other
method can miners suggest of minimising the gold-steal-
ing evil? I have worked on Vic. and N.S.W. goldfields
for over 40 years, and I know what I’m talking about.
Thirty years ago a trucker at Chiltern (Vic.) told me how
he had ‘ got away ’ with a 15oz. nugget from a Bal-
larat claim. He was trucking when the face-men (two)
found the nugget and he proposed to pass it through, as
truckers were not subject to the same vigilant scrutiny
as face-men. He went, accordingly, to the changing-
house, put down his oilskin coat (it was a wet evening),
stripped, and, picking up his oilskin, went into the
dressing-house ; and thence took his oilskin—wherein
was concealed the nugget—home. Five ounces a-piece !
Again, an old Ballarat man told me how a face-man, work-
ing alone in a cross-drive, found a big nugget and put it
aside till late in the shift, when he sent up all his wash.
Then, when the trucker was away, he wrapped the nugget
in a pair of old overalls, trampled the lot among the wet
muck under foot, filled all the mullock and cleanings into
the truck, and sent-up. At night, he went to the rubbish-
tip and raked it over until he found the overalls—and the
nugget. Another instance : A Oastlemaine reefing-co.
lost heavily through the passing of rich stone under-
ground along levels to where men sinking an exploration
shaft could pass the stuff safely through. The men
prospecting were not searched as were those employed
where the stone was mined. The licensing of gold-buyers
is a useless precaution. The latest device of organised
gold-stealers from deep or company-owned mines is to
have stationed at some alluvial diggings one of the gang,
who sells the joint stealings as gold won by himself.

Fifczgibbon burst into loyal and spirited
verse to welcome the unoffending Yorks. Here
is the first slab of his “ Tribute” :
Son of the king—of old England’s king,
So lovingly long in our hearts enshrined,
That even yet it is when we sing
“ God save him ” that it recalls to mind
That prince before he is prince no more
By our gracious King and the Emperor.
The regrettable thing is that, in order to get the
rhyme quite right in that last line, he has to be
called the Empty Roar or something of the kind.
To those who know the feelings of the
men, the surprise of the recent Melb. demonstra-
tions is that none of the sailors of the visiting
warships came into collision with more or less loss
of life. The American cruiser lay exactly oppo-
site the big Russian boat on Port Melb. pier, and
the men loathed each other with the intensity of
two women friends. The Russians glared at the
Yanks, and in turn the Yanks loudly related tales
of Russian brutality in China, and asserted that
while the Muscovites were always willing to
bayonet Chinese babies, they were invariably in
the rear division when armed Boxers were encoun-
tered. The seamen with the twang further talked
with much disrespect of the immense Russian
ship, claiming that their own armament of fewer
but longer-ranged guns could blow the Gromoboi
out of the water at seven miles. Had the war-
ships performed any evolutions on the night of the
naval display, and had the control of the ships
been left to the crews, either the Brooklyn or the
Gromoboi would almost certainly have been acci-
dentally rammed and sunk. The Russian sailors
are, unquestionably, the biggest and most powerful
though most clumsily-built of all blue jackets, but
they have faces which stamp them ethnologically
as of an extremely low type. They were given to
drinking heavily while in Melbourne. How it was
that Bourke street or the Port Melb. pier did not
run red with gore is one of those matters that
nobody oan explain.
BOARDING-HOUSE EUCLID.
DEFINITIONS AND AXIOMS.
All boarding-houses are the same boarding-house.
Boarders in the same boarding-house and on the same
flat are equal to one another.
A single room is that which has no parts and no magni-
tude.
The landlady 61 the boarding-house is a parallelogram—
that is, an oblong, angular figure which cannot be de-
scribed, but which is equal to anything.
A wrangle is the disinclination of two boarders to each
other, that meet together but are not on the same floor.
All the other rooms being taken, a single room is said
to be a double room.
POSTULATES AND PROPOSITIONS.
A pie may be produced any number of times.
The landlady can be reduced to her lowest terms by a
series of propositions.
A bee-line may be made from any boarding-house to
any other boarding-house.
The clothes of a boarding-house bed, though produced
ever so far both ways, will not meet.
Any two meals at a boarding-house are together less
than two square feeds.
On the same bill and on the same side of it there should
not be two charges for the same thing.
If there be two boarders on the same floor, and the
amount of side of the one be equal to the amount of sidp
of the other, and the wrangle between the one boarder
and the landlady be equal to the wrangle between the
landlady and the other boarder, then shall the weekly
bills of the two boarders be equal. For if not, let one
bill be the greater : then the other bill is less than it
might have been, which is absurd.
"Re that Flemington famine —why did the
authorities hand over all the guzzles to one
caterer? Isn’t competition the soul of business?
’Tis said that What’s-his-name is the only local
caterer capable of ‘ coping with the multitudes.’
But does he cope with them successfully ? What
price his Cup arrangements ? An acquaintance of
scribe’s pitched a yarn at the Review with some
waiters, who had struck work because, so they
said, their clothes were being torn off their backs
by famine victims. When asked, as experts, to
apportion the blame of the fiasco fairly, they
exonerated ‘ the public.’ ‘ The public,’ they said,
‘ behaved all right till it saw itself diddled out of
its grub and invited to lunch on empty bottles.
Then it turned nasty and rushed the starvation
show.’
“An advertising doctor and his clan behaved
in true poster-family style throughout the revels.
A carriage and pair, flunkeys and all, were much
in evidence, also Madame’s expensive black
pelisse, and her two sons, rigged up in Etonian
top-hats, collars, jackets, gloves, etc. For their
short-skirted, long-haired daughter, the proud
parents engineered an introduction to Royalty,
encouraged by the fact that, eight years ago, the
same kiddie presented a bouquet to the Duchess
at the opening of the Royal Imperial Institute.
Little Miss What’s-her-name, egged on by the
ambition of her parents, sports a Royal Academy
of Music gold medal, and spends most of her
young life at the piano.
“Nellie Stewart’s voice rose to the occasion in
a quite wonderful way at the Commonwealth
Concert, on May 9th. She had to sing the first
solo of the evening, a ‘ Hymn ’ com osed by
Kenningham, under the awfully exacting title of
‘ Australia,’ and some 11,500 people were waiting
to hear what she would make of it. Another 1500,
or so, had by that time established themselves
firmly in front of the refreshment tables, and took
no interest in the concert, but Nellie’s ordeal was
made all the harder by the distant clattering
and cork-popping of these early feeders. The
plucky little woman sang with twice her normal
strength and sweetness. Granting that the music
was arranged to suit the vocalist, the performance
will stand as Nellie Stewart’s record in the
opinion of thousands of hearers.
“ After Nellie’s triumphant hymn came an
operatic quartette by Madame Slapoffski, Janson,
Pringle and Salvi. This was chiefly notable for
the intense loyalty of Salvi, who sang his part
with both eyes fixed on the Jook and Juokess, and
bowed to them personally bn the slightest pro-
vocation.
“ Ella Rufesell’s diamonds at the State sing-song
had a distinctive glitter even after those of the
Duchess, which had made everyone sit back and
hold tight. The Russell’s Count was in the
neighborhood, listening to his beloved wife’s sing-
ing with bated breath.
“ On the eve of the Royal departure, when the
Duke broke the seals of his box and scat-
tered little bits of sugar to the birds, Barton
got a bediamonded gold snuff-box ; Peacock, auto-
graph photos; M‘Culloeh, gold cigarette case;
Jenkins, gold pencil case ; Colonel Hoad, silver
cigarette case. But what about those knight-
hoods ? From title to trinket seems as big a fall
as that of the disappointed small boy who com-
plained bitterly that when he prayed for a tricycle
God bluffed him off with a new baby-sister.
“The departure of the Jook and co., on Satur-
day, from Govt. House, was more like a dusty
circus than a sad farewelling. A small crowd
wedged itself in between the barriers at Govt.
House gates. A solitary trooper made his horse
play-up whenever that minute portion of the
public looked a bit surgy, which it did
whenever the barriers were taken down to
let a tram roll by. The troops lining the
route, rather tired of waiting, leaned against
the Venetian masts for support. The wind was
north and knifey, and the band seemed bad-tem-
pered. The man in leopard-skin pinafore with
the big drum banged it viciously. A few coaches
drove out. The suite were trotted out in assorted
lots, and then the Jook and Duch., looking very
solemn and unsmileful. She wore a tiny hat —the
big picture toque is doomed in loyal Melb. from
this out, for the Duchess has worn her headgear
small all the time.
“There was a big double wedding at Kew
t’other day, when Softgoods Beath parted with
two of his pretty daughters at once.
“ I was in a show-room, waiting to be ‘tried
on.’ Scraps of talk buzzed over the low walls of a
street of fitting-rooms, and on all sides victims, in
screened-off corners, underwent operation by per-
sons with pins in their mouths, or consulted rever-
ently with toilet-oracles. Across one partition
floated the fruity tones of a blarneying Irish voice
wheedling a customer —a portly foreign vocaliste.
* Alter your purple satin, madame ? Sure,
wouldn’t you fancy a lovely black costume ? One
of those darling sequin-nets with masses of soft
chiffon on the corsage ? Black would suit your
beautiful white skin to perfection ! ’ ‘ One thinks
not of skins when one is no more young,’ replied
another voice. ‘ Nowadays a beaudiful bit saved
bleases bedder mine fat old heart ! ’
“Here my attention wandered to two lively
chatterboxes standing just outside the half-open
door of my retreat. ‘ When the invitations came,’
said one ‘ I was desperate. Not a rag to wear and
not a penny to my name. I thought of putting
on my best things, hat, gloves and all, and jump-
ing into a vat of black dye. Then Mother got my
black tailor-made cleaned, and I worked a lot of
point lace and mounted it on ivory-satin for
cravat, re vers and sailor collar. We rigged
up a black chiffon hour-glass muff with a
posy of scented white violets, and took an idea
for a home-made hat from Chiffon’s Paris show.
A white corduroy crown buttons with chic white
loops and buttons on to a black broadcloth brim,
turned up in front with black chiffon, white
violets and a buckle. Good enough ! For my
part I love the Duchess for setting the fashion of
velvet cloaks and ostrich-edging. Granny’s im-
ported mantle will be a perfect God-send to me,
and will pass for the new Victoria-Mary wrap.’
“ A few weeks ago this apparently doomed city
was threatened with a strike of slaveys. They
seemed to be holding the place up for a time,
asked what wages they liked, did exactly as they
liked, or else —no help in the kitchen at the Cele-
bration time. The missuses at first trembled and
gave in, but muttered, revengefully, ‘Wait till
after the Celebrations, and then— ’ But the
strike killed itself. The girls didn’t want to leave
Melbourne and miss the Celebrations. If they
resigned, and stayed in Melbourne, they could
only get board at famine prices. Their own aunts
and cousins scorned to take them in ; they wanted
to let the spare bedroom at two guineas a night
to a premier or a knight. And, on top of all,
country girls came pouring in and were willing to
wash the dishes at reduced wages if let out on
Celebration day.
“Out of the Celebrations have blossomed, among
others, two very intsresting engagements. One
is that of Miss Bertha Pratt, one of the most
striking frockers in Melbourne, and Mr. Lindsay
Ingles, brother of the late Mrs. Springthorpe.
Mr. Ingles has returned ‘ after the ball ’ in
S’Africa with on interesting limp. The second
engagement is that of Miss Winny Wollaston,
daughter of the learned Dr. of the Customs, to
Mr. Lockyer, Collector of Customs, in Sydney.
Mr. Lockyer, widower, is a keen horticulturist.
So the future bride must make up her mind to
take an interest in slugs and snails, and codlin
moths, and things.
“Big crowds sped the parting of Musgrove’s
opera company. Last night of the ‘ Flying Dutch-
man’ S’Malkum M’Eacharn brought a party of
ladies, and S’Rupert Clarke, as usual, occupied a
box. Our only baronet, by the way, is off on his
travels again. There were lots of Federal belles
on view that night—daughters of Senators and
sich—most of them smartly frocked in all-white.
Federal frumps were also in evidence. And I
never saw such a dismal creature as Lawrence
Mooney’s ‘Flying Dutchman.’ Far too plump
and pasty-faced for a perennial mariner, he looked
more like a lay-preacher, fresh from prayer and
meditation in a flour-bin.
“Young Teck, Cornwall’s brother-in-law, is a
magnificently-uniformed, handsome equerry, much
darker than his sister, but with her very blue eyes
and shortish nose. Their late father, in his youth,
was a charming and penniless Prince, whose looks
won for him the hand of Mary of Cambridge,
years older than himself. Queen Victoria favored
the match because she had an eye for beauty, and
had herself yielded to the wooing of pretty prince-
ling Albert, some years her junior. In later years
she encouraged Beauty Batten burg to reach out
for the hand of her daughter Beatrice. In this
case again the lady was her suitor’s senior.
Prince Teck seems all the more dashing, no doubt,
because of his cocked hat, martial cloak, glove-fit
breeks, and knee-high riding boots. Still, Vis-
count Crichton, a similarly plumaged equerry,
plus spectacles, looks thin-lipped and cross, not-
withstanding his fine feathers.
“ A well-known Sydney municipal lady told
Melbourne friends that she is to be presented to
their Highnesses in Sydney. ‘ Are you going to
practise your curtsey ? ’ asked someone. She gave
a smile. ‘That sort of thing comes to one with
the occasion,’ she replied quite quietly. It does.
At least twenty-seven different varieties of excited
bobbing-up-and-down came to the dames of Mel-
bourne on the occasion, and the effect will take a
great deal of forgetting.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.


A circular just put forth by the Presby-
terian Church shows that its missionary subscrip-
tions, in Australia, amount to 8d. per year for
each person.
What pleasant companions the returned
contingenters will be —so autobiographical—and
so modest. How one writes to an up-country
N.S.W. paper:—
“ We’re just as good as little tin gods here, especially
those of us who have been knocked out by a bullet. The
boys suffering from fever and debility are treated with
the greatest kindness, but they do not boom so largely as
heroes as the man does who had a mauser slug through
his napper. My wound is in the leg and I can’t make
much out of it as an exhibit, but it makes me interesting
all the same. Whisper, I’ve had three proposals ! Sounds
disgustingly vain, doesn’t it? but ’pon my oath it’s true.
One nice little English girl put it this way : “ You’re
going to be ill a long time,” she said, “and you want
somebody to look after you. I’m single.” That was all,
I said: ‘ That’s very good, but I’m fixed out there in
Australia.’”
“ Old Soldier ” to The Bulletin :
As to the idea that the failure of recruiting in
Britain will cause the British Govt, to employ black
troops in S’Africa. It would he a terrible risk. In the
18th century, when Britain and France were struggling
in the names of various native chiefs for the lead in India,
the British and French troops were only auxiliaries with
the armies of the chiefs aforesaid. The repeated mutinies
of Indian native troops show how little they are to be
trusted, and for years after the organisation of the West
India (negro) regiments they were in a state of inter-
mittent mutiny. The black soldier is nominally 7 cheaper
than the white ; but when y r ou consider the cost of the
white soldiers needed to keep the former straight, and
the carefully picked officers required to manage him, the
cheapness disappears. Also when real hard fighting is to
be done the dependence is on the white man alone, and
he must be saved for that at any cost. As Haidar Ali
said, when he saw the British troops fight—■“ If I had
such soldiers, I would carry them in litters, and never ask
them to do anything but fight.”

Government Notices.
The Treasury, New South Wales,
Sydney, 2nd May, 1901.
TREASURY BILLS ACT OF 1899.
SPENDERS will be received up to and not later than 4th
J-• June, 1901, for the purchase of £500,000 worth of
Treasury Bills, to be issued under the authority of the
Treasury Bills Act of 1899, which provides that, for the
purpose of carrying out certain Public Works, the raising
of a sum not exceeding Four Millions sterling is
sanctioned. ...
8 Such bills to the amount mentioned above, viz
£500,000, will have a currency of five years, and will bear
interest at the rate of 3J per centum per annum, payable
half-yearly, said interest to accrue from Ist June, 1901,
and be repayable at the Bank of New South Wales,
Sydney, at par.
'A deposit of 10 per cent, on the face value of Bills
tendered for in Sydney, Bank drafts payable on demand,
cheques on Sydney endorsed by Banks, or Sydney Money
Orders, must accompany each Tender, and the balance of
accepted Tenders will be payable at The Treasury, Sydney
within three days of date of allotment.
Bills will be issued of the following denominations, viz.,
£lOO £5OO, £lOOO, £5OOO, and where possible will be
allotted in the values applied for by successful Tenderers,
but the Treasurer will not in any instance guarantee
division of values to Tenderers.
Applications will be receivable for sums of £lOO (one
hundred pounds) and upwards. . , .
Forms of Tender and all particulars can be obtained at
The Treasury, Sydney, on or after 4th May, 1901.
Tenders to be addressed “Under Secretary for Finance
and Trade,” and endorsed “ Tender for Treasury Bills.
The highest or any Tender not necessarily accepted
2a i2 T. WADDELL.

for instance, whose fame i= universally known—class
themselves as infants in scientific invention and research,
nd modestly tell us that they have but a smattering of
■ w edge of the possibilities, extent, and development
We have only to look at the fine buildings that occupy
the corner of Elizabeth and Bathurst streets to gain some
idea of the extent and importance of their professional
work But to have the privilege of being shown through
the buildin<>- by the Specialists themselves, and to see and
learn something of the real character of their wide prac-
t'ee, is a treat that only comes the way of a fortunate in-
dividual like the writer.
The instrument-room was, as usual, one of particular
curiosity*- Laymen always have a penchant for ei eing
and examining the polished~steel articles with which so
many extraordinary acts are performed upon the human
body. The collection in this room is in every way up-to-
date. Whenever a new instrument conies to the front,
and its worth is proved beyond question, it is added to
this rare assortment. Another room full of absorbing in-
terest is that devoted to the galvanic batteries, of which
there are several magnificent kinds. The noted Gailfe
batteries, the finest French makes, are here in perfection,
irrespective of their high cost. If any article Or mechani-
cal and electric contrivance is found necessary in main-
taining the fame of this institution, the price is never
allowed to stand in the way.
The correspondence department is a veritable eye-
opener. Its sacredness is a bar to a stranger’s admission,
but a full view of the work going on is had through the
glass doors. Half a-dozen typists are seen driving their
machine-writers at electric speed. To the principals only
are known the names of the persons throughout Australia
who are being treated as skilfully in their homes about
the metropolis and away in the interior as if they were
present in personal consultation at the institution.
The packing-rooms are likewise shut off from curious
and prying glances, and the work of one department is a
mystery to the others. No better system for holding
secret the nature of sufferers’ troubles, for safe-guarding
their interests, and giving due heed to the feelings and
sensitiveness of patients, could be imagined. Packages
are not plastered with advertising labels, they are put up
in such a way that only the names and addresses are in
' 1 What a practice is here revealed and what a consola-
tion must it be to these eminent specialists to know that
they are permanently curing and restoring to hfa’th and
happiness thousands of people whose lives were a burden
to them. The young, the middle-aged, and the healthy
are treated with the same amount of success. The pro-
digious nature of the work accomplished by these
specialists necessitates —not the stereotyped morning and
evening two hours’ consultation ticket, but their constant
attention at the institution from early morning till late
into the night. “ I can assure you,” said Dr. Wallace.
“ we don’t put in these long hours because we are fondo-
'»

of electricity, and only a fringe of imagination of the un-
told stores of strength and wealth wrapt up in this
phenomenal power. . „ ~
Therefore it requires no argument to convince the
most pronounced of optimists that the field in any one ol
the great and honored professions of the day is far too
vast for the thorough comprehension of the most per-
fectly brain-endowed man, or body of naturally-gifted
men. Yet in respect of the most delicate and difficult of
all professions-that of prescribing for the ills and
diseases of mankind—we find doctors, scattered over the
globe, offhandedly and without careful, minute, and
searching examination and diagnosis—without any know-
ledge of the past lives of patients—flippantly dashing off
prescriptions for every class of complaint, as if a cure was
as simple as falling over the proverbial log.
Thanks to the march of civilisation, culture, and edu-
cation, people are getting to know that modern medical
men who are worth consulting confine their practice to a
certain class of cases. Realising the utter hopelessness of
mastering and curing everything under the sun, and find-
ing that “ gullibles ” are not so plentiful as they used to
be, the wise and progressive men of medicine devote
themselves to one or other class of cases, and work out
the remedies for them to the best of their skill and
better and more striking illustration of the suc-
cess of Specialists in Skin Diseases, Heart, Kidney, Liver
Affections, Rheumatics, Sciatica, Nervous Diseases, could
the people of this magnificent continent, Australia, have
than the enormous practice of the famous practitioners of
the Electro-Medical Institute. Their skill, attainments,
position, and renowned reputation stamp them as the un-
doubted masters of the classes of diseases and complaints
which have been the study of their professional lives.
When we speak of electricity we think of Edison. Why ?
Because he dwarfs other men in this branch of science.
In the same way, when we talk of specialists, we at once
associate the names of the Freeman and Wallace Insti-
tute. So it is in every branch of scientific medicine;
some one or two men stand head and shoulders above
their fellows. .
It is the aim, object, and devotion of certain doctors to
be pre-eminent in some defined class of disease rather
than to tinker with a host of complaints about which
they can have but a hazy notion, and superficial know-
ledge. To use a hackneyed but expressive phrase, they
are “ Jacks of all trades and masters of none.” Never
since the time of Ricord, the noted French Specialist,
have any men conducted so gigantic and successful a
practice" as that conducted by the Freeman-Wallace
Electro Medical and Surgical Institute.
of hard work than any other medical men ; we do it in
the best interests of sufferers. We don’t keep them
waiting longer than we can help, and we suffer many in-
conveniences for our patients’ sakes.”

One Boer war entertainment now progressing through
the Big Scrub is run by someone with a fine eye to the
fitness of things. The lecture is broken up into sections
with appropriate music in between. Thus, after “ The
Siege of Ladysmith,” someone sang “ Break the News to
Mother,” and after a gory tale about bayonet-charges and
disembowelment came the gentle little hymn, “ Lead,
Kindly Light.”
In “Florodora” comedian Lauri burlesques the
miserere scene from “II Trovatore.” It astonished a
number of musical people when they heard him negotiate
one or two still passages with comparative ease, the
Passages that make the average tenor hold his chest with
oth hands. No doubt Lauri doesn’t care a banana
whether he breaks up or down, therefore he doesn’t
break. Fear of mishap rattles most tenors when they are
on the up-grade in a song. Lots of singers say that the
moment before taking a high note their past sins
flash before them, and they live through all the
wickedness of their lives. One famous Australian Don
Caesar admitted that he could never approach G or
A without seeing a pale gray line of creditors rise up
from the audience. Another said that whenever he
attempted a high note he became dizzy with the thought
that his legs were misbehaving themselves—trying to
form the letter V while he was stretching his neck to-
wards the flies. A man’s voice was no good, he said,
unless he owned a pair of cast-iron knees—the man who
didn’t consider the appearance of his knees when singing
soon got shunted among the old men and the thin girls at
the back of the chorus.
What may happen to a new-chum German tenor in
Australia. His story, as told by himself :—“ No, I vas
never mit Villiamson. I went indo de goundry mit an
Engleesh manager. I did sing at Narrabri. Und de Irish
beobles dey shoudt for anoder song. I did sing ‘ Der
dear leedle Shamboch.’und de Oder beobles joomp up und
gall me a planky Boer. Dot vos bad for me. Der Eng-
leesh manager he go vay midout me, Fritz. Und I had
no money because I sent it to my moder, a month pefore,
in Berlin. Und I dravel und dravel along der bush, und
I meet a man mit a bag house. * Meinheer,’ I say, * I
pray you direct me to Sydney ’; und he say, ‘ Oh, hell !
you nevar get dere 1 ’ After dot he would shpeak no
more. Und I dravel und dravel till I koom to Boggabri,
where I meet Peter Schneider, und he gif me three
vhiskies. Den der bolicemen ged me und throw me indo
der logub. Und ven I vas asleeb in mein cell he koom und
vake me up. ‘ You ged up and sing der ‘ Dear Leetle
Shamboeh,’ he say, ‘or I break your planky nose.’ Und
I sit up chatterin’ mit cold und sing, while he sit outside
mein cell mit a beer can. Ven I fall asleep he hit me mit
der can, und make me sing. Und ven it vas almost day
a bulltog koom indo der logub, und der boliceman called
it Joe. He says, ‘Joe, go in der und bite dot blanky
Boer.’ No, I vas nod drunk. Und der bulltog Joe koom
indo mein cell und bite mein leg. Ven de boliceman let
me go I dravel und dravel to Sydney. Und ven I try to
Bing at der concert I did get Joe der bulltog on rnein
mind, und ven I koom to der top nod I shbleet right up.
Und dey hiss und hiss until Igo off der stage. Now der
managers vill not engage Fritz because dey say he haf a
cow in his top nod.”— Alba D.
