“Melb.”: Ella Russell’s voice is almost exactly like
Madame Boema’s, at St. Patrick’s. But Boema sings in
white hair and broad-brimmed gold specs.
A time-saving circus struck Gladstone (Queensland)
some little time back at 9 o’clock p.m. Got the tent up
by midnight, and opened balf-an-hour later to a packed
house; played till 4 a.m.—prices, 10s. and ss. Then off
again by 9.30 a.m.
Bernard Shaw’s latest, “ The Man of Destiny” (one-act),
represents Napoleon at his Republican General period.
An unblushing beauty beards him in a secluded corner
and dares him to extract important documents from the
bosom of her not-entirely-cut-away dress. The entranced
General looks up at his star. “ Don’t you know what the
English say ? ” she asks—“ that no man’s star is'complete
without a woman’s garter.” “Ah! ” he replies. “ What
would the English say if the French had said that?”
After a pause, Napoleon, referring to a recent hint as to
his wife’s faithlessness, remarks, “ Caesar’s wife is above
suspicion.” Whereupon the lady, drawing nearer, says,
“I wonder whether Caesar’s wife would be above suspicion
if she saw us here together?” “I wonder,” he replies,
gazing adoringly upon her as the curtain falls.
“S.S.”: Criticism of the literary side of the drama is
quite extinct in Australia, if it ever existed, and the critic
only enthuses when the crackling of a few hand-made
epigrams startles him into the conviction that he is in the
presence of literature. According to one or two notice
writers for the Melbourne dailies, Ibsen’s dialogue in
“ Lady Inger ” is “ turgid.” If there is any fault of which
Ibsen’s dialogue has not even a semblance it is turgidness.
Speeches are often long, but their literary quality is
brittle and keen. The story reads beautifully. If length
means turgidness, what of Shakespeare, whose historical
plays when presented to his own countrymen, are shame-
fully cut and bovvdlerised to meet a debauched dramatic
taste?
The names of Williamson’s Grand Opera people are
going to get Australia down and worry her. Signora
Lena di Benedetto, Signora Edwige Faccari, Signori Com-
mendatove Vincenzo Larizza, Fernando Cattadori, Ignazio
Bozzoli, Baldo Travaglini, Evandro Cannonieri, and Olinto
Lombardi. Australia has sampled basso Travaglini before,
but the others are new, and until the popular tongue has
had practice, the co. cannot expect to be anything more
than a mispronounced success.
Mark Twain’s daughter, Miss Clara L. Clemens, has
gone on the concert platform. She has been studying
under Mme. Blanche Marchesi, and, in an interview with
a representative of Leslie’s Weekly, she says that Mar-
chesi mfrre looked upon Amy Castles as by far her most
promising pupil up to the time when the wilful little
Bendigo girl sought a new teacher.
The Commonwealth Concert at Melbourne Exhibition
Building brought tears into the eyes of some of the press-
men present. The committee had thoughtlessly given
them a raised space under the gallery intersected by the
jookal entrance-way, and from one of these press boxes a
desperate reporter might have laid violent hands upon
the Duchess’s diamond crown as she passed to or from
the concert. The sparkling bauble was an awful tempta-
tion. It sat so lightly and invitingly on her bonnie brown
hair. With the aid of a pair of shears or a sharp knife it
might have been lifted from its moorings before the
Duchess could feel the loss.
George Musgrove told his testimonializers at Melbourne
Princess’s, the other night, that the success of his grand
opera season had changed all his plans for the future.
“ I have got a house in London, and I’m going home—to
sell it,” said Mussy. A few weeks ago he talked in a very
different tone about London. That was just after he had
received a cablegram stating that his latest production
at the Shaftesbury Theatre was a success—“ seats booked
for six months in advance,” and all that sort of thing.
G.M.’s new enthusiasm for dear old Australia must be
taken with several grains of salt.
The aid of an intelligent stage-manager was badly
wanted in connection with the public appearances of the
Duke at all Melb. ceremonials. Nobody had sense enough
to take His Highness’s lowness into consideration, and
consequently the plentiful lack of royal inches was made
shockingly apparent. The big, wide, but low chair pro-
vided for him at the opening function was a lamentable
mistake from a show point of view. The writer heard
Bland Holt groan aloud when the Heir sat in it, and was
practically swallowed up and lost. The chair was de-
signed to fit Big John from out of the west. It converted
the Dukelet into a manikin, and served to rob royalty of
much of that respect which it does not deserve. At the
children’s fete the same mistake was made, and the
coming King was planked into a chair that left his head
standing jukt over the balustrade, like that of a
marionette in a box, whilst his wife and other dignitaries
sat bust high above the bar. It is to be hoped for the
sake of “ those bonds ” Sydney personages will learn
from their Melb. experiences, that to stage royalty to
advantage the services of a theatrical expert are abso-
lutely necessary.
Theatrical managers should have a care when making
changes in the cast. The placing of Albert Norman, the
long-standing villain, as heroin “The World” at Melb.

Nellie Farren’s son, J. Farren Soutar, writes trifles for
the stage, like his father before him. Bob Soutar had
Duiit the books of many provincial pantomimes before he
married the 16-year-old Nellie Farren, whose three
children by her middle-aged husband were all born in her
Springtime of life. Hence the neatness of Nellie’s figure
in her early grand-motherage.
In a waxworks show at a N.S.W. country town the
figures were arranged on shelves around the hall, and
when the lights were turned down to show some cine-
matograph pictures, some wag lifted Deeming down and
placed him on a chair next that of a half-drunken navvy.
During the ensuing applause the figure canted over, and,
when the lights were turned up, was leaning affection-
ately on the navvy’s shoulder. The pick-and-shoveller
turned round with a savage : “ yer ! who the ’ell
are yer muggin ? ” But when he caught sight of the
labet— Deeming, Murderer,” around the figure’s neck,
he uttered a loud “ Hurroo ! ” and gaily proceeded to
bash the figure to utter ruin.

Coquelin elevates the Gravedigger into the star part,
acting to Bernhardt’s Hamlet, in America. Once we
saw a gravedigger with a wooden leg, in Brighton (Melb.)
cemetery. It blobbed out of the moist clay with a Ker-
Plunk, like Potter kissing Bellew.
r Ashcroft, the music-hall identity from wnom
Johnny Coleman did not crib the Scarecrow Dance, has
been taking a series of farewell benefits in Dublin, Liver-
pool, Glasgow, and Belfast. A notice of the ben. at Bel-
fast, where the veteran ran the Alhambra for more than
20 years, mentions that he was called upon to sing one
verse of his old “ trade-mark ” song—“ The Solid Man.”
The mistaken idea that Coleman took the Scarecrow ”
from Ashcroft had its origin in Johnny’s happening to
borrow a verse of “ The Solid Man ” aforesaid as an intro-
duction to his humorous dance.

On flag-hoisting day a suburban Mayor treated his
municipality to a biograph entertainment. The lions of
the hour were those Fijian police in reserved front seats.
Soon the host noticed that all his male guests were hold-
ing their noses, while ladies passed round salts, or were
helped out with their faces hidden in their handkerchiefs.
Then a steady stream of citizens started to flow towards
his Worship, all bearing the same story. The hall was
warm, and the Fijians, who had apparently honored the
occasion by extra-special top-dressings of grease, seemed
to be stewing in their own juice, and exhaling blended
odors of fats, old and new. The human nosegays were
promptly transplanted, but the atmosphere kept their
memories greeen, and ever since then the scent of stale
suet awakes in many bosoms a passion of biograph
regrets.
“C.C.” : English mail day at the Sydney
Post Office presents an edifying sight, the office
practically stretches into Martin Place, while
fevered persons are endeavoring to lick and stick
on stamps in the middle of the street. There is a
feeling that if the authorities were to enlarge the
post office premises instead of giving the city a
stale bride-cake appearance with its ornamented
white arches and so forth, they would be doing a
good work. The Duke is here only for a short time,
and an arch or two less does not matter, but the
Sydney citizens will have to post letters for a long
while to come. Besides if the number of struggling
citizens stretches much further into space, they
may knock down one of the Duke’s arches, and
that would be a really serious matter.
Willy Pearson, M.L.C., whose estate of
| Kilmany Park, Sale, was chosen for the Jook’s
shooting party, is a very antithesis of his class.
A capitalist of the most aggravated description,
and one of the biggest landholders in Vic., he
lives very quietly, dodges all ostentation, and is
understood to be not over-thankful to Lieut.-Gov.
Madden for breaking in on his repose with an
Heir-Apparent. Pearson is extremely popular
in his own district; still, people long for the old
times when his father ran Commotion and other
monarehs of the turf. The present head .of the
family takes so little interest in sport that he has
abolished the old racing stable and he has so
little desire to kill anything that he didn’t go
out and help the overloaded York to hold up his
gun.
“Mr. A. Saunders, the well known jeweller
of George-street, opposite the Redfern Terminus,
is always to the front where novelty and originality
are in demand. I have had an opportunity of
seeing his latest design in pretty brooches. The
accompanying illustration gives a faint idea of
what he calls his ‘ Federal Brooch.’ In the
precious metal and choice enamel it is one of the
A Pretty anti Clleotitre Broody.
prettiest souvenirs of a great event I have seen
for some time. A scroll inscribed ‘One Land
One People,’ is surmounted by the Federal
shield ; depending from each end of the scroll is a
gold loop chain ; while underneath all is a heart.
What more appropriate gift could be made in the
form of a brooch during the coming festivities
than this ? The price of the brooch in silk-lined
casket is 22s. 6d. post free.”—“ Mab,” in Fkee-
man’s Journal.


Like a Decrepit and Infirm
Old Woman.”
A WONDERFUL RESUSCITATION.
The Case of Mrs. M. H. EDMONDS.
(BY A LOCAL REPORTER.)
“Will you kin illy oblige by giving me
some particulars regarding an extraordi-
nary statement which was lately made in
my presence ? ” The above question was
put by our reporter to Mrs. Margaret
Helen Edmonds, of 11 North-street,
Welli gton.
“ Well, said Mrs. Edmonds, “ there are
so many sensational rumours going about
now-a-days that do not concern me at all,
but if you will tell me upon what subject
you are seeking information I will gladly
accede to your request if it lies in my
power.”
* I desire to know the nature of the com-
plaint from which I hear you recently
suffered.”
“ Oh, yes ; but you will tire of standing
if I tell you in detail, so please take a seat.
My terrible sufferings started with an
attack of acute rheumatism, which first
showed itself by terrific pains in the knee
joints. Before this distinct and awful
agony appeared I felt sensations of chilliness
running through my body for a couple of
days, and I knew that something was going
wrong. I also perspired very freely, my
skin being very hot. On the second night
after the 'commencement of these symptoms
I was attacked with frightfully severe pains
in my back. My muscles became so stiff
and ached so much that I was afraid to
■tir, as whenever I moved I experienced the
greatest agony imaginable. Indeed, the
slightest movement increased the severity
of the tortures so greatly that I was
obliged to lie in a perfectly helpless con-
dition for hours. I was frightened to death
at the way my pulse was beating. € The
blood was fairly bounding through my
veins, and I had attained a very high tem-
perature. It was only with great diffi-
culty and at the expense of much suffering
that I sometimes managed to hobble about
the house like a decrepit and infirm old
woman. My hip joints were also affected
by the torture that was daily drawing the
life out of me. 1 felt feverish and thirsty,
and my tongue was quite white. The
rheumatism in time spread all over my
body. There was not a limb or a muscle
which escaped the clutches of my dreadful
disease. I got so helpless that I could not
stand on my feet, and when I tried to do
so my legs gave way beneath me. My
limbs were just as useless as though I were
paralysed.”
“ Of course, you we e under a doctor ? ”
“ Certainly I was ; but his treatment was
without avail. Every day and every week
I sank lower. My doctor at last recom-
mended my removal to the hospital, but I
strongly objected, as I have always looked
upon those institutions with detestation and
horror. Howe er a week or so later my
medical adviser told me that my system
was completely broken down and that if I
did no* them to take me to the
hospital J was sure to die. He gave me to
understand that I was near death’s door, and
that my only chance of pulling through was
to follow his advice. I became greatly
tlarmed, as I really felt more dead than
alive, and I consented to go the hospital,
where I remained fora month. I cannot
speak too highly of the kindness and at-
tention shown to me whilst in the institu-
tion. Nobody could be better treated in re-
gard to the painstaking efforts of the
doctors ; but still they did not succeed in
improving my pitiful condition. I became
impatient, and 1* ft the hospital against the
doctor’s will.”
“ It is a wonder you were able to leave
after so much suffering.”
“ You’re right. It took me all my time.
I was so weak that I frequently fainted
away without doing anything calculated to
produce exhaustion. I was unable to do
anything, yet I was always languid and
fatigued. My nerves were in a shocking
state. A knock at the door, or, in fact,
any unexpected sound, set me trembling
like a leaf, and when darkness came on I
was in a state of nervous terror till daylight
appeared. When I closed my eyes in the
hope of falling asleep I had ugly images
forming themselves before my imaginative
vision. Fearful thoughts of coming troubles
continually haunted me, and as I could
never get to sleep to escape my mental
tortu'es I was almost driven crazy.”
“ What kind of diet were you taking ?”
“ Only the very lightest of invalid’s foods,
and very little of that. You can scarcely
credit the awfnl pains I felt in my stomach
after taking food, and behind my shoulders
a feeling was ever present, resembling the
stabbing of a knife. My chest felt as if it
was loaded with lead, and the oppi essive
sensation was always accompanied with
great difficulty in breathing. Windy
spasms came °n very often, and besides
causing no end of violent pains in my
stomach my heart was set palpitating to
such an extent that I thought the rapid
beating would only cease when my heart
stopped for ever. My weight was reduced
to about one-half what it used to be before
my i lness. Headaches were everlastingly
tormenting me, making me despondent and
miserable to such a degree that 1 yearned
for death to release me from my every-day
afflictions. Every morning, on arising from
bed, I was attacked with fits of retching
and vomiting. The disease had obtained
such a hold upon me that I despaired of
ever getting better, and I sank to the most
dir ful depths of misery. When the
doctors failed to cure me I tried many
advertised remedies, but they made me
worse, if possible, than before taking
them.”
“ But you ultimately regained your
health ? ” „ , „ A T
“Of course I did, thank God ! But lam
sure I would never have got well had it not
been for Clements Tonic. A friend of mine
called to see me one day, little thinking t>>
find me so near my grave. After telling her
all about my long months of suffering she
told me she knew of a medicine which
would certainh cure me. I listened with
a feeling of indifference, as I thought there
waß not a medicine in the world which I had
not given a trial. I did not even trouble to
inquire what the medicine was called, so
my friend, apparently disappointed at my
evident callousness, volunteered the infor-
mation that the physic she had so much
confidence in was called Clements Tonic..
“ Yes, and did you give Clements Tonic a
trial ? ” .
“ I am overjoyed at having done so,
though it took a deal of persuasion, as 1
had given up all hope. I sent for one
bottle of Clements Tonic to start with, and
I derived so much benefit from it that I
purchased more. My appetite returned
after several doses, and I was soon able to
eat solid food with a relish. Ihe pains in
my stomach, back, and head quickly
vanished, and my food was no longer
vomited, but digested instead, and did me
good. My nerves were toned and stimu-
lated by C lements Tonic, which also brought
peaceful sleep. The rh umatic troubles
were daily decreased and finally removed
by Clements Tonic, my lost vigour and
strength meanwhile being restored. In
several weeks I was strong, active, and
happy, for which I am under a life-long
obligation to ClementsT< nic, and my health
has been splendid since. I would not be
without Clements Tonic in my house for
the world.” . . ~ „
“ May I have your statements printed .
“ Decidedly; publish these facts in any
way you deem fit.”

Rev. J. Done, of All Saints 0.E.,
Parramatta, being requested by anti-ritualists to
stop his choir from wearing surplices, replied that
the choir would resign if not allowed to wear sur-
plices. Great is the attraction of millinery.
From king to choir-boy, the desire to flash in fan-
tastic attire is overmastering. Soratoh us, and
we are savag93 still.
Dear Bulletin,— l should like to call the
attention of N.S.W. Public Instruction Depart-
ment to lines 9 and 10, page 40, in the Fourth
Book used in the public schools. I have had a
class of boys and girls come to a dead stop at the
passage referred to. Might not matter so much
with city children, who are not supposed to be
acquainted with these things, but the bush
youngster knows all about them ; and, as teachers
are supposed to explain the lesson in detail, the
passage in question is very embarrassing to ex-
pound to a mixed class of 16 or 17 year-olds
Teacher.
Letter recently received by Sydney liquor-
firm :
Jerez de la Frontira,
_. „ 11th October, 1900.
Sir Messrs. , Sydney.
Gentlemen, —
In attention to the next festivity we beg to address you
the present wishing you a nice Christmas and happy new
year.
At the same time we profit of this oportunity to
acknowledge you that Mister who has been our
representative during seven years in wich time has
renewed the contract twice, having the same recently
vanquished for the thirth time, he has not renewed it
owed perhaps to the considerable augmentetion of his
family and the state of his health broken up with the
climate of that contry.
We invite you to favour us with one of your esteemed
orders, and in attention that having not a traveller our
expenses are not so high, we will ship you best quality
within the conditions of our current prices.
We remain, Dear Sir,
Yours faithfully,

The Station-Master’s Lie.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The station-master at Wurraurra looked at the
clock. “ Well,” he said, with reference to the
train I wanted, “she’s doo now, but she’s stopped
t’ pick up some stock at Reading. Maybe she’ll
be an hour or so late.”
Wurraurra is on the branch to Muekilla, the
line constructed by a thoughtful Minister of Rail-
ways for the benefit of a friend, who otherwise
would have had to drive 40 miles to a station. The
friend has long since given up stock-rearing in
favor of stock-broking in Sydney, and left Muc-
killa to the rabbits, gorse, and briars. Neverthe-
less, the Government continues to run a train
eaoh way once a day. Sometimes there are two
or three passengers; occasionally a truck or so of
goods ; more often neither. In any case, the train
is sent, and when there is a dearth of legitimate
traffic it is opportunely found that empty trucks
require removing.
“Pretty quiet time of it here,” I remarked.
“Yes, in a way,” the station-master agreed.
“’Tisn’t often there’s much doing. Last week a
bullock broke loose.”
“ Any damage ? ”
“No, not to speak of. But he kept the train
two hours, and she missed the connection.”
“ That’s bad,” I suggested.
“Oh ! it don’t matter in the ordinary way,” he
said, reassuringly, “but there happened to be a
passenger that day, and he wrote to the mana-
ger, and then I heard of it. As if I could help it.
They only allow an hour and a half’s margin, and
anybody’s liable to accidents.”
I expressed a hope that none would happen that
day.
‘ ‘ Goodness knows ! ” went on the official, with
cheerful fatalism. “ Our old injin goes off mor’n
she orter, and her driver’s rather peculiar. Any-
way, she won’t be here yet. S’pose you comes
across to the pub ter wait ? ”
The suggestion seemed good. It was not until
I acted upon it that I discovered the dual person-
ality of the landlord and the station-master.
“We’ll have a drop outer my bottle,” he re-
marked, as though conferring a priceless favor.
There is a belief prevalent that a landlord imbues
some otherwise unattainable virtue into whisky
by extending his particular proprietorship over it.
“ That’s whisky, now,” he said, enthusiastically,
scoffing his four-finger measure neat. “Different
from the common stuff, eh ? ”
As a man with a love of the truth amounting to
a positive hobby, I ought to have contradicted
the statement that it was whisky. As it was, I
merely gasped and reached for the water.
“Couldn’t sell that to anybody,” he continued.
I agreed. It seemed to me lie would find diffi-
culty in giving it away.
“As I was saying,” the publican-cum-railway-
official went on, “ the driver of our injin on this
line is a bit peculiar. He’s come down, has Sam.
Once he was driver of the main line express, was
steady, and didn’t lose more time ’n most. I can’t
say it’s any fault of his own either.”
“ How did it happen ? ”
“ Well, I believe I know the rights of the yarn.
Sam, you must understand, has the most wunner-
ful eyesight on earth. Try a drop more, sir ? ”
“ Er—l think I’ll have beer.”
“The most wunnerful eyesight ” —he paused be-
cause of a recalcitrant beer-cork.
“Surely that’s a good thing for an engine-
driver,” 1 remarked.
“Yes, you’d think so. However, Sam’s had a
pretty rough time with that sight of his. Once,
before he joined the railway, he got put in a ’sylum
all ’cause of it. Being able to see all sorts of
things ordinary people couldn’t, they wouldn’t
believe him, which is only natural. Of
course he couldn’t see no further through a pine
plank than anybody else ; but it must have been
a bit awkward for the poor chap seeing all his
tucker and everything through a microscope, as it
were. If you’ve ever seen a drop of water through
a microscope, you can pretty well tell what sort
of time poor Sam had. He wouldn’t go through
a street where there was a store for any money, for
fear there’d be cheese in the window. If he just
set eyes on a piece of cheese he’d be off his feed
for days. He couldn’t even drink beer; it was
like a full-sized aquarium to him. In fact, the
poor chap’s life was a fair burden to him, and al-
though he wore smoked glasses, which nobody else
could see through, it ended in his being put away.
He happened to be a witness in a case in
Melbourne, and he said he had seen the defendant
kissing the girl when he was some miles away.
The old Judge naturally suggested Sam was per-
juring for all he was worth, and that riled Sam, so
he up and told the court he could see the Judge
had a glass eye and seven false teeth. I believe
there was a fair rumpus. Anyway, Sam got
packed-away.”
“How long ago was that?” I asked, the land-
lord having stopped to gaze reflectively at the
flies.
“ Oh, about fifteen year or so. Sam’s getting an
old man now, and he was in the asylum about
seven years. When he came out, they wanted
irijin-drivers, and Sam reckoned he could drive,
and got on. Everything went well for a long
time, and Sam even scored heavily by saving a
smash. It was along the straight run between
Pannoowarrack and Murgulop, and Sam saw a
gum had blown across the track. He was putting
her along at sixty to the hour, and no ordinary
man could have seen that tree in time to pull up.
But Sam did, and they made a big fuss of him, and
put him on to the Government specials.
‘ ‘jHe’s told me since that at this ti me he got used
to eating menageries and drinking aquariums. I
had a week once seeing things crawling about,
and it makes me very sympathetic with Sam.
But, as I was saying, I believe he’d got quite
used to it and was even proud of his extraordinary
sight. Anyway, he’d got on with a girl who lived
on a farm only about a mile from the track, and
anything she wanted to tell Sam she’d write in
chalk on the side of the barn, and Sam’d read it
as he passed.
“You’d think that was harmless enough, but
it brought about Sam’s ruin. Mary Ellen, that
being the girl’s name, got so fond of the game
she’d cover the barn with loving messages, and
Sam had to slow-up in order to give himself time
to read ’em. That made him lose time, which got
him into trouble.
“By-and-by he and Mary Ellen quarrelled.
She turned-out to be a vindictive hussy, and
started in to annoy Sam all she knew by writing
nasty things on the barn. It was hard on Sam to
see insults where he had been accustomed to read
tender yearnings, and it naturally upset him.
Then Mary Ellen froze on to another feller, and
this feller took on the job of annoying Sam. He
must have been good at it. One day Sam read
something particularly riling—the barn had a sort
of fascination for him so that he couldn’t resist
looking.
“‘Bill,’ he remarked to his fireman, ‘I can’t
stand this much longer.’
“ Next day came the climax. Mary Ellen and
her feller waited until the train came in sight.
Then the feller took a big piece of chalk and
wrote ‘ Married To-morrow.’
“‘That settles it,’ said Sam. ‘Brakes, Bill.’
And he shuts-off steam, stops the train, gets
down, and with the coal-hammer on his shoulder
makes for the barn. Of course there was a big
row among the passengers. The guard swore at
the fireman, but he refused to go on without Sam,
and they had to wait until he had smashed the
barn into smithereens and chased the other feller
round the house with the coal-hammer. Presently
he came back and took the train into Buroo.
“He was sacked, of course, because, although
it’s hard to see the girl you’ve come to regard as
vour freehold, jumped before your eyes, he orter
have finished his train, anyway. A month or so
later he was taken on again for this line, because
iniin-drivers were scarcer’n gold then, and here
’ : i been ever since.
No, sir, his eyes seem failing him now. There
was some talk of running him for the Senate, in
the hope he’d be able to see what really would be
best for the country ; but I expect he’ll stay here
until he has a smash-up. He’s what you might
call a broken man. Well, we’ll get across. She’ll
be coming along now soon.”
One cattle-truck, one empty bogie, and a passen-
ger-carriage made up the train.
“Sam,” said the station-master, “here’s a gent
wants to catch the mail. You’d better push her
along.”
“ If I could see anything at the other end,” re-
marked Sam, cautiously, wiping his mouth with a
dab of waste.
I nodded.
“P’raps we might do it,” he conceded.
T. H. BRYANT.
Words About Woman.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Woman is naan’s greatest earthly blessing and the
cause of most of his misery. She is his chief in-
spiration to the achievement of all that is good,
grand, and glorious in this world, and at the same
time a labor-saving device to help him make a fool
of himself. She soothes his tired nerves with the
coo of her gentle voice, but she always has the last
word in every controversy with him—and, inci-
dentally, about 97 per cent, of the preceding con-
versation.
Most of man’s trouble is caused by woman, but
so deftly does she pile the load on him that when-
ever his burden of trouble is lifted he wanders
uneasily about looking for more—otherwise there
would be very few second wives. She will cheer-
fully go to the stake for the truth’s sake, and lie
about her age without even being asked. She
will grow weary of an indulgent husband, but will
cleave unto death to the man who beats her re-
gularly. She will break her heart because a man
does what she doesn’t want him to do, and love
him all the better for so doing.
She scorns all advice in the selection of a hus-
band, but takes two other women to help her to
select a hat. The less actual comfort to be ob-
tained from a thing, the more enjoyment a woman
gets out of its possession.
At 16 she is a young woman; at 25, if still un-
married, she is a girl. She will face the grim
spectre Death without a tremor, and swoon at the
sight of a mouse.
The only time she ever does what you expect
her to do is when you expect her to do just what
you don’t expect her to do. The sole reason why
she does anything is simply because she doesn’t
know why she does it.
She jumps at conclusions, and always lands on
them squarely, for the simple reason that, when
the conclusion slips to one side, thinking to avoid
her, it gets exactly in her way. She is the dearest
thing in all the world, and the most aggravating.
She is as she is, and that’s all there is to do about
it. The only man who ever fully understands a
woman is the man who understands that he
doesn’t understand her, and has sense enough to
let it go at that.
E. D.

Anglo-Indian to The Bulletin :
r> ~ -.r- .. ■ • . , ~ . T j-
Re the Victorian perjury-cases against natives of India
There is a general agreement among the most experienced
Indian officials-such men as Gen. Sleeman, who had more
actual experience of Indian crime than any man before
or since his time-that the natives of India are, on the
whole, just as truthful as other men Henry Lawrence,
Bartle Frere, and Bichard Temple say the same.
Macaulay, whom you quote, is-apart from his notorious
general recklessness of statement-not a reliable autho-
rity on the subject. His Indian experience as Legal Mem-
her of Council did not bring him much into contact with
natives, and he never had a colloquial knowledge
4. an ?u na^, lve l an £ ua & e * I have reason to know
that the trouble arose from bad interpreting A
really efficient interpreter should know at least
half-a-dozen languages, quite as distinct from each other
as French, Italian, and Spanish. The usual interpreter
rarely knows anything but Urdu, which is no one s ver-
nacular, but only a sort of composite camp-language.
A witness, giving evidence through an interpreter in a
language which neither thoroughly understands, may.
with every desire to tell the truth, seem to tell a tissue of
amazing falsehoods, simply because he and the interpreter
are at cross-purposes, and don’t know it. Even the care-
fully trained polyglot European magistrate often finds it
hard to know what is meant by witnesses speaking
local dialects. It is not necessary to call these men
habitual liars to show that they should not and cannot
mix with white men. The colored-labor question lies in
a nutshell, the kernel of which is the mutual repulsion
between white and not-white. The colored man may be
the most estimable person alive, but he and the white are
official hierarchy of white men which rules the country is
hated by the native masses with an intensity of which few
out of India have an adequate idea.

A GRAMOPHONE makes a capital
present. Welcome everywhere and by
everybody. Endless amusement for
the festive season. Pleases your family
and friends. It more than takes the
place of a piano, banjo, mandolin, or
cornet, for you hear not only one, but
all these instruments, and many more,
as they are played, not by amateurs,
but by the most celebrated artists. No
matter how remote your habitation, it
brings within the family circle the
actual voices of orators, singers, come-
dians, and story-tellers, who perhaps at
that very moment are delighting
Metropolitan audiences with the same
eloquence, melody, humor, and dialect
that is coming from the Gramophone
in the quiet of a country home
hundreds of miles away.

such performance, we can secure the
very highest talent, and what costs us

Coasting.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The first-salooners pause to hear
The rolling cranks a-thudding,
And ask the watching engineer
How fast the old tub’s scudding.
They argue on the latest makes
Of cranks and valves and boilers—
How long a watch a stoker takes,
And how we pay our oilers.
But up aloft there is a chap
(Above their notice plainly)
With braid and flags upon his cap,
Who steers his ship profanely.
A steamer loves to roll and swing,
A-slewing round and veering—
But she is not a living thing,
And takes a lot of steering.
And Fm up here to see it done
{Port —port a bit and meet her !)
Oh, mate, a-coasting isn’t fun ;
The deep-sea work is sweeter.
We travel up, we travel down,
With dirty decks and funnel brown ;
Six ports from here to Auckland town
(Oh, stabbo’d hard and beat her !)
The look-out on the fo’c’s’le-head,
Whose form the sea-lights soften,
Has had a decent spell in bed
And gets it very often.
His chanting cry, “ Eight bells ; all’s well !
Lights burning clear and brightly ! ”
Cheers travelers more than tongue can tell,
And does so wrong or rightly.
But as we thump and lift the spray
I’ll tell you true and clearly
That I have been on deck all day—
Three spells for tucker merely.
So, if you feel secure, sleep on ;
And if you don’t I’m sorry :
By morning we may all be gone
To work a deep-sea quarry.
But I am here to pull you through
And find the reefs by bumping ;
So, if I shake, you blame the screw —
The darned thing’s ahvays jumping.
The granite cliffs are hard, I trow,
And slimy sivirls the kelp below :
Oh, none will miss us if we go
With broken bows a-slumping /
The tally-clerk will soon be quite
The veriest of fables ;
He’ll come and haunt us in the night,
A-rapping on the tables.
The bales swing high ; the winches scream—
From dawn to dawn they’re toiling ;
I’d rather sweat and make the steam,
Or risk my neck-bone oiling,
Than tally on the wharf all day—
(She only pays one purser)—
It’s “Cram her full to make her pay !
And starve her bridge-hands—curse her ! ”
But gold-rimmed cap and buttons mar
The deepness of my sorrow.
(God only knows just where we are—
I’ll work it out to-morrow.)
The deep-sea skipper is a king
(A half a point more easting !)
Who sleeps the sleep good dinners bring —
Oh ! slumber after feasting.
The deep-sea mate has lots of room
In which to swing and roll and boom :
The coast it is the sailor’s tomb
With white wave-plumes a-yeasting.
The coaster’s mate becomes a wreck ;
For, if you ever lose him,
You’ll always find him up on deck
With something to amuse him.
It’s winches and the freight by day;
And in the night the star-light
Seems sweeter to him than the play
Of tongues around the bar-light.
But some dark night his brain will go,
And you’ll go too, careering
Through fog and shoal, and never know
You’ve got a madman steering !
You’re snoring snugly all a-bed ;
He’ll get a snooze on Sunday ;
So—what’s her speed ? and how’s her head ?
And let’s see—this is Monday.
The man-o’-war she takes no freight ;
I guess I’ll join the Navy ,
And have a red marine to wait
Upon me like a slavey.
A draught of wine I’d like to take,
And go to sleep and never wake
Until the hand of Jones I shake —
His Christian name is Davy.
The song the engines roar is clear
And seems to tell of battle,
With horse to horse, and spear to spear,
And harness-greaves a-rattle.
And all night long they’ll slam away,
A-drip with oil and sooty—
Yet they had rest in port to-day,
And I was here—on duty.
So, if you feel secure, all’s well;
If not, I’m full of sorrow ;
The channel’s deep from here to Hell—
We might be there to-morrow.
Yet while she’s coaled by sweaty gnomes,
I’ll do my best to beckon
The hand of Death from landsmen’s homes,
And from my own, I reckon.
For I’m up here to get you there
(Port —port a bit, and meet her !)
And if my singing makes you swear,
The angels sing much sweeter.
Our hooker’s hanging southward slow,
The way the coasters mostly go,
And I keep laughing ’cos I know
Some day the shoals will beat her.
M.L.
QUILP N.
Fair Exchange.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It certainly was a “rummy little cave,” as the
fair occupant soliloquised, with its French-win-
dowlike opening seawards, and ragged skylight
above, through which the soft light spread over
herself, her canvas, and the dry white sand on
which she sat. And it was a pretty scene that
filled the rocky window-frame. With clever
touches the fair artist worked at its trans-
ference. A short slope of sand, a few rocks
jutting seaward, the rollers in ceaseless, unchang-
ing procession, the nearest partly broken already
into dancing foam, partly stretched—carved, as it
were, in translucent greenstone —curved to its
fall. One, two, three, four, five, six of its swelling
brothers lay waiting their turn, and beyond
stretched the troubled surface of the Rip, away to
Point Nepean.
Away to seaward the four o’clock sun picked
out, here the sails of a brig or barque, and there the
low, black hull and smokestack of a coasting
steamer.
All unconscious of the stranger in “ the studio,”
I came to the beach myself. Long ago I had dis-
covered the little recess, but “no caves for me,”
quoth I; “give me Australia’s God, the sun.” So,
stripping on the sand, presently I stood on one of
the wave-washed rocks, arms outstretched, back
hollowed, and every muscle ready for the spring
that would carry me head-foremost into deep
water. Now I swam, now dived, and ever and
anon revelling in the feel of summer air and sun
on my moist skin, mounted the rocky ledge and
posed, looking seaward, ready for a plunge.
Day after day and twice a day for nearly four
long lazy weeks had I gone through the same
performance, and as I loitered homeward I
thought regretfully that three days more would
see my last swim in the pleasant waters off Point
Lonsdale for fully 12 months.
As I ran up the beach for the last time on the
last day I half thought I saw something in “ the
studio.” Just a quick glance I gave, no more;
but in three minutes I was dressed and wriggling
my serpentine way nearer and nearer to the sky-
light.
Before I was quite close enough to see, I heard :
“Ye gods, but he is a man ! and didn’t he make
just a splendid central figure.” One more wriggle,
and I saw and understood. But I had no eyes
for the dripping diver on the canvas. Just one
thought filled my brain, flying backwards and for-
wards through it like a shuttle:
Whose hair has the rich red gold of the sun
When his kisses fall where the leaf lips are.
I’d been waiting for that girl since I read about
her, and here she was at last.
“Now he’s gone, I’ll have a swim.”
I woke to realities and wriggled back again. I
don’t think sharpshooter ever found perfect cover
so quickly as I did.
Many and many a time have I looked on woman
as God made her, but the sight of this careless,
free, fair-skinned ruddy-haired Aphrodite made
me catch my breath.
Of course I tracked her home, and of course I
solaced myself with to-morrow; and equally, of
course, to-morrow brought disappointment. She
did not come to the cave. She must have done
what I should have done that day—gone home.
I inquired at the house for “ the young lady who
was staying there.” If there was none, and never
had been, then I would know she lived there. If
she answered my knock, I would innocently ask
for the residence of some imaginary Smith or
Jones. If she was still there and at home — God
knows what I was going to say or do. I didn’t.
“She only came for a week, sir, and she lives in
Sydney.”
In Sydney—like a good Melbournian, I cursed
Sydney.
Light out of darkness ! I remembered the
Sydney Artists’ Exhibition. Surely she would
exhibit, and as surely would come to listen to the
criticism of the unconscious public.
Luck held, for within three days I entered the
hall to see Aphrodite talking to, or, rather, being
talked-to by, the only man I knew in all Sydney.
Then I knew that the stars in their courses were
fighting for me.
• • • •
I made the pace fast from the start, and in
three weeks my girl-wife and I were Southward
bound. You see, as I told her, I would gladly
have lost my growing practice to gain her, but she
and the praetioe, too, were far and away the best.
So I said that we must be satisfied with a week’s
spell, pointing out that we had already drawn
three weeks of the regulation month.
“Where were we going?” “Oh, I wasn’t
sure ; would Queenscliff do ? ” I thought I un-
derstood her expression, and flattered myself that
my own face was as brass.
She was my kindred spirit. This I had soon
decided. She could return me kiss for kiss, and
more also. I was satisfied. On the third day we
walked towards the point, and there I remarked
casually : “/ bought your picture.”
“ Did you ?” —she smiled ; “I thought so.”
I stole a look at her face.
“ I knew you were there, darling, hidden above
the cave.”
“ The devil!'" . . . And why did you go in
for a swim ?”• She blushed at last. “ I thought a
fair exchange was no robbery.”
ENIX.
Dear Mr. Editor.—
It’s not quite true. I knew he had been above the
cave, but only because he talked in his sleep.
Yours truly, Aphrodite.
Remarkable letter recently received from
a N.S.W. town in Sunday Brunker’s electorate :
Editor Bulletin. —When in Westralia between 1890 and
1900 I often told people that I was a contributor to your
paper, which I now admit to be a lie, and apologise for
same. On another occasion I told men with whom I was
drinking that I was Price Warung, and that you had
sent me to Westralia to find out all about the convicts,
which was a lie, and I now apologise for same.
When in Mullewa in 1892 I told men with whom I was
drinking that I was going to Marble Bar to report on the
gold mines there for your paper, another lie for which I
apologise. And on other occasions I have told people
that I was a contributor to your paper, and that
you went to school with me in the old country, all of
which I now admit to be lies and prompted by my own
egotism. I offer you a humble apology for having
uttered the above lies. I am sorry for having cracked
that I was connected with your paper, and for the future
I intend to mind my ov.n business.—l remain, yours sin-
cerely, .
The Bulletin accepts the above apology with a
confused feeling in the top of its head, though it
hasn’t the least idea what the writer is apologising
for. Plenty of people went to school with the
editor and turned out quite respectable after-
wards.
